THE RED PAGE.
The Poets' Corner.
TfC was the bounding B—df—d, bluff and boisterous,
I who begot the idea. “See?” he said, “I’m going
I to be the Pierpont Morgan of Australian poetry.
■ You watch this space! ” And after half-a-dozen
journeys to city pubs, and backblocks shanties he
returned to Melbourne with a portmanteau full of powers-
of-attorney signed by every Australian poet of note who
had been sober enough to write his name.
At the preliminary meeting the first point raised was
the definition of a poet for the purpose of Schedule A of
the articles of association. It was finally agreed that
everybody who got paid for his (whatever it was) was a
poet for the uses of the company. The chairman, after
extolling his own prowess, impressed his idea firmly on
those present. “ You can see that practically the whole
of Australian poetry is in our hands. Only decent
organisation is wanted to kill the greatest curse of the
game, over-production ! ”
More drinks were called for, and the meeting proceeded
to elect office-bearers. P —zg —b—n was elected presi-
dent. ; P—rt —11, vice-president; B —df—d, secretary ;
P—t—rs—n, legal adviser ; and D—s—n, S—nd—s,
Br—nn—n and D—1—y formed the council. The meeting
dispersed as well as it was able ; and on the advice of
the council Qu —nn and L —ws—n were led away by
different doors,
foreign Poets Excluded.
In a week’s time the existence of the Poets Corner
became apparent. An ambitious beginner went to every
bookseller in Melbourne, to find that all the rhyming
dictionaries had been mysteriously bought up by
some unknown hand. The fact was publicly commented
upon, but even the most far-seeing of speculators never
associated it with the existence of a ring. It was with
the defeat of the Barton Ministry that the Corners influ-
ence began to be felt.
The winter was spent in preparing a plan of cam-
paign, and the moment George Keid carried his want-ot-
confidence motion the Council got to work. Members
were interviewed, Ministers placated ; and when Reid
announced his new tariff it contained the novelty,
“ Poems, per line, £1 Is." The item passed both
Houses without amendment, Senator N—Id, who
had become one of the most prominent mem-
bers of the Corner, honourably _ abstaining from
voting as (in his own words) “an interesting party.
An amendment of the Immigration Restriction Act was
then necessary to protect the Corner from the importi
tion of pauper labour poets from Britain and Italy
and Reid passed a short Act making it mandatory
that all personsentering the Commonwealth should prove
to the satisfaction of the Customs authorities their in-
ability to write four lines of verse rhymed or otherwise.
The way was now clear for the Corner to start work in
earnest. The council registered a design under the
Trades Marks Act; and all poems produced by the
members of the Corner bore the device of a gilded lyre,
with the motto “ Pro Pecunia,” stamped at the top of the
first page.
The output began to be restricted. The prices of
prime poems advanced by fifty per cent, in the
day when the existence of the Corner first became
known; and the “spec, sellers” were heavily hit.
Next day there was a panic on the Stock Exchange ; and
four of the leading journals in Melbourne, which had been
bearing poems strongly, were compelled to close their
doors.
Toolisb Public Outcry.
A public outerv arose against the operations of the
Corner. Certain well-meaning humanitarians denounced
it as a blight upon the community, and pitiful pictures
were drawn of unfortunate families left poemless through
being unable to pay the extortionate charges artificially
created by the operations of the Corner. Kindly
suburban clergymen started leagues for the production
of poems for the consumption of the poor. Ambitious
poets arose like mushrooms in all directions, eager to take
advantage of the opening. It was then that H—bb—th—te
suggested that the poet's license should be made fact
instead of a mere figure of speech. The Vic. Legislative
Council, always eager to assist in the good cause of
monopoly, passed the necessary amendment to the
Crimes Act, making the unlawful pursuit of poesy
punishable bv six months imprisonment. But Vic.
Assembly happened to be discussing its annual Constitu-
tion Reform Bill at this time, and legislation was delayed.
The Corner turned to other means. Backblocks black-
leg poets were driven to starve in their distant huts.
Provisions, for some unknown reason, failed to arrive
when expected, and horses mysteriously fell lame. As
for the city poets who- attempted to interfere with tne
working of the Corner, a few shillings judiciously spent
among friendly policemen led to all possible competitors
being charged with vagrancy. As it was impossible for
them to prove lawful visible means of livelihood, they had
to 2fO to gaol. A private speculator tried to defy the Im-
migration Act by importing cheap poets from oversea,
and a dozen were landing at Port Melbourne when their
hats blew off and exposed their long hair. They were at
once seized ; the case was proved against them; and, with
their principal, they received long terms of imprisonment.
itbynim on Ro$e-Ceac<$.
As for the members of the Corner, they changed so
completely in outward appearance that their worst
friends did not know them. Ch —r —h was mentioned as
the next Lord Mayor of Melbourne. C—st—u was never
seen without his bell-topper and long frock coat. L—ws—n
spent most of his time in the manicurist s parlour ; and
the climax came when D—1—y was mistaken for Malcolm
M’Eacharn. The luxury that was the downfall of Rome
seemed to be menacing the Poets’ Corner. The members
had so much money to spend that they found no time
to turn out any new poems.
Then B—df—d struck the idea of the Poem Factory.
Instead of getting promising aspirants for poetic profit
convicted of vagrancy, the Corner set to work to buy
them up. Did any decent verse appear in an obscure
place, the author was hunted out and engaged for a term
of years to labour for the Cor.ier. Poetic bound ary-riders
in the Never were no longer starved into submission.
They were brought to town at wages a shade higher than
the squatters paid them. An attempt on the part of
the Trades Hall to organise a Poets Union fell to the
ground, despite the brave show made on Eight Hours
Day, when a hundred journeyman poets, in their working
garb, followed a lightly-clad young lady standing on a
lorry, lyre in hand.
The Corner prospered more than ever. Verses still
appeared signed with the old names, but the nominal
authors had really little to do with their making.
A line changed characteristically, a metre recast
according to the idiosyncrasy of the signatory,
a little mannerism worked in here and _ there, —
that was all the alteration that they were subjected to.
After a time, the operatives became so expert that even
this alteration was unnecessary, and the members of
the Corner spent most of their time at their clubs, leaving
the management of the factory to the dour Scotchman
who had formerly made a reputation as a champion driver
in an engineering workshop.
Split on a Scottish Hock.
One day it occurred to Al— x —d—r H—ct—r D—n—ld
M—K—y that he might do better by starting for himself ;
and, investing the savings of seventy-five years in the ven-
ture, he left the factory with twenty of the leading
journeymen. B—df—d was touring Europe at the time,
and the majority of the members of the Corner had lost
all grip both of the profession and of business methods.
F —zg —b—n made a strong attempt to grapple with the
difficulty, by having all parties arrested for absconding
from their hired service, but M—K —y had obtained good
legal advice and won the day. Then commenced his
action against the Corner for malicious prosecution, and
a series of appeals ended when the Privy Council granted
heavy damages against the Corner.
Meanwhile, orders were being left unfulfilled, despite
the frantic endeavours of the old members to recover
their vanished form. The loss of trade was enormous,
and it flowed steadily to the factory of M—K —y, who
secured the most persistent travellers from Flinders Lane
and sent them all over the Commonwealth forcing his
goods. The final blow came when B —df —d came back
and tried to bring in a gang of foreign poets, only to find
himself blocked by the Act that he had himself en-
gineered. The men were seized, and, to avoid arrest,
F—zg—b—n, P—rt—ll and the rest of the council had
to flee on board a tramp wind-jammer bound for Guam.
Thus ended the Poets’ Corner.
JUNIUS JUNIOR.
Contributors in Chorus.
S.T.S. : In John Mathew’s Australian Echoes ,
just published in Melbourne, this is the fine open-
ing of “ Facing Death ” :
There is an hour which God hath set, when every life of
days or years.
Worn to a flimsy, trembling thread, shall shuddering feel
the fatal shears. . . .
At least, that is how it ought to open. This is
how it is printed :
There is an hour which God hath set.
When every life of days or years,
Worn to a flimsy, trembling thread,
Shall shuddering feel the fatal shears.
The gain in stateliness by the first method is
obvious. What would “Locksley Hall” (for
example) lose if every one of its swinging lines
were chopped in two ?
L.R. writes of the Literary Vampire. This
beast may be of either sex ; but the fact that it is usually
female bears out the contention of many male authors
that woman is rather ingenious and adaptive (or adoptive)
than original. The Literary Vampire is possessed of
magnetism sufficient to induce her literary friends to
talk.—to recall incidents and adventures,—to be foolish
even to the extent of relating the plot of a story, the gist
of a paragraph, or a short article in hand. We may even
read over our copy to her as she sits before us, apparently
not half interested ; but a week, or it may be only a day
later, we see our ideas or plots worked out in print—
possibly in better language—the Vampire having con-
structed and worked into shape the matter we had dug
up. As a partner, the Literary Vampire is invaluable;
but as a friend who sticketh closer than a creditor she
flourishes on your blood and brains. Guard 1
L.O. : It was a youth in a Sydney bookshop who, asked
the other day for Lamb’s Tales , replied, “ We only sell
books here, madam." The same youngster, in answer to
a man who enquired for The Waybacks (written by Henry
Fletcher on the model of “Steele Rudd"), courteously
pointed out the way to the rear of the premises.
Commercial: At our (Sydney suburban) library “ The
Works of Robert Browning ’’ were recently demanded in
the book kept for “Recommendations." A wag foot-
noted objection to the effect that, in the interest of good
morals, he hoped the committee would not admit the
works of an author who lamented “ Never the time, the
place, and the loved one altogether .’’ And, always in the
interest of good morals, the committee did n’t!
S.T.S. : London Bookman contains a standing offer of
remarkable prices for various first editions, some of them
quite modern : e.g., Lang’s Blue Fairy Book “large paper,
1889,” 25s.. Any first edition of Stevenson seems to be
worth from 15s. to twice as much ; and even Phillips’
Paolo and Francesca is worth 10s. The original Alice in
Wonderland has risen to £2. ; and all the works of the
Kelmscott Press (not yet ten years old) run from £\ to
£$. Anything of Pater, J. A. Symonds, or Meredith is in
demand'; but, as usual, the best prices are reserved for
whatever may be suspected of impropriety : Payne’s
Novels of Bandello, £2. and his Arabian Nights, £9 ;
while the latter in Burton’s curious edition is always good
for £20.
Theta: Concerning the spelling of Macarthur : Spelt
so ’tis in accordance with the Scottish custom. Scottish
names should almost invariably be spelled out. I live
among Macdonalds, Mackinnons, Macraes, Macfarlaneg,
Mackintoshes and Mackays: all spell their names as I
have written, and most strongly object to the clipped
form. The Mackays (pronounced Mae-kye, accent on
second)prefer even being occasionally called “Mackey"
(pronounced Mack-ee, accent on first) to having their
patronymic spelled M'Kay. The Scot is peculiarly sensi-
tive about the spelling of his name ; and I once mortally
offended a Highlander by writing Frazer instead of
Fraser. The Frazers are Irish, as are also the Mackeys.
The Irish are usually indifferent as to the spell-
ing or pronunciation of their names. A couple
near me are known as “ Mr. Sharry ” and “ Mrs. Sherry.”
In another family, the old man spells his name either
“ Darmody ” or “ Dermody,” and always pronounces it
Darmody ; while his children all spell with an e. When
the creamery manager asked the old man to stick to one
form, he smiled and waived the matter aside as of no con-
sequence. Speaking to a Mahony, I said, “ You have n’t
got the usual O’ to your name ? ” “ Oh,” he replied, “ I
have it; but I niver use it. Phwat the divil’s the differ-
ence ? ” A perjury case in Warrnambool (Vic.), not long
ago, turned on the spelling of the Irish name Porter,
which, it was sworn, was spelled either Porter or Portar.
All this to a Scotchman is anathema.
The Brunt of the War.
€MILY HOBHOUSE publishes The Brunt of
the War (Methuen ; 2s. 6 d. and 3s. 6d.). 1t was the
Boer women and children who bore the brunt) or
the war ” ; and Miss Hobhouse’s persistence in
their service is known. She was Africa’s Florence Night-
ingale, with the difference that the opportunity
personal ministration was often denied her, and tnac s
had no ministering organisation at her back ; so that
best work was perforce done in arousing with her pen
world-wide sympathy for Kitchener’s victims.
the flood of Patriotic £ie».
During the war, of course, the newspapers were
fed on lies; and now that the war is over no
trouble is taken to bolster the lies. It is ad-
mitted by innumerable witnesses that the Boers, as a
race, were neither especially' dirty nor especially ignorant.
Some families doubtless were so, as some are in Britain
and Australia. But the “green paint,” story, taken as
typical, was a lie which disgraced its disseminators.
And it is cynically admitted, even in London Times
(special correspondence, July 12, 1902) that the scheme
of the concentration camps “ was designed to bring
pressure upon the Boers in the field.’ The story
that the camps were a work of charity and mercy
was a lie. In a sense, to' be sure, the camps were
necessary; since after the Transvaal and the Free
State had been laid waste, after the women’s homes
had been burnt over their heads and their food destroyed,
it may be supposed that Kitchener would be averse from
leaving them to perish of starvation. None the less, the
camps were primarily and essentially a military expe-
dient. Writes the Times special correspondent (loc.
cit.) :
Lord Kitchener’s first intention undoubtedly was
definitely to clear the country of stock and inhabitants
by mobile columns operating from the railway. The
impression generally prevailed in South Africa, that the
Boers, on account of their proverbial domesticity,
could not long endure separation from their wives and
families.
In other words, failing to conquer the Boer fighting
men in the field, Kitchener determined to strike at them
through their wives and children. We will grant that
even this was justified. We will grant that, despite the
barbarism of the expedient, despite its denunciation by
the comity of civilised nations, such war against women
and children was Kitchener’s military right, lay within
his military duty. But the concession extends to the
deportation i no farther. No military necessity can
justify the killing of such non-combatants by cruelty, by
starvation, or by neglect. Or if it be held that Britain’s
national existence was so concerned in the war that it
was compulsory for the war to be ended at any cost of
innocent life, then the argument recoils upon itself. All
war is savagery ; but the slaying of women and children
exceeds savagery. If it were necessary for those women
and children to die that the British nation might survive,
then the British nation is a mere wild beast, an enemy of
human rights and human justice, and does not deserve to
survive.
Cbe ethics ot Patriotism.
Never forget that, apart from the sentiment of
tradition and the emotion of the crowd, patriotism is
sheer self-interest. It is by selfishness that the individual
survives with his family, and the union of individals in a
nation is merely an expedient to secure a better chance
of individual survival, or a survival on better terms. If
the flag and the country do not profit an intelligent self-
interest, then there is no reason, no justification for
them. An intelligent self-interest looks, of course, to the
betterment of the race—not the tribe or nation, but
humanity. Intelligent selfishness is precisely the same
as intelligent altruism—it means not loving another
better than one’s-self, but (in Maeterlinck’s phrase) loving
another in one’s-self ; and (in Nietzsche’s belief) that
other is not to-day’s neighbour in space, but to-morrow's
neighbour in time—the man to come, the potential other,
the better man conceivable.
There are but two things that stand fast in ethics:
Truth—the most we can see; leading to Justice—the
best we can do; devoted to the service of the Future.
When a nation comes clearly into conflict with the
broadest human Justice, it commits an act of ethical
suicide that warrants any of its citizens in casting ofif
allegiance. “My country, right or wrong,” is a base
maxim ; though not always base in practice, seeing that
the admeasurement of turpitude depends in every case
upon the result of the maxim’s application. On the
wide plane of human justice, its exponents rank
as wild beasts, enemies of humanity; and just as
the nation comprehends and effaces the individual,
so humanity, in an ethical appeal, comprehends and
effaces the nation. Given always that these are lines
of general theory, and that it is impossible to prove
their validity for the individual or the nation that denies
the particular application. But, when he is sure that the
life of a wild beast endangers him, the individual justly
destroys it; when a nation is sure that the life of a
citizen endangers it, the nation justly destroys the
citizen; and when the consensus of humanity decides
that the existence of a nation endangers humanity,
then humanity may justly destroy the nation. Only,
we must take care, when we use petty wits and raise our
petty voices, that we really do stand for humanity, not
alone as we envisage it, but as humanity will envisage
itself a hundred or a thousand years hence.
tbo Slaughter of the Innocents.
The human verdict upon Kitchener’s concentra-
tion camps is open to little doubt. The guilt lay neither
in conception nor execution ; but in the manner of execu-
tion. Kitchener deliberately planned without foresight,
and with open eyes blundered into massacre. His
generals, following his orders, collected homeless and
starving wretches into droves, and shepherded them into
camps of huts or tents made anywhere—with no ade-
Iquate provision for food, or shelter, or health.
The greatest population of all camps was 118,408, in
October, 1901. The death-total in fourteen months, so
far as recorded, was 20,177. The average general monthly
death-rate ranged up to 344 per 1000 per annum. Children
were naturally the chief victims. Their average monthly
death-rate in the Transvaal ranged up to 586 per 1000 per
annum ; in the Orange Free State, up to 629 ; in Natal,
up to 327. In thirteen camps it was over 1000 ; during
the month of October, 1901, children died in Brandfort
camp at the rate of 1951 per 1000 per annum t
Why did they die ? From starvation and epidemic
disease fostered by insanitary camp situations and
systems. That they were at first starved is shown by the
fact that the ration-scale was several times increased ;
and as it was increased the death-rate fell. Some were at
first starved more than others ; the so-called “ undesir-
ables,” whose kindred still fought, being given (with a
cruelty quite devilish) a less ration than those whose
kindred had surrendered. “ The scheme .. . was de-
signed to bring pressure upon the Boers in the field,"
writes the Times special correspondent.
How did those fifteen thousand children die? They
died- in long-drawn agony under the eyes of their
impotent mothers —died for want of milk and suitable
food ; died for want of medicine and medical skill pdied
for want of clothes and blankets ; died because they were
crowded together in insufficient space without sufficient
water ; died because, when inevitable disease broke out,
there were no means to check it. And the steam of their
blood rises up to humanity—the foulest offering ever
made to the Glory of Britain.
CIK Sbeddcr ot Blood.
At whose door do their deaths lie ?
Kitchener laid and fired the train that exploded
the magazine of disaster. When he gave his order
he would not or could not see Death staring him
in the face. When the slaughter commenced, he did
apparently little to end it. As soon as Chamberlain took
charge, the death-rate fell markedly. Nurses and doctors
were supplied ; the camps were shifted or made sanitary ;
proper food was given in sufficient quantity ; and the
remnant of the miserable prisoners drew a breath of
miserable relief and stepped out of Kitchener’s hells.
Say that Kitchener was merely a dolt; that his fault
was merely a muddle what a criminal dolt! what a
murderous muddle ! The story of the African concen-
tration camps is the blackest page in all the history of
England ; its shame and disgrace will never be wiped out
while pen can write or tongue has speech. And with
those fifteen thousand little skeletons not yet mouldered
into merciful dust, Chamberlain prates reconciliation of
the races, and his press apologists foretell forgiveness and
amity! Ask of the Boer Rachel weeping for her
children, the children who cried to. her, “Woe is
me, my mother, that thou hast borne me 1"
Will she'forgive ? Ask of the Boer farmer returning to his
desolate home over the graves of his household. Can he
forget? Can the world forget, permeated by the universal
sentiment of fatherhood' and motherhood ?

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
What Used To Be.
Hill an’ ridge an* barren river, all the station
ridin’,
Mobs o’ cattle, flanks a-quiver, in the ti-tree
I hidin’;
I Cloudin’ dust, an’ red sun flarin’; ’member how
we caught ’em,
Wheeled ’em (thousand eyes a-glarin’); ’long the
sidin’ brought ’em !
Ride ! Rouse ’em up across the hill-tops !
Bring ’em down the gullies in the dawn ;
For the boys are set an’ goin’, an’ there’s half the
herd a-lowin’—
Whoo-oop 1 through the yellow of the dawn !
Gleamin’ horns like lines o’ lances—an’ the mob
stampedin’ ;
Why did you—yer knew the chances—head them,
never heedin’
Crowdin’ brutes that tossed an’ rolled yer—
stamped yer inter clay ?
So 111 never more be’old yer, never hear yer say—
Hi! Ring ’em in along the tussock ;
Swing ’em where the gates are set an’ wide—
But the clackin’ hoofs are thunder, an’ yer done if
yer git under—
Steady ! where the gates are yawnin’ wide 1 ”
Wish I’d died wi’ yer that momin’ when yer bed
we made yer ;
Left yer to the night an’ dawnin’ with the scrub
ter shade yer.
Yer was friend more olose than brother—now yei
sleepin’ far—
’N I’ll not ride wi’ any other where the long down:
are.
Now, yer’ll be ridin’ in the mountings,
Though the cattle will not turn to see yer pass ;
There’s no sod or stone will hold yer when thi
shoutin’ whips have told yer—
“ Ride ! the mob is breakin’ in the Pass ! ”
Hill an’ scrub an’ lone grey river—only thine?
I’m lovin’—
I will serve no more for ever (Hey ! the column’s
movin’,
Hear the blessed bugles Mowin’)—you’ll be on thi
track
With the boys—them all unknowin’—bring thi
cattle back.
Ah ! slow ’em down across the shingle ;
Trail ’em up the cuttin’ in the dark,
There was nothin’ feared or tried me wi’ your knet
beside me;
Ah-h ! the crawlin’ homeward in the dark !
M.L.
E. B. LANCASTER.
At the Back of Wyngha Run.
When the shades of night were gathering and tht
plains were growing dim,
And the hushed Bush seemed to listen to th
curlew’s evening hymn,
While the kookaburras chuckled as they watched
the old day die,
And the star of even glittered like a jewel in the
sky ;
When the breezes were a-roam
On our way we cantered home ;
Cantered briskly through the twilight, for our
hard day’s work was done ;
But we stopped at Sandy Rise,
Half in horror, half surprise ;
For we found a swagman lying at the back of
Wyugha Run.
Dead ! the sand had drifted round him by the old
grey boundary fence.
Tramping on to God knows whither, he had
tramped from God knows whence ;
Tramping on to God knows whither—till h
reached the endless plain,
Where his pleadings and his curses and his prayer-
were all in vain.
Not a comrade’s voice to cheer
When the end was drawing near,
And the anguished cries grew fainter, there
beneath the glaring sun ;
None to watch him as he died,
Not a living thing beside,
Save the black crows loafing round him at th»
back of Wyngha Run.
Close beside him lay his billy and the pipe he used
to smoke.
And the dusty swag he shouldered when a spree
had left him “broke.”
It was when the tanks were empty and the drought
was on the land,
And for miles around the country was a waste oi
billowed sand.
Out afar beneath the glare,
Where he’d thrown it in despair,
Lay the water-bag he’d cherished till its precious
load was done.
When he reached the Ten-mile tank
(By the high and whitened bank),
It was just to find it empty at the back of Wyngha
Run.
Maybe there’s a wife a-waiting, in some far-ofi
Southern town,
For the “cheque which giveth comfort,” that
the mail should bring her down ;
But her hopes grow daily fainter, and her hair is
turning grey,
As she hears the oft-repeated, “Nothing here for
you to-day ! ”
But she has no time to weep,
For the’re little ones to keep—
Little ones for ever asking when their daddy will
be done
With his labors in the West:
When he’ll journey home and rest:
Little knowing daddy’s resting at the back of
Wyngha Run.
Or perhaps a mate is searching round about the
land out-back,
Looking for a trace of Billy, or it maybe Jim, or
Jack.
Eagerly he seeks and questions in the huts and
round the fires—
How strong hopes rise up within him as he learns
by “ mulga wires ” :
“ He wa3 spoken to near Bourke,
On the look-out there for work !
He was seen in Riverina when the shearing had
begun ! ”
But he'll use the blades no more,
For his shearing days are o’er,
And his “summer spell ” he’s taking at the back
of Wyngha Run.
N.S.W.
RIVMRINA.

THE BULLETIN STORY BOOK

BY SIXTY BULLETIN WRITERS.
Sole Distributing Agents—
Edwards, Dunlop & Co., Ltd., Sydney and Brisbane.
There are men in this world who are almost successful, almost religious, almost well, almost
happy. But a miss is as good as a mile. A recruit who is 5 feet 9 inches in height, when the
standard is 5 feet 10 inches, is rejected just as surely as if he were only 4 feet in height. It is of
little avail how valiant, and how strong, and how experienced, and how willing to fight he may be.
He is not up to the standard required, and so he is rejected.
If you run and jump 7 feet after a ferry boat leaving the dock, and the ferry boat is 7I feet
away, you fall into the water just as surely and get just as wet as a little child who only jumps
one foot, and you sink just as deep, for you both go to the bottom.
“ Almost ” is a dangerous word. It is so alluring, but is really so deceptive. What you want
is a certainty. You want to be assured. You want definite information. You want—not “almost,”
but absolutely—the best, YOU want a Ruberoid Roof.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 7, 1903.
Concerning Some Financial Cripples.
Probably there is no other country in the
world which carries such a large proportion of
lame, crippled and partially-dead financial
institutions as Australia. They are not
nearly so numerous as they wore three or
four years ago, but still they are numerous
—reckoning banks, land banks, large
building societies which would make very
respectable banks in the country districts of
the United States, and miscellaneous places
which take in the deposits of the public (to
say nothing of sundry assurance societies),
they are much more numerous than is com-
monly supposed. The “ reconstructed ”
institution is peculiar to Australia. In
most countries, when a bank is unable
to pay its creditors on demand it just
dies—unless some other institution comes
forward with the money. The art of
making terms with the unpaid credi-
tor—of giving him something on account
and a long-dated bit of paper for the rest
—of turning him partly into a preference
shareholder, and partly into a debenture-
holder, and partly writing him off, and
inscribing him, and making him “ preferred ”
or “ deferred ” or “ interminable,” and pro-
mising to pay him the shortage out of profits
—in brief, the great art of prolonging the
agony and keeping the snake alive till sun-
set—these things are mostly, if not wholly,
Australian.
* m . 9 \ 9 N. •
It may be admitted at once that recon-
struction was, in most cases, a wise proceed-
ing ; it was bad, but for most of the country
to go into liquidation would have been worse.
It may also be admitted that the finan-
cial institutions which went broke in ’93
were not all wholly responsible for what
happened to them. They perished through
the breaking of a boom which they made in
part, and which was partly owing to frantic
borrowing by the people’s Government, reck-
less land sales, a hilarious belief that it would
rain in the West every year, and a wildly
unreasoning faith in “ our magnificent
natural resources.” Many of the institutions
that perished were swindles pure and simple,
and the promoters thereof ought to have gone
to gaol, but didn’t. Most of those which
survive were concerns which intended to be
honest but didn’t know any too well what
honesty meant; and many of them were run by
ignorant, incapable and pompous bounders
who meant well, but who made a shocking ex-
hibition of themselves by posing in high places
as financial authorities of bottomless sagacity,
and grieving over the crude ideas of Labor
politicians and the like. The bounders are a
little less vociferous now. Some of the insti-
tutions they helped to wreck have, by
immense efforts, wiped off their losses;
some others of them have made great
efforts and have wiped oft' part of their
losses, and are dragging the rest of the
dead body along with them till further
notice ; and some are not doing much
beyond drifting. If the matter was one
which concerned only the shareholders it
would be the shareholders’ exclusive funeral.
Even if it concerned only the shareholders
and the depositors it wouldn't be, strictly
speaking, the funeral of the general public.
But Australia in general has its good name
damaged by having this mob of financial
invalids convalescing publicly, year after
year, on its front balcony, as an advertise-
ment of the hospital inside. And there is
always the fear that just a little more slump
—which may come to pass any day if the
supply of foreign loans gives out—would end
some of the partially-recovered invalids.
Then another long row of funerals would
start from this country’s front door, with
the liquidator driving the hearse and the
depositors weeping in the rear carriages.
• • • •
A Federal Banking Bill is promised for
the coming session of the Australian Parlia-
ment, and, in addition to putting the utterly
unsatisfactory banking laws of the Common-
wealth on a better footing, it ought to con-
tain some provision for removing the horde
of financial derelicts off the stage. Those
which have a good chance of recovery should
be assisted in order to make the recovery
more rapid ; any that have absolutely no
chance should be asphyxiated decently in a
quiet spot and put out of their misery. It
would be possible, in many cases, to give a
large amount of assistance without risk, and
even with a profit, to the Treasury The
case of the Bank of New Zealand, which was
saved and put on its feet again by Govern-
ment interference, is a case in point; and
the fact that the Maoriland Government was
foolish enough to demand nothing in return
for its aid need not be a case in point.
* • * •
Anyone can locate quite a number of insti-
tutions which, if the money were available,
could buy up large quantities of their depre-
ciated securities at a low figure, and thus
greatly reduce their liabilities. If the Philp
Government, for instance, were something
else than the dead broke concern it is, and
could have spent £1,000,000 during the last
few years in gradually buying up the deposits
of its Queensland National Bank at the
figure which seems now to be chronic with
that institution, it could have taken in return
£1,000,000 worth of preference shares carry-
ing a minimum 5 per cent, dividend with a
prospect of more, which would have been a
paying investment for the State. And
on this basis the bank would have saved
about £15,000 a year in interest, and
have wiped off some £500,000 or
£700,000 of its deficit in one act. Such a
proceeding, of course, would make the
Queensland National Bank virtually a State
institution. The State would be the largest
shareholder, the auditor, and, to all intents,
the manager. But, on the other hand, the
benefits would be so large that even the
most excited apostle of Private Enterprise
should be able to recognise them. The
State would make, under any kind of
decent management, a handsome and per-
manent profit on its investment. The
institution would be from £500,000 to
£700,000 nearer to definite solvency, and
also about £15,000 a year nearer to
the same desirable goal. The private
shareholders would be that amount nearer
to a dividend ; a great mass of un-
wholesome-looking securities would be taken
off the market ; and one financial cripple
would be making better progress towards
convalescence.
* • * •
A similar method might have been adopted
in N.S.W. with the Australian Joint Stock
Bank if the O’See Government had not used
all the trust funds for other purposes, and if
the demands for assistance for the Balmain
Choral Union and similar brassy mendi-
cants, and the general habit of scatter-
ing money loosely around, hadn’t swal-
lowed up even the eleven millions of
revenue. It might also have been adopted
in Victoria with the A. 1). and M. Bank,
the Land Mortgage Bank, and one or two
large building societies. Likewise, if the
Bent Government had largely bought up,
especially during the recent slump, the pre-
ference shares and Assets Trust deposits of
the Commercial Bank (though that institu-
tion is no longer among the very bad samples
■of crippledom), and constituted itself a pre-
ference shareholder for the money expended,
it might have made a good investment for
itself and improved the position of the insti-
tution by half-a-million. In every case, of
course, this would mean constituting the
State principal shareholder with a decisive
voice in the management, but it would be a
highly-profitable bargain for the private
shareholders also. As the State Governments
seem incapable of moving in the matter, and
as Australia is weary of seeing the old ghost
turning up daily in the long column
of depreciated deposits that decorate the
share-list, the Australian Parliament should
consider whether it isn’t due to inter-
fere. To do so effectually it would have to
assume large powers to audit and supervise
all institutions which take deposits from the
public ; to order the closing of institutions
whose position is utterly hopeless unless the
parties concerned put up sufficient money to
place them on a better basis ; to come to the
aid of embarrassed concerns which are not
hopeless, and constitute itself a preference
shareholder on terms that will leave it a
profit and yet make things better for the
sufferer ; to amalgamate superfluous institu-
tions under its charge while preserving the
interests of the private shareholders ; and
generally to do something to lift the reproach
that lies on Australia of being about the most
reconstructed country on this globe.
* * * •
These proceedings would put sundry Aus-
tralian banks in the same position as, say, the
Bank of Norway, wherein the State is chief,
but not sole, shareholder. Should they result,
later on, in complete Federal ownership and
consolidation, so much the better. If the
Federal authority thinks of taking over any
existing institution or institutions as the
nucleus of a Government Bank, as has been
frequently suggested, it is better to take
over and amalgamate the wrecks than to
take over a solid concern and leave the
invalids still in full public view. Some of
them are a distinct blot on the landscape,
and some, by reason of the possibility that
another slump may come before they are
strong enough to stand it, are an actual
danger. And it is better, in one sense,
that the Bank of the People should be
formed by taking over existing institutions
than start as a separate and additional insti-
tution. If it started as an additional insti-
tution it would increase competition, which
is too great already ; the extra competition
would increase the danger that one or two
existing banks might go down and wreck
many sober and respectable Australian
citizens in their fall ; and it would make
them try to grind their debtors even a little
harder in their effort to keep up. In an
over-banked country all these things call for
consideration. A process which would make
less banks instead of more, leave two or
three less managements and directorates to
live on the game, and repair or take alto-
gether out of public view certain concerns
which look as if the next panic would pro-
bably overtake them before they have
nearly recovered from the last one, would
have points of advantage.
An Agent-General on Loans.
In financial matters an Australian Agent-General
is a hired ostrich, who buries his head in the sand
for a consideration, and takes a cheerful view of
his State’s credit for a salary. As a rule, he does
it with the air of one who fully believes what he
says ; but it is only necessary to picture what
would happen to an Agent-General who frankly
admitted in London that his State was wild,
foolish, extravagant, and possibly booming along
the high road to bankruptcy, to realise the worth-
lessness of his assurances that all is well. In
financial matters he appears for the defendant,
and the person who appears for the defendant
isn’t expected to state that the defendant is about
as bad a character as anyone could reasonably
expect to meet. The latest Agent-General to be
honestly surprised (at a good salary), and un-
feignedly shocked (for his living) at the aspersions
cast on Australia’s borrowing policy, is Grainger
of South Australia. The following extract shows
that Grainger is quite as much shocked as anyone
could reasonably expect at the money :
With the indebtedness of Australia and the indebted-
ness per head of Australians most writers on financial
subjects deal fairly, clearly, and with knowledge ; others
have to be told again and again that in Australia the
Governments own railways, tramways, harbors, wharfs,
jetties, roads, telegraphs, telephones, waterworks, etc.,
and that several of these undertakings return more net
revenue—after deducting working expenses—than the
interest on the cost of construction. There can be no
objection to the money expended in any country under
Responsible Government being £lOOO per head of the
population, so long as the profit exceeds the amount of
interest at which that £lOOO per head has been borrowed.
I do not know, nor do I wish to ascertain, what the
indebtedness per head of this country amounts to, nor the
area of land still belonging to the people. By the
indebtedness per head I mean the public debt, plus muni-
cipal debt, plus the cost of railways, canals, etc. Such a
calculation, if it could be arrived at, would be of no value.
Our Australian debt has not been expended in glory and
gunpowder, in purchasing remounts from plundering
rascals, and in other utterly asinine blunders, nor is our
taxation, under the heads of income tax, municipal, and
county council rates, as burdensome or as heavy as that
of our generous English critics.
* • • *
Grainger is certainly vigorous enough, but he
is horribly unconvincing, and the care with which
he dodges the question at issue is really painful.
Australia certainly is far less heavily burdened
with national and municipal taxes than Britain is,
and that is just where the largest crack lies.
Australia pays out of loans (largely foreign loans)
many things which Britain pays out of taxes;
therefore, Australia is less heavily taxed. And
the fact that Australia doesn’t, through not
paying sufficient taxes, pay its way as Britain
does, has really come to be regarded as some kind
of a virtue—as a valuable disease to be petted and
preserved ; and Agents-General speak proudly of
it (for a reasonable salary). The Bulletin
is not surprised that it should be so. The world
is full of quite respectable-looking citizens who
would profess to be proud of a caterpillar or a boil
for a moderate salary.
* * * *
In recent months the Australian Agents-
General have been kept rather busy expressing, in
the London papers, their absolutely honest "and
disinterested conviction (which they are paid for)
that Australian finance is perfectly sound. But
not one, so far as The Bulletin is aware, has ever
yet faced this point—which is the real point after
all: The public debts of Australia were certainly,
in a large measure, incurred for the construction
of public works, though in very many cases they
were for public works which are wholly, or to a
great extent, unprofitable ; and the public debts of
Britain, France and the United States were
largely incurred for unprofitable wars. But Aus-
tralia’s debt is mostly a foreign debt, while the
debts of these other countries are almost wholly
owing to their own people. And the JES a man owes
himself, even if he incurred the debt for the worst
possible motives, embarrasses him a great deal less
than the £5 he owes to someone else, even if the
debt was created with the best possible intentions.
Great Britain, France, and the United States,
when the national accounts are balanced, owe no
outside person or State a penny for their roads,
railways, telegraphs, publio buildings, harbors,
canals, or even for their disastrous wars. The Aus-
tralian Commonwealth owes nearly £200,000,000
(£49 per inhabitant) to outside creditors, which
means that about £7,000,000 has to be exported to
pay interest. Not, be it observed, £7,000,000 a
year paid to local creditors, and spent locally in
goods and wages, but £7,000,000 spent in goods
and wages in Britain —which makes all the
difference.
* • • •
A good deal too much self-congratulatory
twaddle is written about the fact that Australia’s
foreign debt was incurred mostly for public works.
If Australia had built its public works out of taxa-
tion, and then incurred the same debt by foolish
wars, provided the war debt was a local one, it
would be a great deal better off than it is now.
The ghastly fact, which no whitewashing arts can
disguise, is that, in a list of the 18 principal States
of the world, Australia owes more money per
inhabitant, which is not represented by remunerative
assets, than any save France and Portugal.
France owes its debt to itself, and is reasonably
prosperous; Portugal owes its debt to other nations,
just like Australia, and has gone bankrupt.
And Australia isn’t, by any means, the only
country which has State railways, telegraphs,
banks, &c., to show for its debt. Germany,
Austria, Sweden, Norway, Denmark, and Bel-
gium also borrowed money mainly for public
works, but they confined their borrowings far
more carefully to remunerative works than Aus-
tralia did, and didn’t call deficits, and State aid
to religion, and military stores, and roads, and
sand-shifting, and immigrants’ passage-money,
“permanent and reproductive works” to any-
thing like the extent Australia has done.
* • • •
Australia has six notable delusions in the
matter of its loan policy :
(1) That it is the only country with great public assets
in the way of State-owned lands. Even old countries
like France, Austria, Prussia, Italy, and Russia have huge
assets of the same kind.
(2) That itis the only country whose debt wasn’t incurred
for war purposes, whereas there are plenty whose debt
was also incurred chiefly for public works, and in all
Europe and most of America it is only possible to find
two countries with so heavy an unremunerative debt per
head as Australia possesses.
(3) That the State lands of Australia are in some way a
security for the public debt, which they are not, for,
under the present system of finance, every penny that
can be screwed out of them is required to cover ordinary
expenditure.
(4) That desert (and most of the still unsold land is
practically desert) is a convincing asset if the figures are
only large enough.
(5) That the fact that Australia persistently refuses to
pay enough taxes to keep itself decently solvent is, in
some way, a merit and a proof of its solvency.
(6) That the fact that Australia’s railways, &c., pay to
a certain extent while the country is in a condition of
boom created and maintained by foreign loans, is proof
that they will pay equally well when there are no foreign
Joans and no boom.
Any Agent-General who would write to the press
about these six matters, and write clearly and
definitely—not fly promiscuously off the handle
about our railways, and our unsold lands, and
other countries’ war debts —would have an excel-
lent chance to say something interesting.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Gippsland Coal Troubles.
The existing strike at two out of the three Gipps-
land coal mines once again sets forth the folly of
the State in not inaugurating a system of com-
pulsory arbitration—purely for its own advantage.
The strike is chiefly over a proposed reduction of
Is. per ton in the hewing rate —from 3s. 6d. to
2s. 6d.—wages of shift men and other employees
being lowered 20 per cent. But there are other
matters which cause the miners to look on the
management with considerable suspicion—the
proposed abolition of the understanding that the
8-hours’ day shall be reckoned from bank to bank;
the new rule that any man absent two days in a
fortnight shall be deemed to have left his job;
restriction of the advancement of wheelers, shift-
men and brushers to be coalmen; and the pro-
posed new rule that no manager, assistant
manager, overman, deputy, engineer, engine-
driver, stoker, braceman, tipper, weighman
or screener shall belong to any coal-miners’
association or union. Another matter which has
caused much trouble between the parties is tin
shifty treatment meted out to the Outtrim miners
since Jan. 1, when a notice was posted intimating
that the mine would be idle till the sth, in order
to fix a new endless rope. On the sth this notice
was extended till the 7th ; again that day till the
8tli; and again that day till the 12th. When the
first notice was posted, many miners asked a
week’s leave to visit their distant homes, but were
refused ; they waited about—and still wait. At
Outtrim mine, which, in the present dispute, is
working hand and glove with the Jumbunna, this
is the fourth strike within 18 months. The
co., being “on its own,’’has hitherto been defeated,
but now a combined attempt is being made to down
Labor in the Gippsland collieries. The third co.,
the Coal Creek Co., is so far working on a tem-
porary agreement, and seems likely to come tc
terms with its employees, though strenuous efforts
are being made to bring it into the combine. Th«
points in dispute are not so complicated or incap
able of solution that an Arbitration Court couldn’/
settle them in a couple of days, but there is no
such Court. If there were, it would have to be t
compulsory one, for any co. that had four strikes
in 18 months would be viewed with suspicior
by the most conciliatory Court in the world.
Meanwhile, the State’s property is lying idle, the
State railways are sending money away to N.S.W
which is badly needed in Victoria, and severa
hundred miners are unemployed. Even Beni
knows the folly of such proceedings !
The Conspiracy Against White Labor
in_N.Q.
About the tricks of N.Q. nigger advocate*
“ J. F. K. ” writes:—
The general feeling towards the white cane-cutters or-
the Mossman I found hostile, bitterly so. No accusation,
however false, seemed too bad to bring against them-
One vindictive remark of a cane-grower, who had not
registered for the rebate, was : “ Wait till they come tc
’s ; they’ll meet their Waterloo ! ” Clearly it was
to be no question as to whether white men could cut cant
in this climate ; the point at issue was—Could white mer
cut cane for the mill if the mill people and nine out ol
ten of the growers did not want them to ? In the first
place, the only communication the employer would hold
with the men was through a “ contractor,” in privatu
interviews, at which no witness from the men’s
side was permitted to be present. Under these cir
cumstances, I was not surprised to hear that the
contractor" threw up the job at a critical time.
Ihe men were then cutting grub cane going under seven


tons to the acre and were behind in their output. The
mill people seized the opportunity to harass them, told
them their deposit was forfeited and their services would
be dispensed with. More than half the men threw up
the sponge and deserted. White cane-cutting in this
instance had been a ridiculous fiasco, and the black squad
was triumphant. At this point a storekeeper came for-
ward with a deposit of £5O, and the mill agreed to let the
gang appoint another “contractor” and start again on
the same terms as before with a greater number of men.
It was at this time (Sept. 9) I managed to beat into the
party working at the Little Mossman. One of the farms
we cut went under seven tons to the acre and was so
overgrown with matted vines three or four feet deep that
it could not have been cut at any profit at all but for the
rebate money. Though we were actually out of pocket
cutting it, rather than be beaten—as it was confidently
assorted we would be—we cut it clean out. When the
last load went off, the owner was so delighted and
astonished that he came among us and said, “ I’ve
had all sorts working for me during the last
five years, but you are the gamest men I’ve
ever struck.” He then gave us a gratuity. Yet
the very next da.v he put blacks in, showing that he
had only registered for white labor in order to clean off
his rubbish and clear his land for a fresh start with nig-
gers. In the meantime the other party had been engaged
on what I had been tauntingly told would be their Water-
loo. They proved thpmselves white men and got
through all the bad ground. On the Saturday the
men were knocked off, it was known that the very last of
the had cane had been cut, and a terrible corner of it had
been going about 3 tons to the acre. All there was before
the men then was some 500 tons of clean standing cane
going about 30 tons to the acre and three weeks to" do it
in. It would not have taken more than six or seven days
at the outside. They loaded 15 trucks that morning, of
cane cut over night, and then started cutting. They had
been cutting about two hours when a Jap. rode
up with a letter from the mill. It was an order
from the manager to cease work. The cane cut
was afterwards loaded on the trucks in three hours It
filled 34 -trucks, weighing 51J tons, cut and loaded by 22
men in five hours, which show's what money can be made
under favorable circumstances in heavy cane. In the
evening, the “contractor” returned with the informa-
tion that our services were dispensed with, as the cane
delivered by us had been 1£ ton short of the specified
quantity for some time, and our £lOO deposit would be
held as forfeit, unless the “contractor” would sign a
document confessing his inability to carry out the work.
Myself and all but 11 of the gang had joined too late to
pay anything towards this deposit, and were not allowed
to have any say. The others put it to the vote among
themselves, and resolved by a majority of two not to
sign. Tney held to their resolution for another 10 days,
and then signed “ confession of failure,” and divided the
£lOO among them. Nigger advocates in N.Q. are reso-
lute not to give white labor a fair trial; and they won’t
stick at anything to effect their purpose.

An Impeached. Lands Minister.
The resignation of Victorian Lands Minister
M’Kenzie, by reason of a charge brought by
Labor member Prendekgast of using his Minis-
terial position for his own private profit, is a heavy
blow to the Irvine Government. It has been in
office a very short time, and imputations of such
a kind are about the worst charges under which a
new “Reform” Ministry can suffer. Also,
when a Tory squatter Cabinet has to drop
mer board, even temporarily, its most bitterly
Tory squatter with the shadow of an awk-
ward accusation upon him, the situation is
more difficult than if it was a different kind
of man who went overboard. It isn’t so much
Pkendergast’s charges as M'Kenzie’s own
explanations that constitute the trouble. Even if
nothing absolutely illegal has been done or alleged,
the story as told by M'Kenzie himself sounds
none too well as a matter of high moral principle.
The gist of the ex-Minister’s narrative is as
follows:
He held 14,000 acres of State land under lease in the
broken and rather inaccessible Fumina district at a very
low rent. He says he lost money by doing this, but
kept the lease so as to have a block of well-watered
country to fall back upon in dry seasons.
Last vear, when the drought was at its worst, he applied
for and obtained from his own Department, a lease of
another 7300 acres adjoining the above, at a yearly rental,
it is alleged, of £6— as M'Kenzie puts it, at “the usual
rental.” He says he took up the extra block because he
wa9 about to put stock on his own 14,000 acres, and it
was cheaper to take up the adjoining block than to repair
his fence so as to keep his stock within bounds. Mr.
M'Kenzie says that this 7300 acres was so inaccessible
that no one else wanted it or would take it. In his own
words, “no person with starving stock would look at
that patch of land.” Yet he didn’t mind looking at it
himself—to a certain extent, at all events.
Then he “ made other arrangements” for his own sheep
near Wood’s Point, and sub-let the Fumina land, for some
large amount. With the rest he sub-let the 7300-
acre block which “no person with starving stock would
look at,” though how much of the rent he received was
on account of that patch doesn’t appear.
He either got permission from his own Department to
sub let, or did it without permission.
He admits having taken up another grazing lease in a
name that was not his own—in other words, having got a
third party or dummy to apply to his own Department
for him. He says this was done in the interests of purity
of administration, so that his underlings might sit down
hard on the dummy if the case called for it when they
might hesitate to sit on their own chief (Minister
M'Kexzie’s representative was not sat upon, either by his
underlings or by himself).
Minister M'Kenzie, by his own showing, looks
like a man who, in a time when land with water
and grass on it was immensely valuable, took up
a large area of it and made a profit out of the
community’s distresses. Whether he did anything
illegal, and whether in applying to himself for land
he gave himself any unfair advantage, is a differ-
ent question. A Parliamentary committee is to
enquire into that. But, however legal his actions
may have been, they look grasping and un-
patriotic. It is further alleged that the same
gentleman who took Minister M'Kenzie’s
land at a handsome profit to Minister M'Kenzie
had, in the worst period of the drought, 68,000
acres of land in well-watered Gippsland leased to
him by Minister M'Kenzie’s department for £2l
a year. This was about three times the area of the
land which he leased (it is said, for something
between £5OO and £1000) from Minister M'Kenzie.
The difference in the rental which Minister
M'Kenzie charged for his own land and for the
public land appears to be just as staggering as the
difference in the price at which M'Kenzie let the
public estate to himself and the price at which he
sublet it to the other man.
Taking “ A Firm Stand.”
From Melbourne come two unsatisfying items of
information. One is a vague and undefinable
assurance that, if the House of 48 Ricli Landlords
knocks the Reform Bill on the head, or amends
it out of all useful shape, and throws out the Pro-
bate Duties Bill again, the Irvine Government
will “take a firm stand.” The other is a rumor
to the effect that in such a case the Irvine Govern-
ment will take the heroic course of dissolving the
Assembly and appealing to the country. If it
does the latter, it might just as well let the
matter alone. The Victorian Council doesn’t care
bow often the Assembly appeals to the country,
•r what Assembly majority the Government
omes back with, and it has scoffed at that body’s
•ppeals and majorities for nearly 50 years. The
<ttBe, as set forth in the unpublished fables of
<Esop, i s something as follows :
The mouse decided that the cat was more of a nuisance
man usual, so he dissolved himself and appealed to the
Jountry for authority to remove that animal. And he
same back with a large majority.
And the cat didn’t move.
When the mouse had read the Bill for the removal of
the eat three times and still the cat didn’t move, the
mouse dissolved himself and appealed to the country
again, and came back with a larger majority.
And as nothing happened he dissolved again and
appealed to the country, and got the largest majority of
all, and it was decided that the cat’s conduct was Un-
constitutional.
And the mouse kept on dissolving hims'elf until he
became quite soluble and had to keep himself in a jug, and
things went on just as before.
hieh unpublished fable about expresses the
i idieulous. position into which Victoria has
allowed its unreformed Parliament to drift.
Ineie is nothing to be gained by dissolving
16 j ~s s? n . W as for “taking a firm
stand,” it is highly questionable if the Irvine
(government has the necessary amount of stand
about it. Nothing ordinary in the way of a
stand is of the slightest use. A century ago
(-Treat Britain faced a somewhat similar-problem
when it decided to shift the alleged Irish Parlia-
5 1 . en ,k.,. wasn’t really an Irish Parliament; the
disabilities laid on Catholics shut out the Irish
people from it almost bodily ; and as a Parliament
consisting mainly of alien Protestant oppressors
in Ireland it had made itself utterly loathsome by
l murder, torture, and other horrors of
the Middle Ages. As it wouldn’t abolish or
reform itself for the good of the country, the gang
of ruffians which formed the majority was bought
out, and departed from the scene for a considera-
tion. Victoria must either follow that precedent,
or make its House of Landlords shift by revolu-
tionary means, or organise a great movement to
hand over to the Australian Parliament all the
urgent social and economic questions on which
the sodden House of Landlords blocks progress.
Merely taking “a firm stand” in the ordinary
acceptation of the term is just as useless as taking
a hat-stand.

The Curious and Bumptious British
Mental Attitude.
Almost every eminently respectable British
paper has had some remarks to make about the
exclusion of the six hatters (who weren’t excluded
at all), and the same quaintly and bumptiously
British attitude characterises the crowd. The
following sample (out of dozens) is taken from
London Statist :
We can imagine nothing better calculated to bring
about disruption. It is to be recollected that Australia
was originally acquired by the people of the United
Kingdom ; that it was settled by the people of the United
Kingdom ; that it was the people of the United Kingdom
who granted to the descendants of those settlers home
rule ; and that the intention all through was that Aus-
tralia should continue an integral part of the British
Empire, and should be open to all Britishers. The pro-
tection of Australia has been by Great Britain. The cost
of building up Australia has been at the expense of Great
Britain. And for the descendants of British subjects
settled in Australia, protected, fostered, encouraged, in
every way favored by the mother country, to turn round
now and refuse to allow their own relatives and fellow-
citizens to enter Australia is an act of actual hostility.
What would be thought here if Irishmen or Scotchmen
were refused admission into England? And yet the
Commonwealth of Australia professes to be an integral
part of the British Empire, while it refuses to allow six
British workmen to land upon its territory, except they
can prove that they are possessed of special skill which
cannot be found anywhere else in the Commonwealth.
Even from the narrow view of Australian interest, the
policy of this Immigration Act is utterly mistaken.
This is the typical British attitude in the matter—
the attitude of the British person in England who
can’t see that any other British person who lives
in a “ colony” has any of the ordinary rights of a
human being, or of a British citizen. Consider
the same question from another standpoint:
Australia was originally acquired by the people of the
United Kingdom. At that date we were as much the
people of the United Kingdom as the Statist or any other
British person, so we acquired it as well as they. And
we have some voice in our own acquisition.
It was settled by the people of the United Kingdom,
or rather by a small section of them. That is to say it
was settled by us— not by the Statist which stayed com-
fortably at home.
The people who stayed at home had the unutterable
insolence to decree, for nearly balf-a-century, that every
British person who went to Australia and helped to add
a new continent to the British Empire, should thereby lose
all self-governing rights and privileges and become subject
to the despotism of a British military Governor. At last,
with great reluctance, they partly withdrew the curse, and
enacted that the British person in Australiashould manage
his own affairs within certain limits—that he should only,
as a punishment for expanding the empire, lose all claim
to have a voice or a vote in deciding the policy of the
empire, and should be so far deprived of self-governing
rights that his legislation should be liable to be vetoed by
the Crown (which never dares to veto the legislation of
the British person who stays at home), on the advice of
the Ministry created by the British persons who stayed
at home. Also all high Imperial office is, in effect, closed
against him, and he can’t even be Governor of his own
State. The British person who stays at home actually
expects the equally British person who goes abroad and
builds up an empire to be abjectly grateful because things
are not worse, though the latter lives under conditions
that would cause a revolution if applied to England—for
even England would revolushe if it were deprived of all
control of the Empire’s naval and military policy, and
saw its Parliamentary Acts vetoed by some authority
over which it had no control—the Kaiser, for instance.
And the British citizen who stays at home and sees his
country enriched by the gold that comes from Australia,
by the rents that come from Australia, by the profits of
great British land-grabbing corporations in Australia, by
the profits of Australian silver coinage, by the exploiting
of Australia by British trading companies of all kinds,
really has a vague idea that Australia was built up at the
expense of Great Britain. He couldn’t tell to save his
life exactly where the building-up expense came in, but
it is a tradition with him that is somewhere around.
Above all, the British individual who has spent all his
life in a London office has an idea that somehow or other
he pioneered the Australian wilderness, struggled over
the mountains, tracked out the deserts of Mount Browne
and Kalgoorlie and Cooper’s Creek, and the wildernesses
of the Overland Telegraph line ; and he is really indignant
under his glistening shirt-stud that the Australianised
Briton who actually did these things isn’t sufficiently
grateful to him, for saying he did them. And the idea
that the Briton who went out and added a new continent
to the British empire, and his sons and grandsons, should
dare to do anything displeasing to the fat, lazy, un-
enterprising Englishman who stayed at home in an office,
and never expanded the empire worth 2d.—well, it makes
the person in the office in London feel too shocked for
words.

If Our Credit Were Stopped.
One Horatio BoTtomley, widely and unfavor-
ably known in many quarters, has been speaking
rather sternly in London about this country’s
anti-colored-alien policy, and threatens that unless
Australia mends its ways Britain will lend it no
more money. Supposing that event did happen
it might be a pretty bad shock at first—yet, alter
all, what a razzle Australia might have ! An
enormous amount of the respect this country pays
to Britain arises out of the desire to keep on good
terms with the man who lends the money, and if
we were once certain that that man would lend no
more, Australia would feel something like Mr.
Toots when he came to the end of his last term
at Dr. Blimber’s commercial academy, and
threw off his allegiance, and called the principal
“Old Blimber.” Now, when a big loan falls
due, Australia absolutely grovels to get it renewed
for the sake of its credit, and pays from £50,000
to .£300,000 in commission, perks, steals, stamps,
boodle, etc., for getting it done. When Australia’s
credit doesn’t need to be preserved any longer, it
will probably cease to worry about these things.
It will, in all probability, just remark, “Gentle-
men, this loan is due. We can’t pay it, but just
hang on to your securities till we can. Consider
them renewed indefinitely at 3 per cent, or 3£
per cent. Send in the documents and get them
rubber-stamped ‘ renewed till further notice.’ ” _ It
might even go further and make its foreign
bondholders subject to income - tax, from
which they have hitherto been free—various
countries have done that when they became quite
certain that they had no further use for their
credit in London. These things might not be
strictly honest, but they would probably be as
honest as could be expected under the circum-
stances ; nobody knows till he tries how much of
his honesty is his own, and how much of it arises
out of a desire to raise another loan. When any
Tory rose to remark that some proposed measure
“would produce a bad effect in financial circles in
London,” the House, for the first time in the
memory of any man now living, would be able to
dash financial circles in London with a loud,
off-hand dash. Australia would grow free arid
riotous, and cock.. its hat over one ear,
and ostentatiously jump on English financial
papers in its club. When any one told the
Treasurer that our securities were down to £BO in
London, lie would be able to say frankly that he
didn’t care if they were down to 2d. in London..
Then a Repudiation Party would begin to arise
which would ask how on earth Britain could ex-,
pect us to pay the interest when it wouldn’t lend
us the money to pay the interest with. There
would be bad times if Britain definitely stopped
our credit, but there would be hilarious times, too.
Australia might have only 3d. left, but it would
drink that 3d. with a large, hairy howl, and refuse
to go home till morning on any terms.

Smellful Hobart.
Hobart, which, if it were a decently clean city,
would have the chance of being hospitable to
20,000 or so visitors from the hot mainland every
summer, is at last thinking of washing up a little.
The present sanitary state of the naturally beauti-
ful little city would disgrace a Chinese town.
Loathsome liquid refuse is run into cobble-stone
gutters, where it festers in the sun between storms
(sometimes for weeks at a time). Open sewers of
the most primitive kind are provided, as for
instance (to quote an official report):
By Act of Parliament the Hobart Rivulet, which flows
through the centre of the city, has been dedicated as a
public sewer. Accordingly, the sewage from numerous
dwellings and business premises throughout the city is at
present directly discharged into it. From a point just
above Macquarie-street, where it crosses the rivulet, to a
point near Harrington-street, the bed of the Hobart
Rivulet is formed either of concrete or stone pavement.
A channel about four feet in width has been constructed
down the centre, for the purpose of carrying off the dry-
weather flow. The paved portion of the rivulet varies in
width from 13 feet to 30 feet. The various pipes which
carry off the sewage discharge their contents directly on
to the paved floor, where it remains until such time as
the scavenger comes along with his broom and sweeps it
into the channel in the centre. The existence of such a
state of things is most reprehensible, and calls for imme-
diate and drastic remedial measures, for it is only in very
rare instances (in times of continuous heavy rainfalls)
that the paved bed receives a thorough cleansing. The
sight and smell of such masses of filth awaiting the ad-
vent of the broom are objectionable and highly offensive.
Natural results, a constantly-infected atmosphere
and an annual typhoid epidemic (so that there
will not be too many recoveries from typhoid,
Hobart Hospital is built by the banks of this open
sewer). It is now proposed to lay down a fairly-
civilised sewerage system. Certainly it is only to
be a small affair, confined to the city area, and
discharging at Point Macquarie right into Hobart
harbor, but as the city population is not very great
(about as large as that of a big Sydney suburb),
and as, before discharge, the sewage will be partially
treated in a septic tank, the system will probably
serve well enough for a generation or so. It has
the advantage of being well within the means of
even so poor a community as Hobart. Mr. E. i 1.
Wilkinson, the engineer for drainage, calculates
the total cost at £79,341, and the annual charge
for interest, up-keep, and repayment of debt at
£5550. Hobart should not hesitate a moment,
but seize this cheap opportunity of stepping into
the ranks of civilised towns.

The “ Injunction ” Weapon.
In the general campaign against trades-unionism,
the “ injunction ” in Equity is proving a powerful
weapon. As regards this campaign, its sudden
origin is mysterious ; it seems to have sprung up
in a night, as it were. Within the memory of
most, the London Dockers’ strike was evoking
general sympathy with trades-unionism through-
out England and Australasia; and if, in the
enthusiasm of a strike in those days, a union-man
was betrayed into such eccentricities of behavior
as went beyond rudeness to a “blackleg,” the
action was tacitly condoned. But, as organised
Labor has progressed, perfected its methods, and
become a political as well as an industrial power,
“ middle-class ” sentiment seems to have under-
gone a change, which is now all at once made
manifest. It would seem as if the bourgeoisie was
willing enough to give assistance to Labor when
revolting against practically intolerable condi-
tions of servitude, but promptly swings to the
other side when further concessions are claimed,
well enough warranted in justice, but not so
markedly necessary as those at issue in earlier
struggles. At any rate, whatever explanation is
the true one for its existence, the fact is obvious
enough ; the Labor cause is faced by antagonism
now, the degree of which is almost unexampled.
Not only is the Political Labor Party attacked,
but also the very principle of trades-unionism. The
favorite method in the latter attack is by “injunc-
tion in Equity ”; of this, there have been some
recent Australasian examples, and its value
as a weapon against militant trades-unionism is
undoubted in all the States except Maoriland and
New South Wales, where industrial arbitration
laws are in force. In those States —where the
law expressly decrees that preference of employ-
ment shall be given to trades-unionists, other
circumstances being equal—it is difficult to see
how a Supreme Court can logically punish a
trades-union for taking steps to boycott non-union
labor, or can restrain it from such action. But in
England, and in the Australian States where
trades-unionism, as a militant force, is tolerated
rather than legalised, there is little doubt that, if
the Judges share the temper of the class from
which they are drawn, trades-unions will find
their power practically paralysed by the free use
of “injunctions.” If a trades-union can be
restrained from seeking to compass the dis-
missal of blacklegs, from protesting against
a reduction of wages by inducing the
workers to strike, from taking action to
seduce from their employment men who would
take the place of striking unionists, it may as well
close its offices and dissolve. There is no longer
any real reason for its existence. And that is the
end which the organised employing classes aim at,
and which several recent “ injunction ” incidents
seem to suggest that they will aim at successfully.
Of course, that would not end the matter, Kill
the trades-unions and you will not thereby kill the
grievances which made trades unions necessary
and the resolution which made them possible.
The workers, repulsed at one point, will concen-
trate their energies to find relief at another, and
the bourgeoisie and its press organs, such as the
Sydney Daily Telegraph, will have occasion
to mourn that they ever conceived the idea of
screwing down the safety valve. The movement
which seeks to give the laborer a more honest
wage for his work, which opposes a social system
that condemns the majority of mankind to hope-
less poverty, cannot be crushed out at this stage.
It has gone too far. The majority has too much
power, and is too conscious of its power. The
injunction, and all the other legal subtleties by
which it is sought now to kill the vital force of
trades-unionism, seem to promise a great deal to
the cause of capitalism. But the promise is a
snare. The social-reform movement will not be
killed, will not even be seriously retarded ; it may
be turned into a more dangerous direction;
and that is the consummation which its enemies
now strive for —unwittingly.

Such Men !
Sir,—Without a doubt the most pleasing event of the
week is the election of Mr. Robert Reid to the Senate.
He is a worthy successor to that splendid man, Mr.
Frederick Sarqood. It is to be hoped that in the elec-
tions, now within measurable distance, we shall have
many such men coming out for the Senate and Lower
House, in place of the many professional politicians that
now occupy seats. . . . Abolish payment of members,
and you get rid of the undesirable class of men who now
get there, and we shall then get decent legislation, and
laws that will allow all to manage their own business
affairs ; and the country will prosper.— J. Leigh Jones.
[A letter to a Sydney daily paper. Note that the sort of
gentleman this correspondent is always signs “J. Leigh
Jones,” or “F. Critchley Snooks,” or “T. Park hurst
Smith,” the reason of which is that one must appear
important somehow, if not by being important, then by
preying upon the alphabet.]
It is a curious thing that in a professedly Chris-
tian community, which should believe that the
poor are the salt of the earth, and the rich the
damned or probably so; in a professedly educated
community which should, with even nodding
acquaintance with any of the principles of phil-
osophy, know that those qualities which make a
man rich in commercial competition are precisely
those which make it least likely that he will be of
an altruistic and public-serving type—the views
of Mr. J. Leigh Jones should find fairly general
acceptance. There is practical unanimity among
Men who Have and a very-widely-prevailing
notion among Men who Have Not, and can never
hope to Have, that the work of governing the
country is in good hands when a wealthy draper or
a more than ordinarily predatory stock-exchange
speculator condescends to go into politics. Such
men, it is reasoned, are likely to do well for the
community, because they have done well for
themselves at the expense of the community.
But apart from the fact that it is only
when tired of doing well for himself and
anxious to gratify some social or other vanity that
the rich man is willing to go into politics with the
dregs of his brain-power— is a graduation at the
school of predatory commercialism the best pre-
paration for public life ? The successful business
man in the drapery line and most other distri-
buting and speculative branches of trade—we do
not refer to the men who build up large manu-
factures and are actual producers mostly
succeeds at the expense of others. His whole
training is directed to teaching him to hood-
wink and bamboozle the public, to sell them
cheap things dear, or to induce them to sell him
dear things cheap. That is the secret of 30 per
cent, profits and large fortunes. To put this man
into politics and ask him to forget all about his
own profits and the profits of his business for the
sake of the community’s profit is to ask him to
turn his back on all the traditions of “business.”
This fact should be obvious enough, but very
little is obvious to the J. Leigh Joneses ; and it
is extraordinary how many J. Leigh Joneses
there are in the world.

Chamberlain’s “Tact.”
A large amount of the praise that has been ex-
E ended on Joseph Chamberlain for the “tact”
e has displayed in South Africa seems quite un-
called for. One remark in particular, instead of
suggesting tact and diplomacy as alleged, reads
rather like a clumsy putting of both feet into a
delicate situation. Chamberlain has told the
Boers that the late war was inevitable because
“two proud and kindred races had gone to South
Africa to stay,” and their natural desire for
supremacy made it impossible to avert a collision.
It would surely be difficult to say anything
clumsier. The two proud and kindred races
are still there. The elements which make
collision inevitable are still there. Britain
has thrice annexed the Transvaal, and
twice the Boers have shaken off the
yoke. They hope to shake it off again, and
Chamberlain has awkwardly reminded them of
the possibility of another war with that end in
view, for his luckless remark applies just as well
to the next collision as to the last one. The mere
fact that a British army is just now in Pretoria is
no proof that the present settlement is any more
definite than the other two. There has been a
British army on an errand of conquest before now
in Paris, and a French army in London, and they
are both gone and have left no trace. The Boers
have seen the back view of two British forces
retiringacrossthe Vaal, and theback view of a third
isn’t impossible. Anyhow, Joseph Chamberlain
has explained to them, in effect, that the hatchet
is unburiable —that where there are two proud
and warlike races in one country a struggle for
supremacy is inevitable. And they are still there,
with all the time between now and Judgment
Day to argue the question of supremacy. The
annexer always assumes that the settlement just
arrived at is permanent and unshakable; the
annexed always regards it as a mere passing
incident. And a look at the map of Europe as it
fluctuated from Caesar down to Chamberlain
shows that it is a toss-up. which is right.

the recent A.N. A. fete. Replying to the toast of
