with socialism.” That, of course, was to please
the Tory faction that supports his Tory-tinged
Government. Continuing, “the strong man”
observed, “That may be a bold statement to
make in this or any other company, but I have
not the slightest hesitation in announcing it as
the honest result of eareful investigation on my
part.” It must be recorded to the credit of the
A.N.A. that a portion of Irvine’s audience at
once dissented from the assertion. There were a
couple of interjections in which Irvine was for-
cibly invited to look at Maoriland, and asked
whether he wanted to go back to the sweating
days when there was no State interference. “ The
strong ” man then began to wobble in a shameless
manner. He floundered around for a few minutes,
and then plunged headlong into, an advocacy of the
application of the principles of socialism to the

r*
“ Robin Hood ” is rather opera with a
flavor of fun than comic opera. It runs on the
traditional lines of grand opera, is full of
meritorious and fairly original music, and must
depend for success on good singing—especially in
the choruses and concerted numbers. And it gets
very good singing from the Royal Comic Opera
co. at Sydney Palace. The cast is far and away
ahead of that entrusted with the last perfor-
mance of this opera at Her Majesty’s, as regards
soloists ; and the chorus maintains the standard of
excellence established decades ago. There are
male members of the R.C.O. co. who are growing
grey in the chorus (female members aren’t allowed
to grow grey, because of the brisk matrimonial
demand for them), and a man who has been prac-
tising to sing in tune 15 years or so ought to be
proficient. The tinkers’ chorus, the Sherwood
Forest hymn in praise of nut-brown ale, and many
another part-song, show the good work of the
supernumeraries of the co., and do much to estab-
lish the success of the present “ Robin Hood.”
* * * •
As to the principals, “Miss Carrie Moore
has at last deigned to show the public something
of her old merit, and as maid Marian—except for
a tendency now and again to force her voice—is
unexceptionable, bright, and winsome. Miss
Florence Young, as Allan-a-dale, is as successful
almost as in “Paul Jones”; boy parts suit her
voice and style. Miss Maud Chetwynd is bright
and pert, and Miss Ghiloni is pleasant in various
ways. Of the long list of male principals no one
is open to hostile criticism, and some do especially
good work—-Mr. Roberts, Mr. Thorley (a fine
figure of “Little John”), Mr. Fred Young and
Mr. Stannis Leslie. The “comic relief” of the
opera is provided by Mr. Lauri as the Sheriff of
Nottingham and Mr. Ward as Sir Guy. The
make-up of the latter, as a flagrant idiot of a type
which is common enough in these days among the
British aristocracy, is probably a gross libel on
the nobility of Merrie Englande in the days of
Richard the Big Crusader. The lords of those
times had their faults; they didn’t wash to any
extent, and they had no idea of the refined Stock
Exchange methods of robbing their neighbors, and
they were predatory in a rough, unpleasant fashion,
which made a mess on the Castle floor and dis-
turbed the air with horrid groanings; still they
were not weaklings, for it was an age when the
tallest survived and the broadest came out
on top. Mr. Ward’s anachronistic nobleman is
a poor creature who could only have been reared
in an incubator; but he is, all the same, genuinely
funny and a marvel of make-up. “ Robin Hood ”
is to be continued until further notice, and will be
followed by “ The Geisha.” The idea of present-
ing a new opera this season has been abandoned.
The Palace stage is so small that to have new
scenery painted for it would be a wasteful pro-
ceeding ; the canvases would be unavailable for
any other theatre.

“ The Lady Barmaid,” which is under
grave suspicion of being really “ The New Bar-
maid,” was begun at Sydney Criterion last Satur-
day, and proved itself twin sister to numerous
other musical comedies that Sheridan has staged
during his stay. Its general ideas are about as
familiar as the now ancient programme observa-
tion that “ every lady who removes her hat during
a performance shows a graceful consideration for
those occupying seats behind her.” Johnny, in
Act 1, is a head-waiter who, curiously enough, does
not upset things—the stage waiter generally does.
Sydney Carden ishis affiuent brother with a horrible
accent. In Act 11., John F. wears the maroon
complexion and red suit with blue facings that
marks an African millionaire; while bis brother,
t hrough too much high living,is reduced to the tray-
carrying business. The rest of the family (who
don’t appear) are mother, who is on the job again;
and Eliza, doing time in Darlinghurst. Mean-
while, the two barmaids are separately discovered
on strong absence of evidence to be the long-lost
daughter of a sun-dried Colonel. The rest of the
plot consists in Johnny, as waiter, telling the good
new barmaid to be sure and let them hear the
cash-box bell ring occasionally, the bad ex-barmaid
having neglected to do so. When he shows her
how todrink toast-and-water with the fleeting dude
of the moment, and charge liim a shilling for it,
the audience applauds till Johnny advises them
not to as it is too hot. There are one or two fair
musical items in “The Barmaid,” notably “Here-
ditary Tendencies,” by Johnny and Sydney
Carden, a topical song or two, and an item by
Tommy, otherwise Miss Tilke Dunbar. Miss
Celia Mavis, in white with red ribbons, is a model
for all publichouse maidens to follow ; while the
Folies Ballet and the dancing of Miss Minnie
Hooper are as good as previous displays of a simi-
lar nature, and not widely different from most of
them.
* * * *
The more or less Hungarian opera is doing
well in its third week at Sydney Royal, and
Madame Slap, as the two girls and the young
man, is as brilliant as ever. It is difficult to fix
the probable date of the story, but as Madame
Slap goes off and badly licks the Austrians in one
capacity and a uniform, while she stays behind
and mashes the short fat Count in two other
capacities and skirts, it is probably supposed to
have something to do with the revolutionary
period of ’4B and ’49. Still, Hungarian history is
filled for nearly four centuries with more or less
reliable tales about how a noble but not extra-
clean nation went out and licked the Austrians,
so, after all, it might belong to almost any date.
And, anyhow, a decent opera has a right to belong
to any date it thinks best.
* * * *
“ The Breaking of the Drought ” has now
an honorable life of many weeks to look back upon,
and it is not likely that anything will need to take
its place at Sydney Lyceum before the end of the
season. The numerous persons who prognosticated
that the Drought Drama would prove too sad for
a sympathetic city overrated the sensitiveness
of Sydney people altogether. The funereal sheep
and the withered horse and other items of public
distress are received with strong approval, and
there seems to be a demand for more of these
bones rather than less.
* * * *
Sydney Tivoli has no new features this
week, Inaudi, the Gracie Emmett Co., Wallace
Brownlow, and Professor Maccann being still the
chief attractions.
* * * *
Only one of the Melbourne shows drew a
full congregation last Saturday. Every seat for
the Armes Beaumont concert was sold some time
before the event, and the night was not quite hot
enough to keep people from taking out their
money’s worth. They had paid to go there, so
rather than make a loss over the transaction they
tottered up to the Town Hall, beaded with mois-
ture and haunted by a fear that Melba might beg
to be excused on account of the high temperature.
But Melba was as good as her word, or even
better. She sang, in beautiful voice, “Olieto
8uol,” from “Les Huguenots,” and gave “Doro-
thy May,” the happiest of her simple ballads, for
an encore. Subsequently she sang Mozart’s sweet,
churchy “ Voi che sapete ” bracketed with “Fille
de Cadiz,” which is saucy, and after several
attempts to escape the encore she threw in Tosti’s
“Serenata,” to her own piano accompaniment.
The weather favored the vocalists all round.
Melbourne was glad to find its old friend, Miss
Ida Osborne at her best, and the voice of
Miss Amy Murphy, from Maoriland, scored a
particular success. But, next to Melba, nobody
gave more satisfaction than Armes Beaumont,
the tenor who made his Sydney debut as Thaddeus
in the “ Bohemian Girl,” in 1863. Hearing him
on Saturday, it was hard to believe that this same
Beaumont had sung the principal tenor parts in
76 operas during the Lyster era, which closed in
1880, about the time The Bulletin was bom.
The voice was “ worked to death ” in its prime,
and still it flourishes when wanted in its 63rd
year. Beaumont deserved to have Melba sing for
him. Fate has been very unkind to him. If he
were a veteran English tenor with his present
vocal ability, instead of a veteran Australian
tenor, -he might fairly expect to be imported to
this country on his reputation, at a vast salary, in
about another five years from now.
* * * *
“ Zaza,” which might be advertised as a
nice, warm play for nice, warm evenings, promises
to achieve its financial purpose and show a profit
on the production. In some respects “ Zaza ” is
a likeable play. The plot is a false conception,
but the duologues—and the dialogue is mostly
duologue throughout—contain some human truths
re the husband, the wife, and the other woman.
Cascart, the music-hall man, in bright brown suit
and red necktie, takes the fancy of this scribe
because he comes along from time to time and
practically denounces the devotion of Zaza to her
blue-eyed commercial traveller (or whatever he is)
as an outrage on common sense. He says
to Zaza—“You know it can’t last, old girl,”
or words to that effect, and he pro-
poses to conduct the female scorcher back
to her native atmosphere of the ’alls. Yet the
dramatist gives a gullible public to suppose that
Zaza’s feelings would never have changed but for
her discovery of a lawful wife in the case. The
commercial gent might have been a confirmed
bachelor with a talent for keeping his occasional
infidelities dark, in which case Zaza would have
needed to love him furiously for evermore, or
forfeit her character as a sentimental heroine.
She took the man on spec, after throwing the
handkerchief to him. There was no question of
marriage between them. She had no misgivings
re his Saturday to Monday absences. Love had
purified the rorty song-and-dance artist of even
the suspiciousness that music-hall experience
breeds. Rats ! Therefore, the artless child of
fiction could get no particular public admiration
without the aid of a grievance, and the wife whom
Bernard treats to chocolate is the grievance.
Somehow the grievance is inextricably mixed with
the chocolate. The tidiness and good cookery of
Bernard’s orthodox home, as compared with the
untidiness and abominably bad cookery at Zaza’s
domicile, is another grievance against the Man.
Nellie Stewart gets a good grip of the heroines
grievance and shakes it for all it is worth. Some
nights her acting seems more spontaneous than
on other nights. When she scores her biggest
effect, a vehement amphitheatre accepts Zaza as
a genuinely noble character, and despises the
pleasant, married Bernard for being. anxious to
remain on good domestic terms with his wife and
child.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal there is a further
continuance of the things that happen “When
London Sleep®.” A previous reference to the
aforesaid drama stated that Inman was dropped
down a trap-hole in the last act. The Bulletin
withdraws the statement. Inman is saved from
the trap-hole by the sudden apparition of Bert
Bailey, consequently there is nothing except a
whack on the head to prevent him from giving a
trapeze performance outside the Indian Exhibi-
tion. ’Which explains why he clothes himself in
pink fleshings in order to conceal himself within
a brazen image what time Willard is preparing to
burn Miss Duggan to ashes on the adjacent grid-
iron. This is Willard’s second attempt to bum
Miss D. during the evening, so when the brass
god yields up its Inman, and the schemes of
Willard are foiled again, the p>olice step in to pre-
vent a third conflagration.

The programme at the Opera House this
week is much the same as before, and the weather
at time of writing suggests that any violent
change of bill would be a wasted effort. The
exhausted panto, at Her Majesty’s comes to a full
stop to-morrow (Friday), after which the theatre
will take a fortnight’s rest prior to a return of the
opera co. The twice-a-night show at the Gaiety
has already full-stoppied, but somebody threatens
to re-op>en the ill-fated house with a once-a-night
variety co. The delusion that Melbourne can
support two variety shows at once has been
frequently exploded, but never destroyed.
** * #
Adelaide Tivoli, now re-opened with the
old-fashioned first pmrt restored, is doing well
with an all-round co. that includes the humorist
Spry and the Harbecks. Seeley and West, just
arrived from England (by way of Perth), have
been droppied into the programme, and the girls
who play net-ball are already in possession of the
stage. At the Royal, the Dampiers alternate
“Monte Cristo” and “East Lynne” with one
evening of Shakespeare each week. Wirth’s
Circus was so successful that it extended its
season ; it is now playing in the country towns.

The reserves consist of premium on shares, £466,536;

undivided profit, £11,518. The fact that the profits are
so much below those of the corresponding periods of
1900 and 1901 is set down to the disastrously high price
of coal. In other respects the figures show no material
alteration. The Metropolitan Gas Co. has issued deben-
tures to the extent of £848,000 (£50,000 worth of deben-
tures—presumably its own debentures—have been bought
up by the co. as a reserve fund investment, so apparently
there are £798,000 worth of these securities in circula-
tion). Through the very ill-judged increase of the
dividend to 9 per cent, any margin that might have
existed for the accumulation of a cash fund to pay, or
partly pay, these off at maturity has practically disap-
peared. The co. is making good progress, inasmuch as a
great deal of expenditure for the extension of its business
to new districts is now paid out of earnings, when it
might be charged to capital; but if the dividend had
been kept at 8 per cent, the co. might have, in addition,
done something in the way of a sinking fund, and con-
siderably reduced its load of debt when the debentures
begun to fall due in 1908.

The Launceston Examiner speaks words of
comfort (partial comfort, at all events) to the people of
Tasmania s sloppy isle “ Our debt undoubtedly is
heavy, but we do not, like gome extreme writers, attempt
to draw comparison between it and the debts of other
countries. There is no analogy between a State which
undertakes railway construction and one which leaves it
to be done by private enterprise. Still we have borrowed
lavishly and spent foolishly.” Just so—but the respect-
able middle-aged paper considers it has done its whole
duty when it has made some perfunctory remark like
that, and it never thinks of telling the people exactly
haw lavish and how insanely foolish they have been. And
the respectable middle-aged paper extracts a world of
comfort out of our State railways, which really represent
a comparatively small portion of our borrowings—and
after all it isn’t so very unusual a thing for the State to
own railways. This is how things look when reduced to
hard figures, in a rough and ready way—it isn’t necessary
to pursue the calculation to the last shilling and copper
to point the moral:—

Great Britain (debt about £24 per head) is
omitted, because it is impossible to get the figures
accurately until the war liability i* put in shape. But
though the list is only a partial one, it is very eloquent.
Australia is full of people and papers that talk as if
owning State railways as a set-off to the public debt was
something quite extraordinary, and as if any foolishness
or extravagance was excusable in a perfectly extraordin-
ary country like Australia, which owned State railways.
Also, it is full of complacent idiots who think that every
other country’s debt is a war debt, and who imagine that
when the value of Australia’s railways is deducted from
its debt it compares well with the best of foreign States,
instead of barely holding its own among the worst. Of
course, Australia has some other works to set against the
rest of its debt, but so have other countries also—many
of them are far further ahead in that direction than
we are.

Complete revenue returns for January will not
be available in time for this issue, but those for N.S.W.
are to hand. This is the remarkable situation con-
densed : Revenue for January, 1903, £882,010; being a
decrease of £83,411 compared with January,_ 1902.
Expenditure for January, 1903, £1,103,302; being an
increase of £501,351 as compared with January, 19C2.
Deficit for January, 1903, £221,292. Deficit for the seven
months of the financial year that have already expired,
£927,588 ; and if “ payments in suspense ” are added the
deficit for the seven months is £1,028,438. Also, if the
deficit brought forward from last year is included
the shortage is £1,265,219. All the trust funds
(£5,885,642) have now been borrowed, and £11,874
from the banks in addition, so even the £1,000,000
which is now being borrowed won’t go far. Loan expen-
diture was cut down tremendously in January, 19.3 (it
was £277,937, or £308,239 less than in January, 1902),
from sheer want of any more money to spend. In fact,
January, 1903, closed with an absolutely penniless Govern-
ment “ facing the dawn,” and draper Law, M.L.A., still
wanting £75 for the Balmain Choral Union.

The Deniliquin and Moama Railway Co.
(Deniliquin, N.S.W.) had a better half-year than usual to
Sist December, 1902, despite the lamentable nature of
the season in its district. A very considerable increase
in the receipts for conveyance of live stock is the chief
cause of this sudden affluence. This is where the private
railway co. scores. It isn’t expected to waste its sub-
stance on charity as the State railways do in bad times,
so it can make a lot of money out of starving-stock traffic
and the like, though, when "the stock is passed on to the
Victorian State lines, to be carried the rest of the journey,
the State is expected to convey it at reduced rates, which
don’t pay axle-grease, and the State management is also
dunned for further concessions, and asked, at the same
time, why it doesn’t make its lines pay as the private co.
does ! The Deniliquin and Moama Railway Co., in addi-
tion to its visible profits, has sometimes unexplained
additions to its renewal reserves, which are shown sepa-
rately :

Australia’s fat friends, Dr. Montague and Mr.
Thomas Lawless, of the Red Indian 1.0. F. life assurance
concern, have gone back to Canada, and, according to
their institution’s paper, the following news has re-
turned with them :
“ Already the Independent Order of Foresters has made
its mark in that country (Australia) in that it has com-
pelled two of the strongest and most conservative Aus-
tralian old-line companies to recognise the fraternal
principal in life assurance. I refer to the Australian
Mutual Provident Society and the National Mutual Life
Association, one of which has already established a
fraternal branch so-called, and the other of which is pre-
paring to do so."

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
■contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOJin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch. *

The Berry Consols Extended G.M. Co. have
given a contract for the supply and complete installation
of an underground electric mine haulage plant to the
Austral Otis Engineering Co. Ltd., as sole representatives
of Ganz and Co. Ltd., Budapest, Hungary. The directors
came to this decision after carefully considering the
seven tenders which had been sent in by the following
firms The Austral Otis Eng. Co., G. Weymouth and
Co., The Australian Metal Co , The Australian General
Electric Co., J. A. Newton and Co., Siemens Bros., and
Knox, Schlapp and Go. Although there were lower ten-
ders the directors, after having advice from an electrical
expert, gave preference to The Austral Otis Co., more
especially because of the great satisfaction given by a
haulage plant supplied by that firm to the Great Southern
Consols G.M. Co., Rutherglen. This plant started on the
17th April, 1902, and has been running continuously ever
since without a hitch, both day and night. The plant
which will be supplied to the Berry Consols Extended
G.M. Co. comprises a complete generating station, light-
ing plant, and three mine locomotives, and will be
running in 6 months’ time. The giving of this contract
to the Austral Otis Co. is once more evidence of the high
reputation the Ganz electrical apparatus have established
in Australasia.*

It is not generally known that in the treatment
of ore as much of the valuable minerals is often lost in
the tailings as is saved by the appliances. This is due
in most instances to the inefficient machines used and
the difficulty in saving the fines and slimes. The best
machine to treat the latter classes are Luhrig Vanners.
These machines are in successful daily use in the principal
gold, galena, copper and tin mines in Australia and
Tasmania, proving their superiority over other so-called
slime machines. Messrs W. & J. Lempriere, 506 Little
Collins-street, Melbourne, are the general agents, and full
particulars can be obtained from them.*

Golden Link’s (W.A.) first monthly crushing
from the Eclipse lease averaged only lldwts. per ton
against the 2oa. obtained from the picked trial parcel.
The battery did very good work putting through an
average of 4J tons per stamp daily, and it is said that,
owing to this, a lot of gold was left in the tailings. This
will be recovered later on by cyaniding. The main shaft
on the Link lease is down 1300 ft. and another attempt
will shortly be made to find the Consols lode at this
depth. Co. looks a pretty hopeless concern v/ith its
400,000 shares, and scrip can’t be recommended, more
especially as there is a 2s. liability on them.

SCHWEPPES MALVERN SPA
By Special Appoint=
ment to His Majesty
King Edward VII.
Bottled at Celebrated AXalverzi Springs. n t i,.
This gasbaggy variety of literature is a striking evidence
of the p*)wer of mere catch phrases. The A.M.P. and
the National Mutual are a great deal more .raternal"
than the 1.0. F. ever was, and have been in that state
longer. But in the 1.0. F. vocabulary, when you charge
a man a great deal more for a £lOO policy than the
A.M.P. or the N.M. does, and give him no surrender value,
and incomparably worse security, and make him liable for
“assessments” as well, and deceive him by telling him you
are selling him an extra special cheap policy, and call him
“Brother” Smith—that is the beautiful “fraternal"
system. And if you charge a great deal less for a £lOO
policy, and give better security, and don’t keep a huge
Red Indian on the premises, then you are an “ old line”
office, and “ old line ” is the last human possibility in the
way of contempt according to the 1.0. F. vocabulary. The
A.M.P. and the National Mutual haven’t gone in for the
1.0.F.'s brand of fraternalism, and any statement that
they have done so is simply the 1.0. F. paper’s lie—there-
fore their policy-holders may feel quite safe.

According to its own balance-sheet the New York
and Brooklyn Tobacco Co. has £1240 of reserves,

Of course things are, in one sense, not quite so bad as
they look. The co. doesn’t set aside each year the
income-tax charge for the year, but debits it when paid ;
therefore, 1902 is debited with £l9O income-tax on the
large profits of 1901, instead of being charged about
£llO on the smaller profits of 1902. But even if 1902
didn’t carry this extra load, the fact remains that the
10 per cent, dividend has been earned with a very bare
margin, and it would have been much better to cut the
dividend down to 7or 8 per cent. In fact, it should
never have been put up to 10 per cent, till a substantial
reserve fund was accumulated.

A large amount of good Australian money might
be saved if the Commonwealth took over, renewed and
consolidated the State debts, but such an action would
diminish somewhat the beastly little importance of the
State Ministers. So Treasurer Turner’s appeal to the
States for full official information about their debts on
which to base a conversion scheme remains unanswered,
Superficial people say that the affair is of no importance ;
the credit of the people in their State capacity is as good
as the credit of the same people in their Federal capacity.
But the money-lender is apt to view the matter differ-
ently. After the Braddon clause expires the Common-
wealth Government will have full control of the prin-
cipal sources of revenue (customs and excise). If it
is liable for the public debt it will pay the interest out of
this revenue. If it isn’t liable it may return the States
enough to enable them to pay their interest, or it may
not —there may be an O’Sullivan kind of Commonwealth
Government by that time which will find ways of spending
the whole lot. In any case a Federal debt will be a first
mortgage on the principal source of revenue ; a State debt
will only be a second mortgage. If the State Govern-
ments choose to do the renewal of their own loans they
deliberately offer their creditors a second mortgage
security when they could, by falling in with Turner’s
proposal, offer a first mortgage. And the interest on
second mortgages is heavy.

The paid-up capital stands at £128,547, so that £44,109
remains nominally intact to provide for the annual loss
on working the concern and any further depreciation on
investments nominally worth £70,729. During 1902 the
co. earned £1032 in various ways, but management
expenses alone absorbed £823 of this, and interest on
deposits and mortgages made the rest of the trouble. If
this and about six other equally unfortunate concerns
ould decide to work together, with one manager, office,
staff, gas-bill, office-boy and board of directors, it is just
barely possible that the Assets Realisation and General
Finance Co. would then be able to pay interest and
working expenses out of earnings. There is very little
apparent hope of doing that “ on"its own.” In every con-
cern that is slowly liquidating there comes a time when
there is so very little left that the income derived from it
can’t possibly support an office and the other appliances
of a limited liability co. single-handed.

For a very young institution, the Darling Downs Per-
manent Building and Investment Society has made an
exceedingly good start, but the management is in much
too great haste to increase the dividend. The original 5
per cent, should have been maintained for another half-
dozen years at least, till a really substantial reserve had
been accumulated. The capital now stands at £15,861,
and by comparison the £7BB of reserves are not large. An
especially good point, however, is that the liability to
depositors is only £350. Depositors have proved a source
of danger to many building societies.

The earnings, though they have again fallen off a little,
are still equal to over 13J per cent, on capital, so the
Perpetual Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. of Tas-
mania is doing very well. It still furnishes as little in-
formation as usual about its investments, and gives
no information at all about what income it derives
from them—“ Commission and Interest” are run together
in one line, and how much is commission and how much
is interest is not explained. Tasmanian financial institu-
tions are mostly pretty solid—the conservatism of the
island prevents them rushing into reckless speculations.
And that same conservatism makes them stick to old
ideas about the dangers of trusting shareholders and the
public with too much information.

The slight falling off in profits suggests bad times and a
sagging tendency in the property market. Even now,
however, the earnings are at the rate of £9 19s. 6d. per
cent, per annum on capital, and very good progress is being
made at writing off the goodwill item, which originally
stood at £67,500. The co.’s principal investments consist
of Treasury bills £7OOO, and £13,502 cash in bank. Pre-
sumably a good part of the latter item consists of fixed
deposits, and the fact ought to be specified. If it is all
current account it looks like a sorrowful waste of
Interest. And even if it is largely fixed deposit it. doesn’t
seem a very profitable mode of investment.

Concerning the much-discussed authorisation of
more debentures by the Sulphide Corp. (8.H.), the board
explained at the annual meeting in London that the power
to issue them would be used “ sparingly and by degrees.”
The co., not content with a creep and a new shaft, has
taken on a friendly law'action too. Pref. shareholders
are entitled to a 10 per cent. div. ; and they claim that,
if a full 10 per cent. div. isnot declared, as much of the
undivided balance as would make up the full pound of
flesh must be “ ear-marked,” and they must get it at the
next division. It’s going to be a cold century for the
“ ordinaries.”

In the half-year to Nov., 1900, the co.’s expenses mopped
up 2s. 3fd. per oz. of the silver, and £lO Is. 6d. per ton of
the lead. If it had cost that much in the past half-year
there would have been a loss of about £19,000 instead of
a gross profit of £73,000. On the basis of last half-year’s
work, production is now possible at Is. lid. for silver,
and £8 19s. 3d. for lead. The directors refer to the
savings made by the new desulphurising and sintering
works; but these savings are not apparent in the
accounts. As a matter of fact, the desulphurising plant
was not fully' at work until the half-year was well through,
and if it does what the directors say, the current half-
year’s accounts should show a much larger profit. So far
as last half-year is concerned, the biggest saving appears
to have been in coke, mainly as the result of the co. sup-
plj’ing nearly the whole of its requirements from its.
own Bellambi (N.S.W.) works.

* • * m
A sample of what Australia can do when it-
isn’t too tired to try :—The B.H. Prop., in the half-year
to Nov., 1891, smelted 127,234 tons ore—“wet” stuff
that almost smelted itself. The coke bill was £100,086..
So far, the co. had been “bluffed off” Australian coke,,
and had bought only German and Welsh. Later on it
tried Australian coke, and finally put up a plant of its.
own, and last half-year made theie nearly all it wanted.
Result— it smelted 134,242 tons of far more intractable!
stuff, and its coke bill was £37,855.

North B.H. Co.’s half-yearly report discloses,
nothing of interest. The co., which is still waiting for-
lead to reach £l2 before it starts its mill, has only £Booi
left in its stocking ; and, as it is slowly sinking its shaft

• • * •
It is assumed by all the Broken Hill co.’s which-
have shut down for a while that they will be able,,
on resumption of work, to produce metal cheaper
than they could when they knocked off; and if they-
can’t there is a cold prospect for shareholders,,
seeing that some of the mines could not then pro-
duce lead at under £l3. The North’s figure was over
£l3 ss. ; the Junction’s and Block 14’s more. With their-
new plant most of them ought to be able to show a.
saving ; but the thing isn’t so dead sure as it has beem
assumed to be. The Sulphide Corporation, for instance,
which has never let expense stand in the way of experi-
ments or new machinery, could produce its lead at £Bl7s.
in 1900; but its last returns work out at £ll os. 6d.
putting silver at the same amount (2s. 3d.) in each case.
Also the full effect of the fall in silver does not seem to-
have been realised by a good many operators in B.H.
stocks, who have got the idea that pretty well everything
depends now on lead. Yet the fact is that, in the case of
the B.H. Prop., a fall of 3d. in silver still more than:
counter-balances a £1 rise in lead—actual figures for last,
half-year were £35,000 and £33,522. With some other
co.’s the fall in silver doesn’t mean relatively as much,,
but in every case it means a lot.

The experts who have been engaged examining
the Cobar and other properties associated with it, which.
are under offer to an English syndicate, have completed
their work, but it is thought that some weeks will
elapse before they will finish their calculations and
transmit their preliminary report. Stated that the-
report is likely to be highly favorable, and, if so, there is
a strong probability that the deal will come off. Nymagee
mine was not included in the original contract, but just,
as they were about finishing their examination of Great
Cohar, the experts got cabled instructions to inspect that,
property. It is understood that negotiations are in
progress to include it in the deal.
• • • *
The flotation of the Conrad Mines, Bora Creek
(N.S.W.) having been successfully accomplished, arrange-
ments have been made for the immediate resumption of
operations under the direction of Mr. John Howell. The
mines have been well developed and great quantities of
high-value ore are ready for stoping. The chief work
now to be undertaken is the erection of a large treatment
plant. The ore is complex and the whole future of the
property depends upon the effectiveness of the plant
selected. From elaborate experimental tests Mr. Howell
is satisfied that the ore can be treated, so as to save all
the metals, at a very reasonable cost. Should this
anticipation be realised the Oonrad Mines will become a
most valuable property.
• • • •
“The Cassilis Co., Vio.,” writes a soured
correspondent, “is a mining will o’ the wisp. For three
years the co. has been ripping out gold, paying wages
and buying machinery. It is working refractory ore suc-
cessfully and putting out almost 800 oz. a month. Yet
the proceeds are swallowed up, and the shareholder gets
nil. Now the directors say they want £2OOO worth more
machinery, and the shareholders will, for some time, get
more of nothing. The four Wilfley tables cannot stop
gold, to the extent of £2OOO a month, going into the
tailings which assay about lOdwts. Two more Wilfleys,
another Edward furnace and a cyanide plant to treat 300
tons of tailings a week are wanted. The present tailings-
heap holds 13,000 tons of sand valued by the co. at £6OOO.
There are 36,000 shares in the co. worth about 275. Some
day, when Gabriel is about due for the band contest, the
Cassilis will pay a div., and by that time each shareholder
will be looking after a furnace and a chlorination plant of
his own."
• * • *
South Star, Ballarat, has been one of the
biggest disappointments of Victoria. Frequently it has
given promise of emerging from the doubtful to a highly
profitable stage, but the promise has never been more
than fitfully fulfilled. The mine has now been brought
to a dead standstill. The bank overdraft is £20,000, and
directors have received intimation that this must be
promptly reduoed. As only one-third of the shares are
contributing, and a 6d. call per month does not do much
more than cover the cost of firewood, and as there is no
production, the directors have been forced to shut down.
Reconstruction is talked of, but what there is to recon-
struct on outsiders cannot see. The incubus of a £20,000
overdraft is likely to weigh down any attempt to reform
the co.
* * • •
The directors of New Loch Fyne mine, Matlock
(Vic.), have been making some interesting experiments to
determine the values of the different sections of the large
dyke formation. It has been shown that practically the
whole of the gold is contained in the quartz veins which
intersperse the dyke, so that the old policy of separating
quartz from country rock, as far as practicable,
will have to be continued. The assumption in all
the Eastern mining districts of Vic., where these
big dyke formations prevail, is that the bodies
are more or less gold-bearing throughout, but the
Loch Fyne experiment must be accepted as evidence that
such is not so. However, it is not the rule for gold-con-
tents of such bodies to be carried only in the quartz.
There are numerous examples to the contrary in W.A.
and N.S.W. In Orange district (N.S.W.) in particular,
there is a big field for investigation in this respect.
There are several alluvial leads which have been worked,
and the experience has been the less quartz the more
gold. The precious metal must have been shed from
diorite and other dykes, which have never been found
and have never even been looked for.

To counteract adverse rumors which were afloat
in London, the directors of the Golden Horseshoe (W.A.)
have issued a special circular, in which they state that there
was nothing in the position or prospects of the mine to
warrant the depression in share values which existed at
the end of last year. _ It is stated that “ the intrinsic
value of the property is greater now than at any previous
date. The ore reserves are larger, and the lodes at lowest
level are rich, wide and strong. The ore reserves actually
in sight and ready for stoping on June 30 last were 446,295
tons, containing 697,2300 z. gold, as against 278,464
tons, containing 460,7250 z., on 31st Dec., 1901. The
directors are assured by Mr. Sutherland that this is a
most conservative estimate, and is made only in relation
to ore actually in sight above 800 ft. level.”

Associated (W.A.) has at last ordered 12 Merton
furnaces to replace the two old long-condemned roasters.
'This substitution will much increase output, and effect
:a saving of about ss. a ton, as roasting by the old plant
works out at about Bs. as against only 3s. by Merton.
In addition, a far higher extraction will be got, as the
■old furnaces, in their dilapidated condition, give such
a, bad roast that the tailings assay about Bdwt. The
imine is looking very well at present, and some splendid
ore is being got at No. 7 level in Tetley’s shaft. The
■diamond drill is just about starting work at the 1050 ft.
ilevel of Judd’s shaft, to try and locate the continuation
■of the rich Oroya chute. Manager Gleisberg is evidently
going to make things hustle.

Sons of Gwalia, Leonora (W.A.) is looking
much better than for a long time past. The lode is open-
ing well on the north of the shaft at No. 7 level, while at
Nos. 5 and 6it was a blank. Under Mr. Loring’s manage-
ment costs have been enormously reduced and efficiency
Increased, so that co. is again working at a profit, and
should soon re-appear as a div.-payer. Unfortunately,
■owing to the resignation of Feldtmann from the firm of
’Bewick, Moreing, and Go., Loring has resigned the
management of the mine, and, together with Pritchard,
manager of the L.V. Consols, is now in charge of the
■firm’s Kalgoorlie office. During co.’s four years’ existence
mine has been bossed by seven different managers, and
will shortly have the eighth. All Bewick, Moreing’s mines
suffer in a similar way. The next man to take charge
may upset all Loring’s work, and again reduce every-
thing to chaos. Shareholders should strongly protest
Against these frequent changes.

SOCIETY
[?]
[?]
N.S.W. Parliamentary economists, what-
ever party they may belong to, should agree to
appoint one member (Wully Affleck is as good as
any) to trace a host of small leakages of taxpayers’
money, and having defined them, present a Bill
for their instant and everlasting stoppage. For
instance, for a Governor’s levee every barrister is
Provided with a cab or other conveyance by the
tate ; on the same occasion every member of
both Houses of Parliament is provided with a
cab. They do not all go, of course, but as many
as like to go get their cabs free ; and, indeed, if
they wish they can take a carriage-and-pair. No
Bill ever passed through Parliament authorising
such fool-pranks. A generation ago an Attorney-
General started the game for his horse-haired
friends, and a Premier for his crowd, and the
impudent robbery endures.
• » * •
One of the worst inconveniences caused
by Bully Bent’s costly “retrenchments” on the
Vic. railways (writes a Bulletin correspondent)
is the midday traffic block at Melb. stations. I
watched a hurrying luncheon crowd trying to get
out of Flinders-street station t’other day. Three
trains had just come in together, every one of
them packed, and for the bulk of the perspiring
contents there was just one gate to get through,
instead of three, as in the pre-Bent days. The
old arrangement was none too speedy, but when
Bent’s seven-days’-work-for-five-days’-pay gate-
keeper had to lock his gate and chase a woman
who gave him the wrong ticket, and imprisoned
40 or 50 people who would get into trouble if late
back at their shops and offices, the full beauty of
Bent’s reforms became suddenly apparent.
Another place in which his praiseworthy policy
has had excellent effect is the Spencer-street
parcels room, where every day scores of people are
to be heard cursing Thomas and all his inadequate
myrmidons because they have missed their trains
in waiting for their possessions.

• • • •
Last Thursday’s issues of the Sydney
evening papers recorded (1) a tragedy at Darling-
hurst, a man shooting his wife and then killing
himself, (2) proceedings against the men charged
with killing a constable by shooting, (3) a Dubbo
suicide, a man shooting himself, (4) a robbery
under firearms at Sydney. In view of the
embarrassed state of the finances, and the serious
frequency of tragedies arising from the careless
or malicious use of firearms, the time seems oppor-
tune for imposing a license-fee and certain regula-
tions on all vendors and purchasers of fire-
arms. A man may not sell poisons with-
out a license, and since effective legislation
on that point was enforced, it is claimed
that in N.S.W. there has been a decrease
of 50 p.c. in poison-casualties. If everyone selling
firearms were obliged to hold a licence and forced
to exercise reasonable discretion in his business,
and if it were necessary to take out a license
in order to own or hold possession of a firearm of
any sort, probably the crop of police-shootings,
family massacres, and didn’t-know-it was-loadeds
would at once decrease. As matters stand, any
fool, any child, any desperado may get a revolver
and ammunition without any question being
asked.
* • * *
Chin Sing, who keeps a store in Lilly
Buck-st., Melb., scored an interesting triumph
over a local Clistian medical man the other week.
The doctor attended Chin Sing for a cut head, put
some stitches in the wound, visited him four times
to see that the healing process was satisfactory,
and charged 10 guineas. The patient paid 7
guineas, and the County Court decided that he
had paid enough. An ordinary Clistian patient
would have passed the bill for 10 guineas—and
owed it. One medical witness, whilst maintaining
that 2 guineas per visit was not an excessive
demand upon a “good mark,” admitted that good
marks were sometimes expected to pay for
bad ones. Which medical doctrine pretty well
explains why the same sort of people who pay for
medical attendance in Britain (where an M.D. is
glad to accept 3s. 6d. a visit as “family doctor”)
are candidates for hospital treatment gratis in
this country. The social standard of the average
hospital patient in Australia is probably higher
than in any other country in the world, not because
Australians are specially disposed to throw them-
selves upon public charity in time of sickness, but
because the medical profession has an inflated
idea of itself, and wants to keep up too large an
importance at the public expense. It was well
that a Melbourne medico received from a Chow a
simple lesson in sweet reasonableness (with costs).
* • • *
The Sydney electric-tram service has
played havoc with the ’bus and cab traffic.
Four years ago there were nearly 400 licensed
’buses plying from suburbs to city ; to-day there
are but 60, and the chances are that by the end of
the year a ’bus will be a rarity anywhere round
Sydney. The trams, the shrinkage of visitors,
the price of horsefeed, and public “ nearness” are
reasons ascribed for the partial passing of the
cabby. Nine years ago there were close on 1650
hansoms in the streets ; now there are only 940,
about seven per cent, of which are rubber-tyred.
• • * *
The speed of the Sydney electric trams is
a matter of serious concern to the public. They
are supposed not to travel at a greater speed than
15 miles an hour when outside the city, but many
of them, when they get good way on, far exceed
this rate. The speed should be placed beyond the
control of the driver altogether by the gearing of
the electric motors being so arranged that 15 miles
an hour would be the absolute limit of speed
secured. A tram which returned empty from
Leichhardt to Sydney at midnight some months
ago is said to have averaged a 40-miles-an-hour
rate. The driver and conductor vouch for the
accuracy of this, but they were careful not to
chronicle their record at headquarters.

If the South Aus-
tralian census returns
are a fair guide, a
man’s chance of losing
an arm (by accident
or otherwise that
doesn’t cost life) is
one in 20,300. If he
loses both arms ht
has the worst luck in
180,000. For every
male that loses an
arm three lose a leg ;
and ten times more people lose one leg
than two. (A good many more lose the two,
but they don’t live). The figures as to
females aren’t very definite; when Melinda has
an artificial limb it isn’t the census-collector that
finds it out —assuming, of course, that he sticks
strictly to census-collecting.

A Rebuke :
For heaven’s sake let Tub Bulletin crawl off the track
that has led it to the imbecility of the Devil’s Year para-
graphs scattered through a recent issue. There are too
many things at work calculated to increase Australian
suicide and robbery-with-violence statistics without any
paper starting to gibber foolishness. If 1903 is the year
of Old Nick because its digits tot up to 13, so was ISO 4,
and 1813, and 1822, and 1831, and 1840. There were five
Devil’s years in the last century. And not only do the
figures of this present blessed year tot up to a baker’s
dozen (by the way, does the association of the two fire
artists in the pull-devil, pull-baker proverb arise from
their supposed joint interest in this number?), but 1912,
1921 and 1930 do the same. There’s absolutely nothing
in the idea but drivelling idiocy, but if it gets repeated as
often as the Dry Dog or the Little Boy at Manly it may
have just enough force to drive a few sun-baked settlers
over the line which allegedly separates them from
lunacy. Instead of trying to further scare people who
have enough to scare them already, what with Woolls-
Rutledge and the drought and the threatened arrival of
Milner, why not point out that if we were decent Moham-
medans and counted from the Hegira it wouldn’t be 1903
at all, wouldn’t reckon up to 13, and so wouldn’t be the
Devil’s Year at all 1 It can only be Devil’s Year in a so-called
“Christian country,” like N.S.VV., for instance. In a
Confucian country it might be the Angel Gabriel’s Year
(or whoever it is that blows the horn in Mongolian
theology).
* * * •
Said that every man has ability enough to
keep a billet that he has ability enough to get;
but if the post of Inspector-General of N.S.W.
police goes to “crawl” ability it will be little
short of a calamity. The capacity for being
servile to the great is exactly the wrong capacity
for a police-officer to have.
• * • •
The news that another blessed archduke
desires to “ renounce his birthright and marry the
daughter of a tradesman ” was announced in Melb.
Argus (and most other Australian daily papers)
under the heading of “ Another Royal Scandal.”
In the eyes of the “ respectable press ” it seems a
shocking thing that a man should want to sacri-
fice a royal title in order to make an ordinary girl
his legal wife. By the way, the Archduke in
question is on the verge of 40. The love of a man
of 40, as Marshall Hall told his Melbourne pupils
a while ago, is of all loves the red-hottest, and,
perhaps, the most purified by fire.
* • * *
Coghlan estimates that the average
Australian consumes annually two sheep and one-
fifth of a bullock, or about 2651 b. per head per
annum—twice as much meat as an Englishman
and three times as much as a Frenchman. The
Queenslander is the greatest meat-eater—3B2£lbs.
per annum being his allowance ; the N.S. Welsh-
man contents himself with 2851b5., while the
Victorian puts away only 2091b5. of flesh yearly.
The frugal Tasmanian is “a whale on” porridge
and potatoes, while in tinned foods the Westralian
comes an easy first.
THE “STRAIGHT GRIFFIN. ”
HE: “ Oh, my honey !”
SHE: “ You drone, why don't you gather me in ?"
The present drought indirectly caused the
shifting of the Quarter Sessions from a northern
N.S.W. town. Through the hard times no
one felt brisk enough to commit felony, and
the small quantity of things worth steal-
ing were carefully guarded, so that the
Judge carried off four pairs of gloves in
succession, with the above result. The next
town is 70 miles off ; expenses in civil actions will
be trebled, and the townsmen are in throes of
indignation at the insult. They contemplate
importing a batch of burglars to recover their lost
bad reputation.
• • » •
The new ocean steamer Corinthia seems
entered for a run of hard luck. At Hobart one
of the derrick booms slid as it was being unshipped
and smashed the bosun’s head against the mast.
A few days later an unfortunate wretch, who was
working his passage from London, got a clout
from the hoisting gear and was knocked into the
hold, falling 30ft. Result, a fractured skull and
one leg broken. The very next day a fireman
called Cocklin sailed slap through the engine-room
skylight and was hauled out again with a shattered
thigh. And finally, to top up, the following
morning a coal-lumper, while at work on the
vessel, tripped and split his head open. He soon
died, leaving a widow and family. The crev/’s
nerve is failing owing to this succesi'on of
accidents.
• • ■ •
Nicholson-st., Melbourne, the broad, well-
lighted, extremely respectable thoroughfare which
runs past the front door of the Exhibition Building,
has a unique record for sensational crime. There
it was, four years back, that two bombs were ex-
ploded at the front doors of private houses. Only
20 yards from the scene of one of these outrages a
wild-eyed Barrier boy made a sensational attempt
to shoot the variety actress of his heart, and then
to suicide; and, more recently, in this street,
arsonist Macnainara burned to death his infant
son. On the other Saturday night the last sensa-
tion occurred, when the residence of a swell
Chinese doctor, nearly opposite the main entrance
to the Exhibition, was raided by two yellow
desperadoes, who obtained admission to the house
on the ground that they had been assaulted by
larrikins, produced revolvers and bowie-knives,
and were about to loot the house, when the
doctor’s assistants came in and made them decamp.

Bishop Frodsham, while deploring the
absence of religious enterprise and the lack of
missionary work in N.Q., said, in addition, that
what struck him most in the North was the
“ absence of sectarian bitterness.” If the worthy
Bishop will only go South to districts where the
churches are strong, where the paganism he
deplores is less evident and where orthodoxy is
flourishing and rampant, he will find plenty of
sectarian bitterness. The broad tolerance on
religious matters which is a prominent character-
istic of N.Q. will not be so observable in 20 years
time when the sky-pilots have multiplied ana the
ohurches are nearly as numerous as the grog-shops.

and Troubles.
Brisbane, with a population (including
suburbs) of nearly 120,000, cannot get even a
tolerable fish supply, though Southport, where
the waters literally teem with fish, is but a few
miles away. On the other hand, such towns as
Rockhampton and Mt. Morgan enjoy a splendid
supply, simply because the Lake’s Creek Meat
Preserving Co. has added the fish business to the
meat business, and, by virtue of cold-storage
facilities, is able to make the thing a huge
success, and purvey for the neighboring towns.

“Cara j How excited the Australian
woman gets over colored celebrities. Ranji. caused
an epidemic of hysteria, a few years ago, and now
IVXajor Taylor has occasioned another. It was
amusing sickening to watch the exhibition
many women made of themselves at the late
cycling carnival. While our own riders fought
out desperate finishes, women were deep in study-
ing each other s hats, but when Taylor appeared,
how great the change ! Some women jumped on
seats and fairly shrieked, and one (a type of
many) stood up and called out endearing epithets
to the darkey. A fashionably-dressed girl in front
of me, one night, with a goggle-eyed youth, com-
pletely lost her head when the black made one of
his jumping sprints, and throwing her arms round
her boy s . neck, she shrieked with joy, ending
up by breaking her fan on his Panama. It might
have been girlish abandon, but it was dis-
gusting all the same !

Editor Bulletin. —Below is a copy of cir-
cular recently issued to N.S.W. teachers. I
understood that all this question of religion had
been dropped, and. moreover, that civil servants
had the option of stating their religious beliefs or
not. Following does not look like it.—Peda-
gogue.
Department of Public Instruction,
Sydney, Ist January, 1903.
Memorandum to the Teacher,
Public School.
In order to enable the Premier and Chief Secretary to
lay upon the Table of the House certain information pro-
mised, in reply to a question asked by Mr. Quinn, in the
Legislative Assembly, I have the honor to request you to
be good enough to state the name of the religious de-
nomination to which you belong, and, if you have
teachers under your supervision, to supply similar infor-
mation in regard to each of them on Schedule overleaf.
The information should be supplied by return post.
J. C. Maynard,
Under-Secretary.
Visitors to Sydney are warned of a gang
of “ring-finders” working at or near to Redfern
railway-station. You alight from the train look-
ing fairly innocent, when a stranger apparently
picks up from under your very feet a gold ring set
with gems. He calls your attention to his luck,
and, after you’ve admired the jewellery, offers it
to you for £l, or 10s., or 5s. —as much, in short,
as he thinks lie will get. If you purchase, you
have a Brummagem article, worth 3d., and he
sallies off to “find” another ring and another
“mug.”

A fine, with the alternative of impiison-
ment, was imposed in a Sydney court, the otht r
day, for larceny-by-finding. A sixpenny-bit caused
the trouble, but so loose are ideas on the subject
of picking tilings up in the street that a lesson on
the matter in State school-books would not be
amiss.

Chamberlain, appealing to the wealthy
Johannesburgers, mostly Hebrews, to shoulder
some of the cost of the war, suggested
(sardonically, probably) that they would not be
more careful of their purses than they had been of
their lives. It may not be generally known that
the Johannesburg Jews fought most valiantly in
the war. Never will the exploits of the famous
Ten Tribes Brigade be forgotten. Time after
time they charged the foe (100 per cent.), until the
mere sight of their banner (three golden balls on a
silver field) was enough to put to flight the largest
commando of burghers. One drawback only they
had, and it was fatal. The diamonds they wore
made them so conspicuous that they could not
conceal their movements. The last of them was
killed in ambush just before the struggle closed.
It was not till after that, that the War Office, with
the fatuity which has ever distinguished it, issued
an order that the members of the corps were to have
their jewels painted kharki—but the heroes were
then dead to the very last man.

Sydney Evangelical Council has petitioned
the Governor-General to appoint a Royal Com-
mission to inquire into the charges made by Car-
dinal Moran in connection with the recent Samoan
war. An impartial investigation of the 3ort
would probably prove entirely unsatisfactory to
both gangs of sectarian squabblers, and would be
therefore welcomed by the general public. But
the Commision is hardly likely to issue. A surer
way, if the E.C. is really in earnest about an
inquiry, would be an action at law by one of the
individuals who regards himself as slandered.

At Carlton (Melb.), one Conroy, still a
youngish man, was recently sentenced for the
hundredth time for various offences, Nothing
like continual justice for reforming a man. See
how it has reformed Conroy.
* * m •
Once a man comes under the control of
the “System” there are several new offences,
quite unknown “outside,” for which he may be
punished. A couple of Pentridge prisoners were
lately given four days’ solitary by a visiting
magistrate for “quarrelling,” while for declining
to say how they got certain marks on their faces—
apparently the results of the quarrel—each got an
extra seven days. Which was rubbing it in with
a vengeance!

Safe to predict that the next winner of
Melb. National Gallery students’ scholarship will
be a Jew, like the Altsons (Abey and Meyer). In
fact, the strongest specimen of painting from the
nude in the recent exhibition of students’ work
was Cohen’s figure of a reclining woman, boldly
drawn (back view) and admirably flesh tinted.
Cohen is a diminutive fellow, a mere ‘ ‘ bit of a
boy.” The present generation of art students in
Melbourne ought to have talent to compensate for
their lack of physique.

“P.X.”: Melba “went crabbing” in
Adelaide, or as near Adelaide as the crabs are ;
but she went in a boat, and that is not the way to
do it:—
The Port River, which is really an arm of the Gulf,
slops over a vast expanse of flat mangrove beach ; the
tide runs in for two or three miles. The idea is to wade
out as far as you can among the derelict bottles and jam-
tins (it may be a couple of miles at least), armed with a
scoop-net on a 6ft. pole. Sometimes it is also necessary
to make a tub fast to your belt and tow it after you, and
sometimes it isn’t. The tub is to hold the crabs that are
caught. The big blue fellow doesn’t wait for the net, and
the chase is often exciting ; the tub helps the crab, and
the artful dodger really doesn’t want much helping.
When beaten he has a knack of turning, and his jaws are
too powerful to be trifled with ; baggy pants—the baggiest
in the lumber-room—are therefore recommended. These
and boots that don’t let in the shells, which are sharper
than needles —boots that do so are Perdition. Luckily,
perhaps, the beach isn’t too easily got at from the city,
and so the unique sport is preserved.
Melbourne has produced an alleged lady
burglar, and if all of her species are equally
inefficient the sex had better give up burgling and
go on with its sweeping. Her method when found
out (she worked by day and appeared to be regu-
larly found out) was to laugh a ringing laugh, and
say that she was pretending to be a housebreaker
for the wild humor of the thing, and now she
wanted to be led at once to her dear friend Mrs
Jones so that she might kiss the new baby
instantly. Being assured that it wasn't Mrs
Jones s house, she would blush all over with shame
at her mistake and apologise abjectly. She was a
dark-haired siren of only 28, so she got off regu-
larly for a while, where a woman of 56 with an
apron made of bagging would have fallen in at the
first time of asking. But in the end an inhuman
householder refused to accept the blush as suffi-
cient explanation, and now the law is going to
decide, if possible, whether she is a real burglar
or a, practical jokist, ora crank with a mania for
finding Mrs. Jones’s baby.

It was not a good testimonial to the value
of “expert eyidence ” that was afforded by the
recent Sydney “Lost Diamond” case. A lost
diamond haying been entrusted to the police
two men claimed it as theirs :
.. H y? on Ridley, 28 years in the jewellery trade, “ knew
the diamond before the court. To the best of his belief
It was the property of Milverton, and belonged to the
ring worn by him. It fitted the setting exactly. The
setting in Lemon’s ring was very similar to Milverton’s,
and both were so near that the stone would fit either.”
Nathan Owles, jeweller, said “ Milverton’s was not the
correct setting for the stone. The diamond fitted the
setting of Lemon’s ring exactly.”
Evan Jones, jeweller, said it was “evident that the
diamond had been in Milverton’s ring before and belonged
i not fit the setting of Lemon’s ring so well
as that of Milverton’s. The setting of Lemon’s rin>' wai
entirely different.” ”
Celestin Paillis, diamond-mounter, deposed that
after trying the stone in both settings, he unhesitatingl!
stated that it only fitted Lemon’s.”
Joseph Wainribe, working jeweller, “had purchased
the diamond in Melbourne not quite 12 months ago, and
had set it for Lemon.” He had weighed it, and named
its weight exactly.
Mr. Lemon got the stone, on the weight of evi-
dence. If it had been a criminal case, with only
the Crown collecting “expert evidence,” probably
there would have been a unanimous chorus of
experts to send some poor devil to the gaol or the
scaffold.

LOVE IN FETTERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
t held Love to me with reins of hair—
One golden hair and a cobweb fine ;
And his face was ruddy and debonnai’r,
Nor strained he ever the dainty line •
But my heart, poor heart! with desire atoss,
Fluttered, grew cold, with fear of loss.
I bound Love to me with cords of silk—
Two silken cords of delicate hue,
Studded with opals pale as milk,
Clamped and fastened with sapphires blue :
But Love turned restless and tried to stray,
And I would keep him for ever and aye.
I chained Love to me with ropes of gold,
Fettered his arms and his errant feet
His eyes I bandaged with gauzy fold
That none but mine his eyes might meet.
He struggled a moment, then bowed his head
The chains fell, useless—poor Love w’as dead.
JILL.
A MATINEE TRAGEDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas at a matinee,
When Nellie Stewart “panto” played ,
There sat in front of me
A lady who a hat displayed
Built up with flowers and feathers hijh,
So that no stage could I espy.
In front of her there sat
Another lady, neat and trim,
Who wore a “ sailor ” hat,
Low-crowned and with a narrow brim:
Of course you will infer that such
Would not obstruct the view so much ,
The overture was o'er ,
And as the curtain upward went
The lady with the store
Of flowers and feathers forward leant
To where the other lady sat,
And asked her to remove her hat.
The other, thus besought,
Removed her hat to please this dame :
1 naturally thought
That Number One would do the same—
But she, with coolness most sublime,
KEPT HER HAT ON ALL THE TIME.
SYDNEY.
