A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 3, 1903.
My Dear Arini—
The Sydney Ladies’ Swimming Club (founder
had to say Ladies’, I suppose, instead of Women’s,
as the State Governoress is at its head) is the
bantling of a little woman enthusiast, Mrs. Loftus.
She use to be a thin, white, small person, always
ailing, when a doctor told her, in effect, to go to
the fish and consider its ways. And her husband,
who could swim as one finned and gilled and
tailed, taught her to swim, so that within 18
months she could beat him —at swimming, of
course. And now that she is rosy, and sturdy as
a little whale, she is an enthusiast for swimming
for women. S.L.S.C. was only formed in Novem-
ber ; the earlier members, who didn’t know
enough to keep them from drowning, are now
racing each other. There are some 30 members
in the club, which is very altruistic, for neither
founder nor executive makes anything out of it.
Mrs. Loftus and the committee teach all the
flounderers to swim, and save their own and
other people’s lives in several different ways.
On three days a week you may see the club,
dressed in two yards of navy-blue twill with a
white yoke and a shield with the letters 5.L.9.C.,
at Coogee or Rushcutter Baths. By the way,
only three out of every 100 women who go down
to the sea in bathing-clothes are able to swim a
stroke. Mrs. Bland Holt (who often brings the
baby used in the melodrama) and some women of
the Holt co. may be seen splashfully disporting
themselves in Coogee Baths. No men are per-
mitted to join S.L.S.C. Mrs. Loftus looks coldly
on mixed bathing, and no professional women
swimmers are welcome. Probably the club will
hold a carnival in March, when, as a cynic
suggested, it ought to invite the Blind Asylum
band to play its swimming music. Miss Nellie
King is non- sec. mermaid; Mayoress Hughes,
Mrs. G. H. Reid and Miss Rose Scott are also
members.
All the women now doing the Block in Sydney
have the trail of the cheap-sale over them.
Especially do their hats show it. And why will
any woman with less than a perfect complexion
and of more than 20 years wear those white muslin
frilled hats ? “It is impossible to get a becoming
fashionable hat,” writes an acquaintance, from
London; “they are all tambourines, or plates
and bell-ropes.” Miss Harrie Ireland, in a Bland
Holt role, wears a hat which, I heard a woman
say, shows “real character.” It is one of those
light, soft, wide-brimmed sunburnt straws, and
around the crown is tied a very narrow piece of
orange velvet—only that and nothing more ; but
under the brim, over a fascinating left ear, are
six black ostrich tips and a little tuft of more
orange velvet.
Primate Saumarez Smith, unlike most men
with a quiverful, is careless of the matrimonial
prospects of his daughters. “He does not give
the girls a chance,” say sympathetic people.
Lieut.-Gov. Madden, Lord Hampden, and other
notabilities generally had eligibles on their staffs.
The Primate, having four daughters in Sydney
and two in London, made his only son his chap-
lain ! This young gentleman is called “ Straw-
berry ” by his ex-companions of Sydney Grammar
School, and “ that sweet child ” by young-old
matrons.
Sydney has not overwhelmed the French cruiser
Protet with hospitality. She has been berthed at
Neutral Bay, where nothing is to be seen, and the
shore can only be reached with some trouble. It’s
like giving a distinguished visitor aback bedroom,
instead of the hostess turning out of the front
room in her honor. The officers tell in their im-
perfect English of the hospitality enjoyed at
Tahiti and Noumea. Save for some formal calls
paid by officials and some private entertainments
given by French residents, the presence of the
foreigners in Sydney has been practically ignored.
And the foreigners are glad. One day they had
a reception, and a Sydney “lydy ” with a cake in
one hand and a cup of tea in the other, stood over
against the bareheaded Commander and shouted
to another lydy : “ Say, Mollie, wonder if the top
of his ’air was shot off ? ”
That fine old Demosthenes who is to be mar-
ried early this month to a rich widow (reported to
have the wealth of an Attalus or of a Tyson), was
the means of introducing the lady’s daughter to a
suitor, an Englishman “of good family.” Within
a fortnight, the latter was received into the
Church of his bride, and the marriage of the
.young couple precedes that of the parent and the
parent-in-law by the space of a week or two.
Edward Lloyd, the English tenor, will be sup-
ported in his Australian season by Peterkin (the
basso), Emily Foxcroft (contralto), and Jean
Newman (soprano).
Ada Crossley, flushed and delighted with her
American triumph, will probably come to Aus-
tralia fairly early this year. Negotiations are
now under cable.
Talking of musicians; Maud MacCarthy has
appeared with that august body, the Phil-
harmonic, in New York, and the critics were not
much kinder to her than to Dani. Maud
played in N.Y. in her childish days. Max
Schluter has returned to Denmark and further
study. Franz Wilczek, after a conquering tour
of the Pacific coast, is back in N.Y., where it has
been 28deg. below zero. He may revisit Austra-
lia during the Columbian summer.
Miss Ethel Mollison, J. C. Williamson’s leading
lady for.“ Are You a Mason?” is described as
much prettier than her portraits. She is above
middje height and a blonde.
The orchid (its botanical name is like a mouth-
ful of ashes) that is shown this week in Searle’s
window was grown at Mr. Hugh Hixson’s place at
Summer Hill. Parent plant has been in his
orchid house for seven years, and this is the shy
thing’s first blossoming in Australia. It has a
fell look. Someone told me that it came from the
Malay archipelago. The stem is quite 7ft. long,
and from a foot or so above the base to the top of
the stem depend blossoms, perhaps to the number
of 250, of a dull yellow, spotted with brown. The
tawny ground color hardly predominates over the
brown blotches. The plant has several other
stems (or telegraph poles) of flowers.
A Sydney citizen recently had a new baby
added to his household. Infant is so like her
father that the likeness was apparent to the other
child, aged seven. “ Where did you get the new
little girl ? ’’ she asked of the doctor. “ I bought
it,” he replied; “at the greengrocer’s.” “How
curious,” said the child, “ that they should have
one so like father 1 ”
Brisbane is going to have a regular “India
relish ” in the way of a divorce case. Drawing
room conversations to be repeated are said to be
smarter than the “ Dolly Dialogues ” —and more
compromising. Petitioner married his moneyed
little brunette some seven or eight years ago.
Emily Soldene, writing from London, bemoans
the price of fish and oysters. “A lovely turbot,
with a lovely fat, white front ” (imagine Emily’s
eyes—two glowing notes of admiration!) “two
guineas.” “A fine lobster—yes, ss. 6d. —and a
nice pair of soles, 7s. 6d.” There is, after all,
something in man’s gibe that woman learns to
write about eating long before she learns to eat—
eat, that is to say, in the sense of eating , not of
merely stoking herself.
In print the list of legacies left by the late
squatter Illiffe of Albury looks like a Tatt.’s
sweep. There are close upon 50 legatees, most of
them women or girl relatives, connections or
friends of the kindly, old, childless widower. Yet
his fortune was small—only £11,850. What a lot
of happiness a rich man can throw round by
“remembering” people! Sydney has not for-
g itten the will of the late J. T. Neale.
A young American man among us is much sur-
prised and disgusted at the way Australian women
dress their children. “Whilst they are at the
very pretty wax-doll-seeming period, they are
dressed beautifully, like regular picture-children,
but, from the age of seven or eight onwards to the
time when they ‘ come out,’ they look like inmates
of charity institutions.” Which observation has
some truth. At a children’s party at Elizabeth
Bay I saw two little sisters, about three and
five respectively, dressed in French fashion. Kate
Greenaway infants present were quite over-
shadowed by the Frenchies, who were like 50-
guinea wax dolls at Chicago Exhibition. They
were low-necked, short-sleeved and socked. About
10 inches of highly goffered muslin and lace
underskirts stood out above the pink bare knees.
Their tiny knickers had rosettes of blue ribbon.
They wore short silk mits and bangles, and close
chiffon hoods with strings and sprays of French
flowers.
Sydney is full of annual meetings. I note that
the Dental Hospital, which has more than justi-
fied its existence by the relief it has given to the
tooth-aching poor, treated 935 cases for the year,
with an average daily attendance of 25.
Menie Muriel Dowie’s (Mrs. Norman’s) divorce
is a splendid advt. for her books. In all Sydney
public libraries you will hear inquiries : “Give me
something by Miss Dowie.” Personally, I was
not greatly surprised at the rending of the bonds,
for I had read her “Gallia, the Real New
Woman.” Of course, she is best known by her
book “A Girl in the Karpathians.” Henry
Norman, the petitioner, author, traveller, and
M.P., ought to have been brainy enough for any
advanced woman. Mr. Fitzgerald, the co-re., is
also something of an author, a great traveller and
climber.
The effrontery of the ping-ponger! A Sydney
lady without a handle to her name has got a letter
from a German town which runs so:—
Frau Brown. Hochwohlgeboren (right honorable). Dear
and most respected madam.—You will no doubt be a little
astonished at the receipt of this letter, and more so, per-
haps, since it comes from one who is a total stranger to
you, but to whom you were well known when he visited
in comradeship with your son and nephew. Dear
lady, I will now approach the subject on which I intend
to broach you. We are several young English people
here, including two from Australia, and we have all joined
together to form a ping-pong club, but at our first meet-
ing we found our funds, inadequate to furnish a table,
chairs, a room, &c., and we have decided to write to our
friends and relatives for their contributions, and though
I have never had the honor to make your acquaintance, I
know your generosity well, and therefore appeal to you
in this letter for any donation you would be happv to
give. In hopeful anticipation of a speedy answe'r, I
remain, .
A women’s club in Sydney was much disturbed
by a tailor, who occupied the floor above, using
his goose too vigorously, and he was told if his
thump, thump did not stop, “steps would be
taken to have him removed.” A meeting of more
than usual interest one day caused a great babel
of tongues below the tailor, who promptly sent
word down that their cackle interfered with his
goose, “and he must take steps,” &c.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Miss ‘Stonington’ Clarke was sandwiched in
witn Melba’s family party at the Beaumont
benefit on Saturday night. Society for this
occasion had changed its auarters—deserting the
balconies for the body of the hall. A small
sprinkling of Toorak stars lay outside and beyond
the Mitchell clan.
“At the Armes Beaumont benefit the most
brightly dressed lady was Mrs. Alfred Taylor,
in a fiercely vivid rose-pink silk frock.
Femininity found 105‘ in the shade a
food excuse for leaving off as much as possible.
>raughts were created to the waist, shoul-
ders bereft even of an apology band, thatches
freed of upholstered frames and millinery odds
and ends. A city auctioneer’s wife made the
biggest effort to keep cool by wearing nothing
whatever above the big bust line.
“ Melba did not regulate her frocking to a high
temperature. She was severely encased in a
topaz-shaded silk of a flower pattern, with sudden
gushes of roseate tulle at the elbows and the usual
flying pennants of the same roseate hue from the
waist. At the conclusion of each number (she
gave of her bounty to the Beaumont bill) a
glass of iced water was handed her at the
foot of the platform steps. A new arrival
from 'Ome expressed his surprise at the Voice
Wonder’s air of familiarity, and the general devil-
may-careness of her demeanor on our boards. In
London, this tourist declares, Melba’s way of
coming on and leaving the platform is a slow,
dignified march, and her pose one of Cold and
Calm Rigidity. In gay Paris, after a grand opera
scene, she may sometimes show graceful abandon,
but she is ordinarily much the majestic prinia
donna —unflurried, self-sufficient. As for plumping
down on the piano stqol, pitching her ’kerchief on
the lid, and striking off a gay symphony for her-
self—the traveller is Aghast. It has upset all his
notions.
“Miss Louie Mueller, one of the few handsome
platformers the Marshall-Hall school has yet
turned out, was, next to Melba, the most highly-
chiffoned contributor to the Beaumont programme.
She secured a ‘Gibson’ effect in foamy cream.
The benefit-taker was fortunate in having a gifted
little song-bird, a tender little twitterer, in Miss
Doris Carter to demonstrate the quality of his art
of instruction. . The Maoriland soprano, Miss
Amy Murphy, by her confidence showed that she
knew something about debuts.
“They did honor to Melba at the Victoria Park
race meeting in the Holy City, by allowing her
carriage to crunch the verdant lawn, that she
might be let down at the V.R. enclosure of the
stand. They have never done as much for a Gov.-
General, or even for Royalty.
“ The only member of Melba’s troupe who took
the fancy of the Westerners was little Signorina
Sassoli. The minute the stolid little Italian ap-
peared at her melodious harp, a delighted shout
would shake the tin halls of Forrest Land. The
rest of Melba’s ‘ support ’ fell flat.
“ Melba has a way of sweeping up her vegetable
trophies that suggests a housewife snapping up
the towels for the weekly tub. There is impatient
haste and a practical turn in the act. Also, she
recalls the demeanor of an autocrat yielding a
point with a ‘ Now-nO-fuss—That-will-do ’ gesture
as she comes on to give an encore. When she, on
the other hand, gives a negative shake to her
head, there is an emphatic doggedness in ti e
denial. ‘ I shall not sing, ’ she seems to say, with
a foot-stamp, ‘because I shall not. So there ! ’
“‘Music’ is not inscribed on the plain post-
cards that summon one to a Melba ‘ At Home ’ on
Friday (6th). As much of society as hides its
midsummer term behind the drawn blinds at
Toorak is invited, and wonders what’s to be the
afternoon bill-of-fare. Up to this, covers for
twelve have been the largest order for ‘ My ora ’
hospitality.
“Wild whispers are abroad as to the number of
Philistines, and others, a Samsoness of the song-
world has slain.
“Judge Madden was delighted to welcome his
new grandson, Mrs. Harry Osborne’s second baby
and second son. Mrs. Vallange’s recent baby is
also a boy, so the Waxcellency has three grandsons
to one granddaughter. To use his own words, he
himself ‘ never had but one boy/ hence his glee
when his first grandson, little ‘Pat. John Osborne,’
came along. The C.J.’s brother, Mr. Frank
Madden, has also only one son to counter-balance
several daughters. This young man, evidently,
does not inherit the ‘dirty left’ of the elder
Maddens. According to newspaper accounts, he
came downstairs t’other night in the Madden
mansion at Kew(Melb.)to look for a suspected
burglar. The intruder hit him in the mouth and
knocked out a front tooth. Young Madden was
dazed for some time, and small blame to him, but
it seemed over-placid for him to go straight to bed
when consciousness returned and stay there till
ten o’clock next morning. Then he rose and in-
formed the caretakers, who were on duty during
the absence of most of the family.
“At the Turkish baths in Melbourne (the city
has only one such establishment nowadays ; 10
years ago it had three) a large proportion of the
bathers on ‘ladies’ days’ are barmaids. The
average barmaid is a cleaner person than the
average woman of domestic life, anyhow. Even
the high-toned Toorakienne, with lots of time to
spare for face-massage and manicuring, rarely
troubles to purify herself by means of ‘ Nature’s
reviving sweats.’ A female attendant at one of
the Turkish baths being asked whether the
smartest girls were regular customers, said— ‘ Bless
you ! No. They only come here once in their
lives—the day before they get married! If they
don’t get married they never patronise as at all ’
One pictures the cynical T.B. lady eyeing the
unwonted spinster who comes up to the hot rooms
for her bridal ‘clean,’ and murmuring to herself—
‘ I wonder whether she’s making a good match.’
“ ‘Zaza ’ is the sort of play that sets a painted
man-huntress on a pedestal to be worshipped
Said man-huntress, to do her justice, hunts for
love, not for pay. Having said that, one has
summed up all her claims to heroineship Taking
a feverish fancy to a comely unknown, she com
mandeers him to her dressing-room, saturates him
with scent, smites him (literally) with her legs
and swoons finally—paint, scent, legs and all—
into his half-reluctant arms. The fair, human
Etna institutes no inquiries into her captive’s
domestic connections, while he, for his part
makes no promises for the future. Yet, at the end
of six months, when it is discovered that Zaza’s
prize still cherishes a sneaking regard for his
unsuspecting wife and child, the audience ig
cheated into letting its tears down-fall on behalf
of the painted lady.
“Nellie Stewart’s ‘Zaza’ clothes are blowsy
and bold, compared with those worn by ‘Sweet
Nell ’ and the ‘ Country Mouse.’ Miss Stewart,
artist that she is, bows to the fact that the
Parisian music-hall siren, with her name yet to
make, would favor toilette-tactics, ‘ loud ’ rather
than chic, boisterous rather than becoming. Most
of her costumes are scarlet. The . red-cloth
walking-skirt, worn in the love-in-the-cottage
scene, is appallingly frank in its anatomical reve-
lations. When wearer is seated the outline sug-
gests a ‘ nude ’ done in cochineal.
“ Parents seemingly make quite a point of
taking children to plays of the ‘ Zaza ’ type. They
intend the treat to act as a warning, no doubt.
The youngsters are just as likely to regard it as
an example. Scribe heard two school-girls in the
train discussing the grisette’s ‘mashing’ tech-
nique, with frank enthusiasm. Said the one who
had seen the play to her less fortunate chum:
‘ First of all she drenched herself with scent-
heliotrope it was. Then she half-closed her eyes
and gazed in darling Beatty’s face, right under his
eyelids, like this. Then she rocked backwards
and forwards in her chair and kept touching him
accidentally with her foot. Her frock was dis-
gracefully short, you know, and talk about a low
bodice !'
“If there is in ‘Zaza’ any lesson likely to
benefit a ‘ respectable young person ’ it must be
that good housekeeping tells in the long run.
Bernard’s lawful wife, unaware even of the ex-
istence of her rival, nevertheless unknowingly
fights that rival, when she keeps her home as
‘fresh as a rose.’ Zaza, squalid slattern, dusts the
breakfast cups with her petticoat and throws the
‘ combings ’ from her toilette-brush over the
balcony into the garden. Her lover laughs good-
humoredly when she serves him with underdone
eggs and burnt beefsteak on a soiled tablecloth,
but when he’s not in a picnic mood and wants a
dainty meal and a clean bed, he goes elsewhere.
Some people imagine men don’t object to a dash
of humorous dirt. In reality, they’re far cleaner
than women. Have you ever got a man to help
wash-up ? If so, you know that boiling water and
soap failed to satisfy his ideal of cleanliness.
Heaps of elbow-grease went to the rim of ever)'
individual cup, because ‘that’s the part they’ve
been drinking out of, you know ! ’
“The Mrs. Macdonald whose wing is stretched
to shelter the two Castles sisters to Europe is
reputedly a very shrewd dame. Her late husband
was a general and a diplomat, and his widow has
seen service in Austrian Court circles. She sports
a superb medallion—a gift from a crowned head.
Generally suspected that she was the ‘ One Who
Has Known Her’ writer of that Saxon Crown
Princess vindication article—which, by the way,
wasn’t a particularly shrewd production.
“Mrs. Edward Miller has returned from
abroad, after an absence of two years. She spent
the time watching over the shadow’s of her sons at
the academic desk. The Edward Millers afforded
another example of the weak politician and the
strenuous she-partner. I can recall a feeble effort
on the part of the Hon. Edward to denounce
Woman’s Suffrage in the House. He spluttered
on stupidly, the while his spouse sat in the
visitors’ gallery show’ing manifest impatience.
She could have said the whole thing herself in
two or three commanding sentences.
“Earl Carrington heads the list of Australian
ex-Govemors (so home-trotters advise) who are
hospitable to Australians in the Big Smoke. The
yawnful earl goes to some trouble to be kind.
Doesn’t content himself with merely supplying a
sheaf of introductions, but spreads a feast, and
takes the chair at his own board. The Marquis
of Linlithgow’s weak health has saved him from
more than postal courtesies to inquiring globe-
trotters. As for Lord Brassey, the Faller-off, he
sails away in his Sunbeam at frequent intervals,
and is not easily buttonholed. Earl Beauchamp
stands sponsor for Art and Letters generally.
“ As an instance of Melbourne climatic variety.
Last Wednesday week a bride was unable to step
out from the church into a deluge of rain in her
flimsy white satin shoes, and was carried by the
blushing bridegroom to the carriage—adozen yards
away. A week later another Melbourne bride
was visibly melting. Her tulle veil had lost all
its starch and hung in limp folds over the moist
face of the newly-sacrificed.
“ The honeymoon has become an energetic
jaunt. A pair went off a month or two ago for a
bicycle tour through rural Victoria. A fortnight
back another newly-hitched couple started on an
equestrian expedition. Instead of providing
themselves with quiet old nags, trained to pull-up
at kissing-posts, they selected two thoroughbreds
that promise to take all before them through the
Gippsland forest.
“ The latest domestic fracas arose through the
wicked agency of the telephone. Mrs. Blank was
ringing up her dear old hubby to bring home an
extra pound of pork mysteries, or something,
when, in the interim of waiting, with her wifely
ear cleaving fast to the receiver, she found herself
listening to an animated conversation between
the dear fellow and a barmaid. She has gone
straight away to her relatives.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Veteran £ £ Smith has just built a
country residence at Berwick, Vic., where he
holds open house, mainly for ’theatricals.
Characteristically enough he has named the villa
“L. L. Vale.”
John Norton has been gipsying through
Tasmania, and, at latest, was in Hobart. John’s
resemblance to the brief, round Bishop of Tas-
mania has caused consternation among the short-
sighted ladies of the bishop’s flock.
Single-taxer Hirsch seized his chance to
air his pet fad during the adjourned Budget
debate in Vic. Assembly; he spoke for four solid
hours to a thin House. The deliverance occupies
54 columns in Hansard, and Max now considers
his mission in life within sight of fulfilment.
Ragman Reid, alleged Victorian senator, is
unquestionably the most flamboyant speaker in
Southern public life. His words are wild and
whirling, and, though they have little meaning
the average public cares more for sound and fury
and, getting it from Mr. Reid, is satisfied.
Tit Bits (London) is in possession of some
interesting and exclusive information re the
Owe’Sullivan :
Born, in Tasmania, the a ? e of ten he was installed as
printer s devil in the office of the Hobart Mercury To-
day he owns both morning, evening, and weekly journals.
Charles Gavan Duffy, in his first speech in
Victoria, declared he had promoted the ’4B insur-
rection in Ireland and that he neither repented it
nor would apologise for it. “I am still a rebel
to the backbone and spinal marrow,” he cried.
He wasn’t, tried for treason, but sent to Parlia-
ment. Lynch would have fared better had he
been bolder.
The lately-deceased (in England) Lieut.-
Col. St. John, who was for several years one of
the staff of Sir Hercules Robinson when the latter
was N.S.W. Governor, was twice married to the
same daughter of Sir Hercules. The second
ceremony was necessary because the lady had been
divorced by her husband in the interval, the co-
respondent being an army captain.
Ex-editor Haddon, of the Argus, will be
64 to-morrow (Sunday); General Downes, late
Victorian Military Commandant, will be 69 on
Tuesday, on which day the birthday of Attorney-
General Wise also falls; Sunbeam Brassey will
be 67 on Wednesday; J. J. Duncan, leader of
S.A. Council Opposition, 58 on Thursday ; and
squatter A. B. Murray, the oldest of S.A. pas-
toralists, who sat in the first Parliament in 1857,
will be 87 on Saturday, Dec. 14.

A correspondent from Melbourne :
The “ bosses’ ” train had just arrived (9.45 a.m.) at
Flinders-street from Brighton. As the passengers—mer-
chants, lawyers, consequential managing clerks —rushed
along they ignored the struggles of a small boy with a
big parcel, who was vainly attempting to get a start on
his bike from the kerb, till a portly, well-groomed per-
sonage kindly held the machine whilst the youngster
mounted and got away. It was his Waxellency, the
Lieut.-Governor of Victoria.
They tell of a “fairy godfather” to Aus-
tralians in England that he is getting a little bit
weary of the r6le. One Australian writer’s
clothing-emporium sent to him a little bill for the
habiliments of literary genius. Another great
writer, calling at the castle, or whatever it is, in
a full and Bohemian moorl, and finding that the
fairy godfather was not at home, borrowed half-a-
crown from the butler !
Salary offered by tho Commonwealth for
the New Guinea judgeship (£lOOO a year and no
pension rights) is not nearly enough to attract a
good man. The post is, financially, only equal to a
3>500-a-year billet in Sydney, taking into aocount
the climate, and so on. Said that Tom Slattery,
M.L.C., has his eye on the thing for his son-in-
law, barrister Lyons.
W. J. Lyne’s desire to picnic to North
Queensland and New Guinea is characteristic of
the plodding, stick-at-it manner that, despite a
host of defects, keeps him still about the front.
Nearly 40 years ago he was starved out of those
tropic parts. To get away with his life he had to
struggle almost alone over a thousand miles of
inhospitable country. Now, just on 60, it is the wish
of his life to go there again, and he will go, too,
in all probability.
Rev. Dill Macky, at Bathurst, the other
Sunday, refused to let fly at the Pope on the Lord’s
Day :
He hoped that none of those present expected him to
denounce the errors of Popery or to put before them the
claims of the mission on account of which he was touring
the country. He never did so on the Sabbath Day. He
took advantage of the opportunity that was given to
occupy the pulpit and preach the simple Gospel.
Which seems to suggest that Dr. Dill has in
his heart some doubt as to the holiness of his
sec tarian mission.

Premier Seddon, as a Jingo, takes himself
very seriously indeed. A Xmas card circulated
by the ex-radical is inscribed:
Peace in South Africa, Qoronation of King Edward,
Christmas greetings, and best wishes for a Happy New
Year with the compliments of the Prime Minister of
Maonland. A year of industry, crowned with Peace and
Prosperity.
The card is “adorned” with portraits of the

keeper.
The Laugh of ex-Premier Peacock is now
seldom heard in Vic. Assembly. That forced and
unpleasant guffaw at last brought on a throat-
trouble, and nowadays Peacock merely smiles in
a tired way on special occasions. But a weak and
fraudulent imitation of the Laugh is frequently
heard, and listeners are much disgusted when
they find that its perpetrator is only produce-
merchant Keast, who divides the honor with
Albert Harris of being the amiable butt of the
Assembly. Peacock, though superficially healthy-
looking again, lias of late fattened out in a way
that seems to betoken trouble.
Premier Seddon, who is now with great
reluctance accepting big cash testimonials from
the M.L. public, is regarded as one of the
closest * of men, though reputedly worth some
tens of thousands. King Dick is not averse to
being entertained, but of entertainments he gives
none. He travels free per rail and steamer, and
so does his family, and the country supplies him
with a free house, fuel, and lighting. His Majesty’s
acceptance, now, of big money gifts from his sub-
jects is rather curious in view of his request in
London to Reuter’s agency to state that—“ There
was a movement in the colony for presenting, the
Premier with a sum of money, but at his request
it was not proceeded with.”
Commutation of Arthur Lynch’s sentence
from death to penal servitude for life leaves room
for a further concession later on. But Lynch is
of the unhappy, bungling, timorous, Charles I.
type of man who get precious little sympathy
until he is dead. He hadn’t the courage of his
change of flags, and tried to shuffle out of his
responsibility for it, by which he offended Fenian
sympathisers without conciliating the British
Government. Palpably a futile fellow, drifting
into treason without malice in his mind, or even
any thought of the seriousness of the step, and
then taking refuge in weak falsehood.
Arthur Lynch, M.P., whose death-sentence
for treason has been commuted to life imprison-
ment, was born at Smythesdale, Ballarat, in 1861.
Old man Lynch, still on deck, also had a tussle
with Britain—-at Eureka stockade. Young Lynch
is M.A. and C.E. of Melb. University, and is by
now a thorough cosmopolitan, having dwelt in
Berlin, Paris and the U.S., besides taking casual
glances at Turkey, Morocco, Canada, Germany
and Russia. Went to Ashantee as war-correspon-
dent, thence to the Transvaal. Has published
selections of his poetry, and “Modern Authors,”
“A Koran,” “Religio Athletae” and other
books. Is a finished swordsman, and a brainy
but not too well-ballasted young man.

writes a Bulletin correspondent, “I noticed a
lock of grey hair, tied to a piece of cardboard,
inscribed, ‘Lock of Henry Parkes’ hair.’ This
barber, in answer to my inquiry, said, ‘Oh
yes, that’s off the old man’s head right enough ,
but I had a greater curio than that. After
shavin’ ’im once, I put the whole cutting
into the cage of my canaries, which were buildin’
at the time, and they made a nest of it, and
hatched a brood in it. But some curiosity-hunter
shook the bloomin’ thing. Only yesterday,’ he
went on, ‘ I cut Bishop ’s hair ; he’s off to
the Old Country, and,’ showing me a lock of the
episcopal clip, ‘I kep’this because, as I says to
him, “ Maybe it’s the larst time you’ll ever ’ave
your ’air cut.” ’ Cheerful valediction ! ”

E. W. Christmas (a painter and a man—
not a festive season), who recently brought out the
collection of British pictures for Melb. “Federal
Exhibition,” states that John LongstafFs work
was in high repute in London when he so unwisely
returned to Australia some years ago. “I was
surprised,” says Mr. Christmas, “to find what a
name Longstaff had left behind him. Of course
he is *in the Swim ’ again now, but he was lucky
to get there, after having sacrificed his first
chance. The chanoe rarely comes to an artist the
second time.”
Generally understood in Melb. press
circles that F. W. Ward, who leaves the Argus
to renew his editorship of Sydney D.T., will be
succeeded as political leader-writer on the Argus
by “Dave” Maling, long identified with that
paper. Maling is one of the old school of Argus
men, a Tory to his finger tips, and regards any
statement in the leading columns of his journal as
holy gospel. Since Willoughby dropped writing
political notes as “Timotheus” and took the editor-
ship, Maling has followed gracefully in his foot-
steps as commentator, even sticking religiously
to the practice of having just three literary quota-
tions in each column. Willoughby was maliciously
said to write his three quotations first, and then
turn off .the surrounding paragraphs. Maling’s
prospective rise is not likely to make any great
difference in the Argus reporting staff, which was
getting rather overstocked with the “ literary,”
as distinct from the recording element. *
“ What may happen to a man in Australia,”
from Melb. Age :
The ups and downs of life were vividly illustrated in a
case which was called before the City Court yesterday.
A venerable looking old vagrant, rejoicing in the name of
William Carrington Smith Heale, rather astonished the
Bench by relating an outline of his past career. He said
he had been in Victoria since 1848 ; that for 18 years he
had been a manager of the London Chartered Bank,
portion of the time at Ballarat; that for five years he
had been in the service of the Bank of Australasia, and
that his father had been for 37 years connected with the
London Chartered Bank, also had acted as executor for a
large Collins-street estate. This aged financier, according
to the police, was found camped beside a fire on a piece
of land at the corner of Lonsdale and King-streets, and
had for years been carrying about pieces of firewood in a
bag, having no visible means of support. His defence
was that his wife worked in a restaurant where she earned
“good money"—good as far as it went—and that she
supplied him with meals. The chairman commented on
the painful position to which Heale had reduced himself,
and imposed a sentence of three months’ imprisonment.

“Mr. T. Horn has joined the board of
the Great Fingall.” It recalls one of the families
that, in a few years, have almost faded out of
Australian memory. There were three brothers,
”• , S., and T. 8., who for years were
moderately-successful 8. Australian and West
Darling squatters. In that way they got in early
at Broken Hill; W. A. was one of the provisional
directors of the B. H. Prop.; T. S. was the Ade-
laide broker, and could get off only a few hundred
shares. W. A. then went into Parliament, and
used to spend his nights building up smart things
to throw off on the spur of the moment next day.
It was he who guided Gov. Xintore into his suc-
cessful Broken Hill specs.; and, in return, Xintore
chaperoned the family in England. Apparently,
they still do a bit “in the city.”
George Brookman, M.L.C., the mining
magnate, who gave £15,000 to Adelaide School of
Mines, is understood to be much hurt because he
has not received even a C.M.G. in return. It has
been suggested that he should double the gift, and
then see what will happen, But he hesitates to
put more money down the shaft, lest it should
agmn prove a “ wild cat.” Samuel Wilson aiid
Walter Watson Hughes gave £20,000 to Mel-
bourne and Adelaide Universities respectively,
brought off the bauble they coveted, but
Brookman gift is evidently considered below
the mark. It was Brookman’s brother who
resigned the Mayoralty of Perth last year because
his predecessor (Alec. Forrest, Bigjohn’s brother)
was made a C.M.G. on the occasion of the Duke’s
visit, and not himself. Geo. Brookman learned
courtesy behind a grocer’s counter, and has
remained amiable despite his mining successes.
Ihe ex-Mayor of Perth, however, has not managed
to take such a firm hold on public esteem.
“ Galvo ” : Re Donald Murray’s tele-
graphic typewriter:—
Murray was a born genius, and only the few who knew
hovv the man persevered under great difficulties toper-
feet his idea can appreciate it. He began entirely with-
out electrical or mechanical knowledge, and it is most
unusual for a man to begin to acquire such knowledge at
the age Mr. Murray did and to succeed. He bought a
lathe and stuck to it until he could use it as well as any
mechanic. Then he studied theoretical electricity and
8” a fair grasp of that. Finding it necessary to have
Clans for his patent, he learned mechanical drawing and
ecame an extremely neat draftsman. Between times,
he studied for and became first 8.A., then M.A., at
Umversity. All this time he was empfoyed on
the S.M. Herald staff ; and, whatever her faults, th« old
lady of O’Connell-st. does not encourage idleness By
Murray s method press-messages can be sent from a
central point to any number of offices at the rate of a
couple of hundred words a minute, the matter being
automatically set into type at once. When Murray took
his invention to America it was at once grabbed at by
one of the big U.S. telegraph co’s., who installed him in
an office with a salary of 2000dol. a year and the free and
avT Use ot elr l' nes {or the purpose of experimenting.
About a year ago the Scientific American published an
Illustrated article highly commending Murray’s invention.

THREE STAR
Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— In the cate of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
T. P. Winter, Acting Administrator of
British Papua, tells of a curious form of mourning
he saw on Trobriand Islands. A man was seen
wearing on a string hung from his neck several
joints of a human backbone, also a human jaw-
bone with a fine set of teeth. A woman also wore
a similar necklet. Inquiry elicited that the man
was mourning for a wife, and the wife for a hus-
band, and that the emblems of sorrow formed
parts of the skeleton of the dear defunct. Tells,
further, that the people of the Trobriands are
very smart at learning arithmetic, their language
containing 9999 separate names of numerals.
That custom of mourning for your late wife by
wearing her teeth in your ear is a truly touching
one.

A correspondent from “ the Islands ” to
the Sydney L.T.:—
The natives of Vila, New Hebrides, had a very enjoy-
able Xmas Day. Converts belonging to all the tribes. m
the island were invited by the Presbyterian missionaries
to a somewhat sumptuous entertainment. The menu
comprised fine fat pigs roasted whole, varied with beef,
supplemented with vegetables, and ending with a dessert
of native fruits. A French correspondent affirms that
this is how the Protestant missionaries secure and retain
converts. The native fondness for “ fat pig ” is, he says,
irresistible.
On the other side, it is itffirmed that the.R.C.
padres keep a very respectable brand of gin for
their converts, and Presbyterian pig. is easily
countered with the spirituous consolations of the
other faith. A Yes-No ( i.e ., Pork-Gin) religious
man ought to do very well at the Islands.
Ten aborigines, who had been gaoled at
Port Augusta (S.A.), for stealing beef on a
Centralian station, were released on a recent
Tuesday. They were marched to a station on
the G.N. line, placed on board train and carried
to Oodnadatta, 600 miles north, and then let loose
to find their way home as best they could. Gaol
meant constant food and house-room, but they
will, in the future, like the wild beasts, have to
“seek their meat from God.” If they take it
from the squatter’s runs there will be either a
bullet or more gaol for their portion.

Another competitor for the drought prize
—Woodlands, on the Lower Darling anabranch,
Far West, N.S.W. The rain register for 1902 was
l - 58in. “ Woodlands ” —the irony of it! But,
after all, that is fairly characteristic of nomencla-
ture out in the mulga. Picture Sturt’s Meadows,
for instance —sleek sheep_ waddling in the waving
grass; sleeker cattle dozing in the shade of the
branching gums. In real life, though, Sturt’s
Meadows haven’t a stick of timber, and for many
years past have had hardly a blade of grass. See
a little bare galvanised-iron “humpy ” at the end
of a small winding dusty track that branches from
a deeper winding dusty track, in a setting of bare
rooks and a clump of eight ragged mulgas, and if
you are not told it’s Mulga Vale it’s most likely
because the man with you doesn’t know.

Vic. up-country trappers have been losing
heavily of late over consignments of dead rabbits
to Melbourne. Instead of cool, up-to-date cars
for their carriage, Bungler Bent provides a string
of slow-moving ones, and the other day, on arrival
in Melb., 20,000 putrefying pairs had to be thrown
out.

Be H. Stockdale’s remarks (8., 24/l/’O3)
about aboriginal grave memorial stones
Near Lake Bolac (Vic.) there is an old aboriginal bury-
ing-ground, wherein, some years ago, I found lying along-
side the remains of a skeleton seven stones varying in
size from that of a cricket-ball to that of a marble. These
stones are brown and very hard, the two medium sizes
having a peculiar raised “ blister ”on them. The largest
was watch-shaped, and the others became more spherical
as they decreased in size, the two smallest being perfect
spheres. Found three separate complete sets of seven,
which could not be differentiated. H.S. is probably right
in hlB ,dea that BUch stones" have been moulded.
Scattered aroU nd in the me place were other stones,
da j. w ith round edges, with small hollows in the centre,
probably intended to be held between finger and thumb
for grinding; also a stone hollowed out like a rude
mortar, with which I fancy the other kind of stone just
mentioned was used as a pestle to grind seeds and roots,

“ Polstan ” : Be “S.P.’s” ferocious
iguana (B. 17/l/’O3). The iguana, when in
danger, always makes for a tree or a stump,
and if surprised in a clearing, will often
mistake a man or a horse for such. Man
or horse aforesaid then usually. “ pulls foot,
and the reptile follows in its fright.

“ Ajab” : At first, Lake Way was a
great “specking” patch, and the gins—of
whom there were a good crowd —were great
speckers, and brought in a lot of slug gold.
After a time, however, they got fat and
lazy, and brought in but little, and the
miners of the camp were at their wits’ end
for a means to induce them to continue
specking. One day Polly appeared wear-
ing a gorgeous red dressing-gown. Then,
later, Bitty, a young gin, came round,
smiling, and looking lovely in a blue vel-
veteen dress. It was elicited from Kitty
that Charley Johns—a new arrival—had
given her the dress, and she had given him
“big feller gold.” Charley Johns had also
given Polly her gown for “two feller gold.’
Then another gin called Nelly turned up
wearing a bright red dress, and the same
day came Mary garbed in a dress of pink
and blue cretonne. A month after Charley
Johns’ arrival a dozen gins were wearing
gorgeous raiment, and Charley Johns was
still the man in charge. The other miners
who had no dress stuffs to barter made
every effort to get rid of him, and even tried
to buy him out, but all without avail. All
the gins were out specking for Charley. At
last Charley Johns, in his ignorance, lent
his rifle to a nigger to get a kangaroo.
This was a serious offence, and merited ex-
pulsion from the camp. The verdict of
the miners was soon enforced and Charley
Johns left, but he took 320 ounces of gold with
him.
• * • *
What is considered a record ride was
accomplished last October by a drover named
Charlie Turner. With three changes of horses, he
covered exactly 800 miles between Leigh’s Creek
(S. A.) and Winton (Q.) in 14 days, the route being
via Cork and Birdsville. It was to inspect a mob
of cattle for Sid. Kidman.
•'• * •
It takes a lot to stop some women from
getting home. Last Dec. a girl started from
Winton (Q.) by coach for Boulia, but on reaching
Caddie Creek it was found impossible to cross the
flood by vehicle. The driver and a passenger
then fastened a strap round' their bodies, and with
the lady clinging to it between them, successfully
negotiated a 70yds. swim. At Middleton she
swam another flooded creek on horseback, and
reached Boulia in time to have Christinas dinner
with her parents.

* • * *
“F.R.” : Tea controversy so far seems to
stand at this. (1) Bushmen don’t boil their tea;
(2) you can’t make good tea without boiling it as
the bushmen do. Facts remain that overdrawn
or boiled tea is highly injurious, even though it
may satisfy a “ craving ” ; and that nine-tenths
of Australian tea is overdrawn or boiled. It is
quite easy to believe that indulgence in a strong
solution of tannic acid in time sets up a craving
for more of that “dope”; but that only accen-
tuates the case against badly-made tea. The
stimulating and refreshing qualities to be got
from the tea-leaf (not always, by the way, from
the cheap tea-leaf, which is, as often as not,
“exhausted” leaf re-fried and re-colored with
chemicals) consist of theine, caffeine, and other
volatile extracts. They are driven off by boiling
or stewing. Australians as a race would shoot
better, digest better, and work better it they
drank more coffee and less ill-made tea. Coffee
is even more directly stimulating than tea, when
pure and properly prepared, and is a valuable
enteric disinfectant. A coffee-drinker won’t get
typhoid fever easily. And coffee can be grown
with ease in many parts of Australia.

“ Wiljar” : The laboring class in wayback
townships is having a rough time just now. There
has been practically no harvesting, and all private
and council work is postponed for want of funds.
A general exodus has taken place in search of
employment, many men leaving wives and families
behind in sore straits. Common now to see thin-
faced little kiddies, barefoot and half-dressed,
exploring the bush all day for firewood, “ under-
ground mutton,” and other necessaries. Rabbit-
skins, horse-hair, etc., are exchanged for flour,
and what ’possums the small hunters capture go
to make stew.

“T.G.F.”: Reports come to hand daily
from the country re the natural signs of approach-
ing floods, as recognised by the aborigines. We
are informed that the ants are seeking higher
ground; that the wild-fowl are deserting the

“Early on Tuesday morning a sensation was caused in

creeks for the waterless gilgais ; and that whirl-
winds are becoming more frequent. Ri iable
flood-sign is the occurrence of the great inland
dust-storms, reported at intervals during several
weeks past. As for whirlwinds, I have lived for
years in the sun-dried West, and have watched the
spiral dust-columns, known as whirlwinds, come
season after season, but never a decent shower of
rain. Dust-storms occur only at intervals of years,
coming from the West in the form of huge clouds,
brick-colored or blue-black. They pass over
slowly, the sun being obscured for 12 to 15min.
If the records are looked up, it will be seen that
for a couple of months previous to the last floods
heavy dust-storms were reported from all parts of
the West and N.W.

“ Paw-Paw ” on the green tree-ants of the
N. Queensland scrubs :
Their “nests" are always in trees, and are curiously
constructed. A suitable bunch of leaves is selected, and
the edges of the leaves are drawn together and cemented
with a fluid which is apparently emitted at will by the
little workers. When the structure :s completed it is
a comfortable dwelling,perfectly rain-proof. Entrances are
located at convenient points, which are strictly guarded.
The other day noticed that a colony was using a
“ lawyer-vine ” as a bridge between two trees I broke
this, and leaving the ends about an inch apart, “com-
pletely suspended the traffic.’’ News of the disaster was
quickly passed along the whole line of communication,
and a consultation apparently took place, after which
large numbers gathered at each end, one reaching over
the other till “ hands were joined” over the breach, and
traffic resumed over the living bridge. The aborigines
gather large numbers of these ants, squeeze the moisture
from them, mix with water, and drink it with relish
• » * *
From a letter from N.W. Queensland :
“As I write I can hear a continual ‘chop-chop.’ It
is dad and Will stripping the last few trees of
their foliage, to keep the stock alive. The tree
that grows above this tent goes to-morrow; then
we move on further South, to strip the country
there ; and, that done, on again—till the drought
breaks up.”

What with hot summers, “ the theft of
the Murray waters,” and the salting of Lake
Alexandrina, South Australia seems to be dying
of thirst. Lake A. is now so salt that stock
cannot drink from it.

How’s this for nerve ? Two brothers were
out rabbiting near Woodstock. One put his
hand up a log and was rewarded by a snakebite.
They had no knife, so the bitten man placed his
finger against one sharp stone and juggled it off
with another.

shepherding on Yalgoo run. The find yielded about
£90,000 worth of gold from a shallow deposit, and Billy
got £5OOO as one of the original vendors. He subse-
quently married Nellie and took her to England, return-
ing in course of time to his old haunts and occupation.
Last, saw Nellie about 12 months ago, employed cooking
at a hotel at Melville, a small mining camp 12 miles from
Y'algoo. Melville is named after Clarence Melville, an old
telegraphist, and the original prospector of the place. .. .
Believe he netted £3OOO from his discovery.” Now,
“ Nellie ” did notdiscover the Emerald. The prospectors,
falling in with shepherd Billy Pearce, were advised by
him to try a locality, to guide them to which he sent a
gin. They found a reef and began to sink, but not
thinking much of it, prospected a bit in spare time. One
Sunday morning, two of them out together simultaneously
knapped stone showing fine gold, one specimen being
absolutely yellow with it. That was the Emerald. They
let Billy Pearce stand in. Dollied a lot; first crushing,
16oz. to ton. When writer was there they had got out
most of the stone forming the rich, shallow, boat-shaped
deposit, and were putting through the whole of the thin
layer of surface covering the rock on the 8-acre lease, ana
said to be going loz. to the ton.

Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is thq largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited.

Mrs. Sarah Lewis, of 5 Avon-street, Glebe
Point, Sydney, says:—“Some 12 months ago,
a friend of mine told me she had used your Bile
Beans, and that they had cured her of biliousness,
and as I was at that time run down from anxiety
and over-work, she advised me to try them. I
needed a real “ pick-me-up,” as I felt as tired in
in the morning as when I went to bed, and my
appetite was greatly impaired ; in fact, I was quite
done up, and did not know what to take to recover
my strength. Although I was a little prejudiced
against medicines in general, I was persuaded to
give your Bile Beans a trial, and it is with much
pleasure that I tell you that a very few of them
worked a miracle with me, and I began to feel
the benefit at once by being quite bright after
taking one at night, and my usual health began to
return to me, and I would have no other
medicine in the house. My daughter*, was
also troubled with pimples on the fac£> and
also at times neuralgia, and both these complaints
were cured by the careful use of Bile Beans.
These few words may not voice my full opinion
of your medicine, but will give you an idea of the
high estimation in which I hold Bile Beans.”

curing biliousness, headache, indigestion, constipa-

bad breath, anaemia, loss of appetite, sleeplessness,

large box, (contains three times the quantity of

“Ses”: Be sharks (B. 31/l/’O3. “F.”
says “sharks do not attack clothed persons—as
witness divers,” etc. If a shark is hungry, he’ll
go for the clothes quick enough ; though a naked
white man, or a person dressed in white, runs
more risk than a black man, or a person dressed
in black. White makes a glaring mark in water,
and thus attracts. As to divers, they have
frequent conflicts with sharks, but are apparently
safe while on bottom. A veteran relates a close
shave he had once, while pearling. He’d been
cautioned never to stir from the bottom until he
had looked up and around, and, in making his
customary survey, spotted a huge shark overhead
watching him. He dodged behind a rock, the
shark following with a slight movement of its tail.
Then he conceived the idea of blinding the brute
with mud, and worked for dear life till the imme-
diate water was thick. Then, slipping round the
rock again, he rose to the surface, and was hauled
on board just as the shark rushed at him.
* * » *
A runaway-boy in Mackay district dis-
played so much resourcefulness and self-reliance
that his feats deserve to be chronicled. On being
brought home after a voluntary absence of some
weeks, he was asked, “Where did you sleep?”
“Under tree.” “And how did you live?”
“Lived on goanners ! ” There’s a future ahead of
that boy.

“ T.G.F.” : Owned a broody hen, but
had no eggs for l.sr to hatch. She did a “ dry
hatch ” in the corner of a large box.
About this time our cat had four
kittens in the bedroom, and as we
wanted the bedroom to sleep in,
Blobbs, the boy, put the kittens in
the case where the hen was sitting
wisely on nothing in particular
except its own tail. Went round a
couple of days later and saw the
mother of the kittens drawing them
one by one from under the hen.
She then suckled them and went
away. As soon as Madam Cat left
the youngsters the feathered female
gathered them under her and
seemed, prouder than if she had 50
chickens. The hen did all the hard
work and the cat just called in
occasionally.

“D.” :T ve a bulldog. Lately
it showed a tendency to develop
into a flea-bag. Rubbed stuff into
it. No good. Fleas increased. Some
of our incubated chickens made
friends with bully, and began picking at
his waistcoat during a quiet sleep. Bully
resented—then consented. There are no
fleas on the bulldog now, and the chickens
have ceased to cultivate his acquaintance.

“Ino” Concerning a Bird of Sleep :
Many years since, a friend of mine told me
that he and three other carriers, when down
some river for wool (think it was the Lachlan) shot
and ate some birds for supper. They all fell asleep
no more until the sun was up next morn-
ing ; then they found themselves sprawled about,
and everything just as they finished supper. They
mentioned it to a station hand. He said an aborigine
told him “ S’pose you eat that bird, tumble down sleep
quick, jump up next day all right.” Was it a fish yarn ?
• * * •
Do you eat pork ? Extracts from the
report of the Mayor of Bendigo (Vic.) on certain
local piggeries:—
In consequence of complaints made to me on the con-
dition of some of the private slaughtering houses, and of
objectionable matter being run into a drain within this
city, I visited Conway’s yards, where it is alleged that
dead horses are thrown to the pigs kept there. Although
I did not actually see pigs feeding on the carcases of
dead horses, there were distinct evidences that carcases
had recently been there, in the shape of pieces of hide,
skin, bones, and entrails. The whole yard was in a filthy
condition, and many pigs were wallowing in filthy
matter. In a large open space, immediately adjoining
the pig yard, were seen about 30 frames or skeletons of
beasts (a large number horses), which were lying in a
natural watercourse leading to the pig yard, through
which water, during wet weather, would flow.

Luck! A Merriwa (N.S.W.) woman was
bitten by a venomous snake on Saturday of t’other
week, and, after the usual treatment recovered,
but only to be bitten again on the following
Tuesday. She again recovered.

Re the Sydney P. A. Hospital.
A correspondent (name not published for
reasons which will be readily understood) makes
a specific charge of hospital mal-administration
which is calling for prompt inquiry :
My wife fell sick and was sent to a private establish-
ment for a delicate operation. Shortly after, a renewal
of distressful symptoms showed that another operation
was needed. Owing to my inability to pay the private-
hospital tariff this time, I applied for her admission to
Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney. On a Sunday morning,
armed with a doctor’s certificate, I called there and asked
for the Superintendent. I was informed that the official
was “round the wards." I waited for upwards of an
hour, without avail. At night I tried again, and then
learned that the Med. Sup. I had been kicking my heels
for in the morning was in reality away from town, on a
holiday, and the institution was in charge of another
medico, who presently gave me an Interview. He took
down my n une, address, age, etc., and my employment,
salary, family, etc., and asked what I was prepared to
pay ? I mentioned an amount, and negotiations were
nearing a satisfactory issue when I interrupted proceed-
ings on the patient’s behalf. It was on the subject of
medical pupils’ admittance to the proposed operation
Perhaps the following letter to the Medical Superinten-
dent will explain best:—
“6th Jan., 1903.
to my interview last Sunday evening
with Dr. -—, I have now, on my wife’s behalf, to inform
you that, owing to your refusal to allow' an operation to
take place at your hospital without the presence thereat
of medical pupils, my wife declines to enter vour hospital,
As I was personally assured by Dr. that the hos-
pital authorities will make no exception to the custom
observed on these occasions, and I do presume that, in a
case such as the present one, where my wife (without
adequate means to pay for private medical attention)
absolutely refuses to submit to that intolerable condition,
the hospital authorities adhere to their custom.” No
answer was received to this protest up to January 21.
It has been complained before that Prince Alfred
Hospital is too much of a “clinic” and too little
of a “hospital”; and to this very question
of the attendance of medical students at
operations and examinations reference was made
some time ago. But it was stated then—officially,
we believe—that the presence of strangers at
operations was not insisted upon when women
patients objected.

Indeterminate Sentences.
A wild and whirling letter from “Kiwi” of
Maoriland (somewhat condensed):
Enlightened opinion is against Titus Salt (B. 3/l/’O3),
in his disparagement of the indeterminate sentence.
According to Havelock Ellis (whose book, “The
Criminal,” is the best English work of the kind available)
the experiments at Elmira Reformatory, N.Y., have been
eminently successful, and in any case th.e fixed sentence
is obviously unscientific, an I therefore indefensible.
What should we think of the doctor who applied the same
treatment to each class of ailments without regard to
each individual case? Yet that is precisely what the law
does vvith criminals, although modern investigation tends
mote and more to show that crime is often a disease.
Criminologists divide criminals into two great classes—
instinctive criminals and criminals of passion ; and
medical opinion unanimously condemns the legal con-
tention that mental unsoundness is no excuse for a crime,
provided the perpetrator knew he was doing wrong.
Medical opinion, supported by so eminent a judge as
Baron Bramwell and such an enlightened thinker as
Havelock Ellis, declares that the real test should be :
“Could he help it?"—in other words: “Was accused
subject to an uncontrollable impulse?” The inability of
the law adequately to apportion punishment on cases as
they arise has long been realised ; and hence the law
prescribes the maximum penalty only, leaving a wide
margin to judges to indulge their individual idiosyncrasies.
In theory the law is not vindictive, but in practice the
public (which makes the law) is savagely vindictive, and
is perfectly satisfied as long as the culprit is punished.
Now, criminologists are agreed, and experience shows,
that punishment neither reforms the criminal nor deters
others. The whole theory of punishment proceeds upon
the erroneous assumption that the offender is a normal
man, whereas he is anthropologically, socially, physically,
and morally an abnormal. He doesn’t feel punishment,
for instance, as we fancy he does, but we measure him by
our standard, all the same. Men like Lombroso, Ferri,
Garofola, and Ellis do not want to abolish, but to
rationalise, punishment, and they are unanimous in
denouncing existing punitive methods. Most crimin-
ologists favor abolition of the death penalty,
and they agree in demanding better classifi-
cation of prisoners, abolition of solitary confinement,
and of flogging. In fact Ellis considers flogging more
brutal and demoralising than hanging. He also severely
condemns putting prisoners in charge of comparatively
illiterate men. Prison warders, he insists, should be cul-
tured and refined men and women, just as sick-nurses
are. Our whole prison system, in fact, and our methods
of dealing with crime generally, are sorely in need of
reformation.
To which a few words may be said in reply :
(1) Elmira prison is not the humane place it is cracked
up to be ; recent revelations have revealed that in some
respects it is a most brutally conducted institution.
. (2) “Criminologists” is a fine, large satisfying term,
but even llavf.lock Ellis might possibly, if he were
given an individual burglar or bogus-bank-promoter to
reform, make as bad a job of it as any ordinary gaol
warder. Anyhow, there are not enough expert criminolo-
gists to go round, so we get back to the fact that the
man in charge would often be some broken-down political
hack, and he would decide the real length of the sentence
instead of the judge. The judge generally knows a little ;
he is generally honest in his own narrow way ; and he
acts in public. The alleged “criminologist”—probably
somebody who used to vote for Seddon —would really
be a secret court, and might be actuated by petty malice,
relig ; ous prejudice, or the most corrupt motives.
(3) The present methods may be bad, but at least there
is re ison to believe that crime is diminishing a little
under them. A sentence fixed publicly by a Judge and
reviewed by the public may be unscientific ; but it doesn’t
necessarily follow that a sentence fixed or readjusted
secretly by any irresponsible person who may be called a
“ criminologist ” will be any more scientific.
(4) It is absurd to talk of all criminals as abnormal. If
that is so, everybody is abnormal. Crime is simply that
variety of sin, or objectionable conduct, which is suffi-
ciently large and tangible for the law to take hold of.
The other sins the law gives up in despair. It arrests the

woman who kills her husband with an axe ; it doesn’t
arrest the one vtho nag's a nervous husband to death, tor
it can’t locate her. It fines the.unmannerly ruffian who
spits on the pavement ; it fine the equally objec-
tionable one who spits on his own floor, for it can’t find
him—and so on. If all criminals are abnormal, then
everybody is abnormal, and when everybody is abnormal
then everybody is normal.
(5) To get down to business, will “Kiwi” send a
sufficient list of expert criminologists (men who can
really be trusted to wield the enormous power that must
be given to a man who runs a reformatory on the
indeterminate-sentence principle) in his own State to
control the gaols of his own State on the “ scientific ”
principles suggested ? He can leave out Lombroso,
Havelock Ellis, &c. They will be wanted in their own
countries.
Health Essential To Beauty.
In many of its aspects the civilisation of the
ancient Greeks has never been equalled. No race
was ever endowed with a juster perception of
natural beauty, or greater skill in interpreting it
by various forms of art. After three thousand
years the skill of the Greek sculptor is as pre-
eminent as in that far-distant day when he
handled the chisel and mallet. As a judge of
female beauty, too, he was faultless. His marble
creations still extant prove the fact beyond
question. One trembles to think what such
consummate as the ancient Greeks would
have said of that modern barbarism, the laced-in
waist. Surely the words of their language, com-
prehensive as that language is, would nave hardly
sufficed for their denunciations. None perceived
more clearly than they that only what is natural
is beautiful; and ill-health being an unnatural
condition, they modelled their statues so as to
depict health and strength in absolute perfection.
Miss Ethel Williams, of 9, Wemyss-street,
Sydney, N.S.W., has, we think, a full share of
beauty. But she was once in danger of losing it—
a danger now happily averted. “For several
years,” writes Miss Williams on 27th June, 1902,
“my life was rendered miserable by indigestion
and general debility. It would be impossible to
oxaggerate my sufferings during that dreadful
period. My strength declined daily, I wasted to
a shadow, and became pale, nervous, and dejected.
My parents were alarmed on my account, and
obtained a variety of medicines for me ; but none
of them did me any good, for unfortunately
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup was not one of
them. Often at meal-times I would have to leave
the table to vomit; and on such occasions as this
did not occur I would, soon after swallowing a
few morsels, feel acute pain in the chest and an
oppressive sensation of suffocation. I could not
sleep at night, but was so drowsy and heavy in
the day that I would sometimes fall asleep over
mv work. In addition to all this misery, I was
often afflicted with severe sick headaches and
bilious attacks. I continued to grow worse until
an old family friend, hearing of my illness,
recommended Mother Selgel’s Curative Syrup.
He spoke so highly of it that my father at once
bought a bottle. I did not think there was any
likelihood of its benefiting me, but 1 persevered
with it to please my parents. Within a week I
discovered that the Syrup was doing me sub-
stantial good. The pain in the chest diminished,
and I could eat, work, and sleep better than I had
done. Eight bottles were sufficient for my com-
plete and lasting ouye.”*

Some of Bishop Webber’s reasons for
demanding British support of his Brisbane
cathedral scheme:
1. During the past 17 years Q. has received from the
old cojuntry 80,026 unprbpvrlied immigrants whose
passage has been paid or assisted by the Q. Government.
By the 1901 census 37 p.c. of Q. population is Church of
England.
2. The seven years’ drought has impoverished Queens-
land. Many thousand sq. miles of Q. belong to London
companies and absentee owners and a great part of the
money made in Q. is spent in England.
3. Of £18,8*1,987 paid during the last 17 years in
interest on public loans no less than £17,4§6,G93 has been
paid to.the old country.
And the moral to all this is—according to the
Bishop—let the British investor shell out in aid of
the cathedral. Meantime, wouldn’t it be better if
the average Queenslander set himself to work
diminishing, by discriminative legislation, the
number of absentees? Wouldn’t it be well if a
stand were made against this foreign-loan business
so that there should be no increase in this annual
drain of millions interest, mostly paid to the
old country? Were this done it is quite possible
Q. might continue to exist without having to send
a Bishop home to cadge for a cathedral.
