POLITICAL
POINTS
Why has the Mongrelian capitalistic press
ceased its wild shriek about the impending ruin of
the sugar industry ?
N.S. W. Political Labor League has decided
to retain Federal Old Age Pensions as a fighting
plank in its Federal platform.
Australians are meeting with little luck in
war matters. Now all the sailor-men who took
part in the late Chow row get medals except the
Australians of the gun-boat Protector.
Commission of inquiry as to the Federal
oapital site seems to be taking evidence mostly as
regards Fogs, Gooseberries, and Bricks; the pre-
cise connection of these matters with the suitable-
ness of a national capital is hard to understand.
M‘Kenzie Vic. Minister of Lands, im-
peached for afteged dummying, frankly told the
Vic. Assembly that he is “what is called a
squatter.” Just as well to remember that Premier
Irvine selected “ what is called a squatter” for the
head of the Lands Department.
Senator Glassey is a politician who has
“ thorough ” for his motto. He has just started out
on a lung exhibition, intending to address over 50
meetings between Normanton in the north and
Toowoomba in the south. Mr. Glassey receives
very little press countenance in Q., and hopes to
achieve per lung what he cannot get done by
leading articles.
Queensland Protectionists have been
galvanised into activity by George Reid’s volcanic
fiscal crusade. The local organisation, split over
the Kanaka-labor question at last election, is now
concentrating and collecting funds. Q. manu-
facturers feel that it is better to have industries
and no Tommy Tanna than Tommy Tanna and
no industries.
Snow’s Adelaide tramway syndicate has
got a year’s extension of the time in which it
is to pay for the old tramways—till the end of
1903. By it 3 Act of Parliament it is bound to
establish over 40 miles of electric tram by the end
of 1904, but it will probably want an extension of
time on that matter also.. Syndicates mostly
exist on the Ministerial doormat, cadging for
extensions and concessions.
OVERHEARD ON AN OCEAN-LINER.
DE JONES: “ Well, you'll excuse me for a little
while—I want to go and visit my tonsorial
expert .”
MRS. DE BROWN: “ You surprise me! I wasn't
aware that you suffered from any throat-
trouble.”
Sorrowful truth from the leading columns
of Sydney D.T.: —
Without vetoing Commonwealth laws, Imperial states-
men wishing to thwart the aspirations of the Australian
people have a surer means of doing so by “nobbling”
the colonial politicians who are entrusted with the duty
of giving effect to them. Of course, this does not imply
anything in the way of sordid corruption, but there is a
side to human nature upon which other influences can
always be calculated to operate with more or less effect.
With weak statesmen the glamor of Imperialism, when
brought to bear upon their weakness, can, and frequently
does, cause them to see things in quite a different light
from that of practical Australian interest. In this way
our laws may be shaped unconsciously to suit the con-
veniences of Downing-street.
According to Brisbane Courier, Premier
Philp is weak and halt; Foxton is an arbitrary
despot; Dalrymple is a “tired and weary ad-
ministrator”; Cribb is a Treasurer who cannot
grasp the needs of the country. The Courier’s
abuse is the one piece of political luck these poli-
ticians have had during the existence of the Poll-
tax Government.
Minister Lyne, spouting at Perth, said
that, for his own part, he would not hesitate to
have the trans-Australian railway constructed at
once, “ because there can be no true federation
between Westralia and the Eastern States with-
out it.” Meanwhile, nothing more is heard about
a true federation between Tasmania and the main-
land by means of a Government steamship service.
Freedom comes with the British flag to
S’Africa. Advt. from Natal Mercury :
Absconded from Waterfall Farm, near Hill Crest,
an indentured Indian named “ Munusnmy ” ; dis-
coloration of skin on left side of chest and left cheek.
Also, indentured Indian named “Ponusamy”; scar
on right shoulder-blade, mole on right palm. Any-
one harboring same will be prosecuted.—H. G.
DAVIDSON.
“Fat” politics struck bedrock at the
Senatorial conference between the two Vic.
Houses, when Mr. Pratt, M.L.C., annoyed at
Dr. Maloney’s dema'nd for a division, arose and
solemnly moved “ That no more divisions be
taken.” The motion was even too much for the
House of 48 Rich Landlords to take seriously,
and President Wrixon pointed out smilingly that
Mr. Pratt’s own motion might a division.
Marconi’s wireless telegraph system is now
so far advanced that chess-matches have been
played between two Atlantic liners, the moves
being telegraphed from one ship to another.
Messages are being regularly received across the
Atlantic, and the new system is about to be in-
stalled to fill the breaks in the Cape-to-Cairo line.
It is getting very near time to cease calling tele-
graph lines “ permanent and reproductive works.”
Premier Irvine, at the A.N.A. banquet :
“ He would take the opportunity of stating the
obligation of the country to those broad-minded
squatters in the Western District who had com-
menced to part with their land to smaller land-
holders.” Consider the noble spectacle of the
b.m. squatter selling the land, which the State
gave him, or which he dummied from it, at £1 an
acre, to the small man at 1000 per cent, profit—
and then only driven thereto by the fear of the
coming land-tax or the fact that it pays him better
to sell land than to rear sheep.
A large part of P.M.G. Drake’s time in
Q. was occupied explaining to hungry billet-
hunters that, under Federal law, a Minister
hasn’t even a chairmanship at his disposal. But
still the worn-out ex-M.P., and the political
hanger-on vow that Drake’s cake will be dough at
next election—“if their vote and influence count for
anything.” Meanwhile, the honest citizen, who
wishes a civil service career for his son or daughter,
rejoices exceedingly. Curious that most ex-
M’s.P. fancy they have a mortgage on the State
which they have fooled or fleeced.
“A Victim” gives in brief “the
strength of” N.S.W. Civil Service Super-
annuation Fund grievance:—
By the Superannuation Act all civil servants
were forced to contribute 4 per cent, of their
salaries to a pension fund. Fully 50 per cent,
of them protested against the reduction, having
a lively remembrance of John Robertson’s pre-
vious Pension Act that “went bung.” How-
ever, all contributors to that fund were repaid,
with 6 per cent, interest added. Not so in the
present case. Hundreds of contributors can-
not get a cent of their money, through hav-
ing either retired or been removed from
the service. A clause in the Act stated
that removed C.S.’s were to forfeit all
right to a pension, but nothing was
said about getting back their
money contributed. Depart-
mental officials state that
neither the past nor the pre-
sent Acts provide for a
refund, so stick to montfy
that in equity they should
disgorge. The whole affair
from first to last is a bare-
faced robbery.
“ Glenara ” gives
food for reflection :
Re the necessity of keep-
ing the “ Federal Idea”
well in view of a care-
less country, with the
great dailies continually
pouring poison in the
ears of the politically-
asleep people, it would
not be very strange if
Federation did become
a thing to be only men-
tioned with curses. But a
great contributing cause to
whatever genuine dissatisfaction exists (as
far as Vic. is concerned) is, that the people
feel that they have, to a certain extent, been
tricked in the matter of State Parliaments. There
is no denying that when Federation was being fought
for, press writers and platform speakers made a great
point of the saving that would be effected by the
abolition, or, at least, vast reduction of the S.P’s.
Up to date they have been neither abolished nor
reduced. lam not contending that the abolition of
the S.P. will be a very great benefit to the State-
probably a well-elected, well-paid House would be’
worth its keep for a long time to come. But the people
have become thoroughly imbued with the notion that
S.P’s. are useless, unnecessary, and undesirable. And
sooner or later they will have to go. For the sake of the
Federal Idea, the sooner the better. And the party
Labor, Liberal, or Tory—that removes them will have the
strongest hold upon the political affections of Victoria.
There are rival unions in Westralia just as
there are among N.S.W. bush-workers; but the
Westralian registrar avoids the N.S.W. blunder
and steadily refuses to register the two for the
same class of men. Where both unions exist the
A.M.A. is to represent the miners and the A.W.A.
the laborers ; where only one exists whichever of
them it is may represent both. Amalgamation
might be better, but neither side approaches the
solution in a very promising spirit. Many earnest
laborites are getting tired of the wrangling
between the leaders, and feel very like bumping
the two lots of heads together.
The Australian Government, as the law’
now stands, can’t build the transcontinental rail-
way to Westralia (that line which the bumptious
Dry Dog man says they ought to have started
before now) without permission from the State
Parliaments of both S.A. and Westralia. Jenkins
Iscariot solemnly promised to introduce a Bill
granting the authority, but Jenkins’ Bill has
never appeared, and there is no news about it.
This may mean that the S.A. Government wants
only one transcontinental line (to Port Darwin), or
it may be only Jenkins’ chronic incivility.
Considering the sort of Governors now
bestowed, apparently by system, on the States,
N.S.W. for one should repeal the Parkes motion
still on the records of the House, or say what
interpretation shall be given it. That motion,
carried enthusiastically at the time MTlwraith
slammed the door of Queensland in policeman
Blake’s face, prescribed that N.S.W. Ministry
should be informed of a Governor’s name before
his appointment, and stipulated that only men
who had held distinguished civil positions or had
had eminent careers in the House of Commons
would be eligible.
An expert to The Bulletin :
In view of the fact that the Federal P. and T. Dept,
has to pay for new lines, repairs, &c., out of revenue, the
falling off in telegraphic receipts for Nov. and Dec. makes
it look as if P.M.G. Drake will have a hard row to hoe to
make ends meet, or even avoid a big shortage. The cheap
telegraph rates came into force Nov. 1, and as that month
and Dec. are reckoned the two heavy months, things look
blue for the lean times ahead. The
P.O. authorities assumed that extra-
cheap rates would lead to increased
business, but that expectation is
frustrated by the average business
man, who cuts down the address to
one or two words, uses a code, and
sends no signature, thus getting his
wires cheaper by 25 to 50 per cent.
The charges are, in many instances,
altogether out of proportion to the
distances traversed. Thus a 16-word
message from Thargomindah to
Burketown (say 1900 miles) only costs
9d., and writer saw a message pass-
ing from Pt. Darwin to Thursday
Island (about 5000 miles of wire) at a
cost of Is., and it had to be “ re-
peated ” over nine land lines and one
cable 1 In Europe or America,
where labor is cheaper and popula-
tion more dense, such a wire would
cost 3s. or 4s. Imagine a man wish-
ing to wire a 16-word message from
Glasgow to Lisbon, or from Copen-
hagen to Oporto, and offering Is. as
payment! The zone system of
charging, as in U.S.A., would be a
good way out, but the Constitution
forbids discrimination between the
States of the Commonwealth. Prac-
tical men consider that what will be
necessary, if ends are to meet, will be
—within the State, 10 words, 9d, each
additional word Id. ; inter-State, 10
words, Is., each additional word
Id. ; address and signature, if any,
to be counted. It seems a fair pro-
phecy that an Amending Postal
Rates Bill will be on the agenda-
paper next Federal session. Apropos
—lf the Constitution forbids any dis-
crimination ’twixt States, how is it
that it costs more to wire to and
from Tasmania than between any
other two States ?
Because there is a private cable-
line to Tas. for using which the
Govt, has to pay.
Q. Minister Dalrymple, speaking at the
A.N.A. annual banquet in Brisbane, referred to
a possible combination of malcontents, Indepen-
dents and Laborites as a “fantastic mixture of
oil and water —which would never mix.” What
price the Continuous-Coalition Ministry of which
the sardonic Dalrymple is now a member ? Does
the innocent Minister remember two men named
MTlwraith and Griffith? Does he remember
himself ?
One Queensland Labor member (promi-
nent in his party) is understood to hint that there
is a probability of a fusion of various “fag ends”
during next Q. State session—moderate Laborites,
the present fragmentary “ Opposition ” and vari-
ous discontented Philpites who think it is time
to come in out of the wet. The idea is to make
Speaker Morgan Premie*-, but Mr. Morgan is in a
very snug and secure billet and is constitutionally
easy-going.
It isn’t a bad thing to live in the electorate
of a Minister of the Crown—if that Minister is
Bent. Workmen’s trains arouixd Melbourne are
limited to a 9-mile radius, but down Bent’s way
workmen get the reduced rate as far as
Mordialloc —15 miles. Also, the Mordialloo
“ workman ” has an extra train laid on for him
which reaches the city about 8.45 a.m. The
mechanics of Mordialloc mostly wear bell-toppers
and Panamas.
According to Wynne, M.L.C., the Vic-
torian House of 48 Rich Landlords will accept
parts of the Irvine Reform Bill. It will accept the
ratepayers’ rail as the qualification for voters; it
will utterly reject the double dissolution and the
joint sitting; it will require two paid Ministers
instead of one; it will throw out woman’s
suffrage; it will not reduce its members below 42 ;
and it declines to lower the qualification for
membership of the Council to less than £5O annual
rateable value worth of property. And most of
the Council plainly regarded Wynne as a dan-
gerous revolutionary for going so far.
British papers are already scoffing at
Jenkins Iscariot’s land-grant transcontinental
railway. They speak of the 90,000,000-acre steal
as mere sandy desert, and reckon that only a
person with earth-hunger in a very bad and very
ill-judged form would look at Jenkins’ offer. The
book-fiend Premier seems to have misjudged the
situation. Any number of companies have built
land-grant railways in his own country ; but, un-
fortunately, good land is scarce in Australia, and
nearly all there is of it would be required to satisfy
the rapacity of one such atrocious steal company
as the Canadian Pacifio. If Jenkins got hold of
an equally rapacious syndicate he would probably
require to give it the whole Territory, and some
cash as well.
About the meanest feature of Q. poll-tax ia
its operation on the Kanaka population. AKanaka
receives a monetary wage of £6 per annum. Out
of this he has to pay a poll-tax of 10s. per annum,
or 8J per cent, on his wretched wage. Surely
Premier Philp, who was lately so distressed about
the prospects ahead of Tommy Tanna, ought to
see to a matter of this kind. No Kanaka agent
ever explained this matter to Tommy when he
embarked for Queensland.
Robert Reid, the Victorian M.L.C. and
alleged Senator, has resigned his seat in the State
House of Landlords. Thereby he acknowledges
that, just as the Federal law says, it is not legal
to attempt to sit in both Parliaments. And the
same Federal law, whose existence he thus admits,
also says that his election was null and void
because he was a State M.L.C. at the time of his
nomination. A wiser dog, when he had a week to
find out that the other thing was only a shadow,
would have hung on to his bone.
That Single-Tax bore, Maximilian Hirsch,
M.L.A., suggests that Victoria should “shut
down all its non-paying railways.” Quite a lob of
non-paying railways both in Victoria and N.S.W.
could be made payable by promoting closer set-
tlement along their routes. It is the big non-pay-
ing landlord, not the non-paying railway, that
wants closing. Would Maximilian also advocate
closing roads in thinly-populated districts, or any-
thing else in the way of a relapse into economical
barbarism ?
New Senator Reid will now have an oppor-
tunity of directly impressing the Commonwealth
Parliament with a sense of its duty. In Vie.
Council recently, he bitterly railed against the
ingratitude of Federal members, because they did
not fully and frequently express “their indebted-
ness to the State Parliament ” (not the people,
mark !) “of Victoria for placing at their disposal,
without fee or reward, a Government House and
a Parliament House which cost so much.”
Melb. Argus lately received a “ Contribu-
tion from China to the Drought Relief Fund,”
from H. McGowan, of the Custom House, Canton;
also a letter containing this :
Things are a bit slow here : the Chinese are selling
their children and women folk for a few dollars, and so-
great is the supply that a girl worth 400 to 500 dol. a few
months ago is at present only worth 20 to 30 dol.
Now the Argus has persistently barracked for
Foreign Missions, the Open Door, Free Admission
of the Yellow Agony and so on. Does that
respectable family paper want to see girls sold in
Australia at a fiver apiece when the next big
drought comes?
There is something very shady in the
Irvine Govt.’s manner of toying with the question
of a new Commissioner for Vic. Railways. Over
18 months have passed since Mathieson left, and
Irvine promised, on becoming Premier, to imme-
diately get a competent man for the Commissioner-
ship, but has got no nearer to it than twice
repeating the appointment of traffic-manager
Fitzpatrick as acting-Commissioner. All the
time, his Govt, has been making the shallowest
pretence of trying to find a permanent railway
manager. But it was obvious from the first that
this would not suit the book of Minister Bent,
who only wants a nice subservient person about
whilst he is restoring the system of political con-
trol, and incidentally making himself solid with
his constituents.
Melb. Age started belaboring the Irvine
Govt, at the most opportune time possible. The
very day the MMvenzie trouble came along, it
assailed Mines Minister Cameron with a vim
reminiscent of the Trenwith days, and a day
later began asking Bent in a threatening tone
whether he had really got a railway policy, or
whether it was his intention to keep on doing
nothing at the top of his voice. If the select-
committee inquiry into the M‘Kenzie cases leaves
the slightest shadow of suspicion round the devoted
head of that excellent Scotchbyterian land-dealer,
it s a million to one that Syme’s paper will come
down upon the whole Irvine crowd with a thump.
The rehabilitation of a somewhat discredited
journal is the first consideration, and present
indications are that by “slugging” Irvine and
his colleagues that result will be most speedily
achieved.

A Legacy from Typhoid Fever
RUNNING 50RES IN LEG FOR
EIGHT YEARS
.... CURED BY ....
VITADATIO
Epson, Auckland, December 16, 1901.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir,—lt gives me the greatest of pleasure to know
that you are about to bring your medicine, VITADATIO,
before the public and my friends in the old country. I
am sure they will thank and even bless you from their
hearts for the good you will be doing, as I, for one, out of
many thousands can prove. When you get to Plymouth
you can show my friends what I say, also at Liscard and
Bodmin, the place of my birth, especially, where they all
know m?. I had the misfortune to contract Typhoid
Fever, which caused other serious complaints, and I was
laid up 12 months. When I was able to get about the
disease went into my right leg and it was swollen very
much. Inside of 12 months it all broke out into one mass
of sores. I called in my doctor, and he told me he could
do nothing, as I would have a bad leg the rest of my
life. Not being satisfied I saw four other doctors, and
they all told me about the same thing. I gave up hopes
of doing any more work, but being advised by my friends
to try other medicines, I did so, which cost me about £4O,
and I never received any benefit whatever from them. I
then tried a newly advertised remedy, but after taking a
large course of it I was no better. 1 then thought the
doctors were right, so 1 gave up trying any more. I was
suffering for about eight years, when one Saturday night
I went to the city of Auckland and was looking at the
shop windows when I saw a lot of strange looking bottles
in a window, with a frame of testimonials from different
people. I said to my daughter “ Another lot of quackery,”
when your agent, Mr. Ibbotson, said to me I was mis-
taken, and after a little conversation with him he advised
me to try it, which I am thankful to him that I did.
After taking 10 bottles I saw a great change in the leg,
which made me persevere with the medicine until I had
taken 36 bottles, when I can truly' say my leg is well.
For I can run, skip, and jump, and I am better in health
than ever I was in my life. 1 never weighed more than
lOst. lOibs., but now lam 12st. 2lbs., and never feel tired
or out of sorts. If anyone doubts my statements they can
write to me, or to a dozen other people who can testify to
my cure. I may add that I am doing all in my power
for others to try VITADATIO for the suffering com-
munity. Wishing you a pleasant voyage to the dear old
country.
I remain, yours truly,
T. H. ROWE, Blacksmith,
Auckland, New Zealand.
For further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.
Correspondence Invited. Write for Testimonials.
The Price of Medicine is 5/6 and 3/6 per bottle.

The sale of liquor on Sundays would soon
be put a stop to if every policeman were as reso-
lute and as resourceful as Sergeant Harrison, of
Redfern (Sydney). He had been watching a
hotel, and, from suspicions thus received, knocked
and entered. There was nobody in the bar and
no direct evidence of liquor having been sold
Jt>ut he
went into the bar, and, after examining the glasses came
to the conclusion that a good deal of drinking had just
been going on He found glasses, bottom upwards
which contained froth. F
The Bench considered the case proved on this
evidence. Hitherto convictions for liquor-selling
on Sunday have been hampered by the fact that!
the law being tacitly condemned as unreasonable
reasonable methods of proving lapses have not
been used. The Bulletin, apart from the issue
of Sunday beer or no Sunday beer, would like to
see the Sunday closing law mercilessly enforced
Whilst the law exists it should be respected'
-f hen, if experience shows it to be intolerable it
can be amended. ’

"A Plunge in Panamas.”
r\ESPITE the scarcity of this fashionable headgear, as
the result of special arrangements when visiting the
centres of manufacture,

The first prosecution under the bye-law
against spitting on footpaths has just taken plaoe
in Adelaide. The notices prominently displayed
have been enough to check the habit, but a sailor
who offended was fined a shilling and costs as a
warning.
Three new consumption “ cures ” are just
now before the public, two serums cultivated by
European doctors, and an inhalation method put
G woman—the idea of which
is to attack the bacillus in its feeding ground, the
lung, with hot dry medicated air. Since the
present rational” treatment of phthisis is by
~ °Pj en which practically means warfare on
the bacillus by oxygen, it is possible that methods
of cure will in the future include some medication
of the air breathed, with the idea of increasing its
germicidal properties. A resort to “mountain
air practically means that ; and the hot dry
medicated-air idea may prove of some value ; the
latest Sydney idea, though, cannot be seriously
discussed whilst it is a “ secret remedy.”
People behind the scenes in Yic. politics
believe that the cold but reputable Irvine, if not
jumped out of the Premiership within a month or
two, will voluntarily throw it up. When the
JVI ivenzie affair was brought on in the Assembly, it
waa evident to all that Irvine was sick and tired
of it all, and for the first time in his Parliamen-
tary career the icy Equity lawyer got an emotional
hitch m his voice and seemed on the point of tears
It was exactly the same kind of break in the voice
frequently recurring, which heralded the physical
and political collapse of Irvine’s predecessor,
Peacock. And the Iceberg winces badly under
the slightest personal allusion. When little Dr.
Maloney the other night playfully spoke of “ the
profession at which Mr. Irvine got his living ere
he became Premier” the Yic. “reform” boss
looked as uncomfortable as though dining with a
savage who ate peas with a knife.

Unionist until something better turns
up writes
Vic. Government’s “ultimatum” to the Loco. Ensrine-
drivers and Firemens’ Association and other railway
employees unions, demanding their severance from the
Trades Hall, is about the most audacious attempt vet
made upon the liberty of a section of the community by
the Bent Government. The blow seems to have been
aimed directly at the loco, men, for, whereas other
organisations have been affiliated with the Trades Hall
for years, the Drivers’ Association had only joined a few
hours when “ sat upon ” The Acting-Commissioner’s
memo, to the various bodies concerned drawing their
attention to Rule 32 is a very carefully-worded document
( Rule 32 is— No employee shall be a member of a
political association of any kind.”) He suggests severance
from the Trades Hall—it cannot be taken as an order
and was no doubt written regretfully. It has been sug-
gested that the societies thus called upon “receive” the
precious memo, and order it to “ lie on the table ” pend-
ing further consideration. It is an important matter
and warrants the men’s earnest consideration for (say) a
year or two. The systematic crushing of the loco, men’s
association adopted by the present Govt, is a bitter con-
trast to the tone adopted towards it only a short year or
two ago. Then, at the men’s annual banquet (there was
none last year!), Ministers, M’s.P., and head railway
officials vied with each other in saying the pleasantest
possible things about that “ splendid branch of the ser-
vice.” Verily the glory of the footplate has departed.

The dignity of the suburban Mayor falls
in sometimes. Recently, on the night on which
the nominations for the local council closed, his
Worship, having retired early, was aroused a few
minutes before midnight by loud, authoritative
knocking at his door. Poking his head out of his
top window, his lordship found half-a-dozen
persons awaiting him below. On demanding
their business, he was informed that it was
official. They wished to nominate another
aldermanic candidate ere it was too late. Then
the pride of that suburb’s church waxed angry,
and was about to bang his window down,’
when the spokesman of the crowd reminded him
that he was compelled to accept nominations up to
twelve that night. The spokesman also enlarged
on the awful consequences of a breach of the law,
and did it with such eloquence that, before he had
quite finished, the mayor was at the door in his
nightshirt and demanding the papers, for Heaven’s
sake, before the clock stopped striking. The
deputation then handed the documents over with
much seriousness and retired. Next morning his
worship opened them and found them to consist
of a demand to know where he got that hat. or
something to that effect.
What; should have been described in the
daily papers as a “pathetic incident” occurred
outside St. Kilda cemetery on the day of the
gorgeous Sargood funeral. As the magnificent
spectacle wound its way from .Dandenong road
through the gates, with the military bands re-
sounding, a shabbily-dressed, elderly Irishwoman
leaned agaunst the fence and sobbed bitterly.

An Ex Mayor’s Opinion.
The opinion of a man is often useful, especially
when the man is well-known to be a master of the
subject upon which he speaks; then his state-
ments are more than mere opinions, they are
absolute facts. Such, for instance, is the statement
recently made by Mr. P. A. Phillips, ex-Mayor
of Auckland, who, after spending many years in
public service in New Zealand, has now come to
permanently reside at Woollahra, Sydney. His
opinion of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills is one
founded upon the most substantial grounds, for he
himself has personally tested them, and found
them, use in his own words, of “ inestimable
value. ’ _ “ I suffered,” he says “from flatulence,
dyspepsia, and indigestion, together with their
usual train of horrors. In my search for a remedy,
1 tried every known medicine, and I must have
taken a cart-load of drugs and patent medicines.
All of these were useless. None removed the
cause of my trouble, and it was only when at last
1 took Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills that I found
tv tv*- 8,11 , a P e . rman ent cure for my ailments.”

purifier, and a positive cure for biliousness,
indigestion, constipation, headaches, sallow com-

and blotches, and for female ailments. Put up in
amber bottles and the full name blown thereon.*

• * • •
Australian Peer, who died the other week, was
a persistent opponent of the more famous Abercorn
during their 3 and 4-year-old careers. They met over
a dozen times, and Mr. James White’s chestnut
champion had practically nothing the better of the rivalry.
Whenever they met in a w.f.a. race one or other of them
actually won, and in nearly every case the beaten battler
finked second. Abercorn was first and the Peer came
third, behind Niagara, in the A.J.C. Derby, but in V.R G
Derby it was the Peer first, with Abercorn and Niagara
running a dead heat for second. Abercorn took both the
Degers. In their other contests up to two miles
honors were easy. Moreover, the Peer won a Sydney
Gup, carrying 8.6 as a 3-year-old ; and although Abercorn
outlasted his rival on the turf, he never managed to
capture one of the big handicaps.

Famous ’Murkan-bred trotting sire Honesty
died at Wangaratta (V.). Late Mr. R. Towns, of Hobart-
ville (N.S. W.), brought Honesty and Childe Harold to
this continent at same time. Both have improved the
quality of Australian trotting stock immensely.

Big disappointment Skein Dhu, who, for long,
while doing watch-breaking things on the track, failed
to materialise under colors, and so brought wealth to
Mo, ran near her best at Canterbury Park, Saturday.
She struck hard luck in being badly interfered with in
the straight by Santoi, who passed the post half-a-neck
to the good. Protest by Skein Dhu’s owner was dismissed
by stipendiary stewards, and notice of appeal to A.J.C.
given ; till that is decided, bets are in abeyance. Con-
trary to usual state of affairs when nags of Skein Dhu’s
sort score, she was heavily backed, and in the last race
(Shorts Handicap) same day, carrying 71b. penalty,
picked up a big stake for her stable easily.
• • • *
John Hill, head of South Australia’s one big
coaching firm, has been telling people how to feed
horses. The prescription : From 251 b. to 301 b. per diem,
in four doses. (Only heavy draughts require a full 301 b.)
To make 301 b., take 31b. of bran and pollard, 21b. of oats
Jib. of molasses ; the balance, sound chaff.
Full merit of N.S.W.’s success by five wickets
over Victoria, last week, doesn’t seem properly under-
stood. ’Twas a bowler’s pitch that the greater part of
home team batted on in its initial innings. Victoria
was very much more favored in this respect. Duff evi-
denced lots of Trumper in his work when piling up that
132—quite the best display Sydney has had from
him, and an exceptionally bright showing. Last day
revealed another N.S.W. man with big possibilities
ahead in the person of R. N. Hickson, who rose
to a ticklish occasion and, together with Noble,
was responsible for an unfinished effort of 152
a record for sixth wicket in N.S.W.-Victoria matches.
Hickson, a novice in inter-State cricket, has been on the
N.S.W. side in four matches this year, and while doing
well previously his latest score—B9 not out—is his best.
Home players wanted 268 to win when they opened their
second innings. Once, with 5 down for 118, things looked
blue, but the Hickson-Noble combination saved the day,
and to that pair alone is the win due. Noble’s compilation
was 102, but his was a lucky innings; he gave three
palpable chances and the ball beat him badly many times,
still it was fine under the circumstances.
* * * *
Saturday last saw Victor Trumper engaged in
club cricket for the first time since going to England.
8000 people watched him, and in 2h. 45min. he hit up 335
for Paddington against Redfern. Twenty-two strokes
brought fivers, the bails disappearing into adjacent
streets, through kitchen windows and other places.
But nobody minded, they were proud to have their win-
dows broken by Victor Trumper. Thirty-nine strokes
brought four each, and the afternoon saw him make the
record individual score for Sydney cricket. Every time
Trumper opened his shoulders crowds ducked their
heads ; and the conduct of the spectators and the fields-
men all went to prove that Sydney takes champions far
too seriously. The mana of Achilles was a miserable
thing compared to that of a champion of any sort in
modern Sydney.
* • • •
Lord Hawke’s team has not found opponents
worthy of its willow in Maoriland, where opposing teams
of anything, between 11 and 22 have been beaten by
wickets and innings. Said that the team is itching to
measure its strength against Cornstalk and Victorian
teams and is threatening many centuries, but if the
measuring takes place the Hawkes will find a vast differ-
ence between Maoriland and Australian cricket.
• • * •
Melbourne press and public have been com-
plaining lately of the deadly dullness of cricket, but if
they went out countrywards they would find the “ grand
old game” played with quite sufficient liveliness and
spirit. On a recent Saturday, Cathcart and Salt Creek
met for a friendly game, but before the finish, players,
bats, stumps, fists, ball, black eyes and spectators had
become hopelessly mixed in a wild and woolly rough-and-
tumble. Country cricketers are suggesting the advis-
ability of having a new rule to the effect that all players
who do not feel equal to a “ scrap ” might be allowed to
leave the field under the protection of a white flag when
the game begins to get too willin’.
• • • *
Inter-State cricket-match concluded last week
was the 70th played between Vic. and N.S.W. Victoria
has now a lead of but four games, the last few seasons
seeing the mother State steadily gaining. Unless the
Vies, can unearth a bowler to keep Trumper and Duff
quiet, N.S.W. will soon be in the lead again, a position
not held by her for 25 years.
* • * *
For Petersham Wyrallah against Glenroy, at
Norwood Park, Sydney, Saturday, T. Brown bagged 10
wickets for 1 run, the result of an overthrow. Eight
were clean bowled, one caught, and one l.b.w. Alto-
gether, same afternoon, same man secured 16 for 10, and
did the hat trick in each innings.
• • * •
N.S.W.’s unbroken record of victories in con-
tests for the Sheffield Shield for 1902-3 was gained, as tar
as batting was concerned, by three men—Duff, Noble and
Trumper. In the four inter-State matches the dozen
N.S.W. reps, totalled 1955 runs, of which the trio scored
583, 343, and 393. Duff’s average was 73. Howell
headed the bowling with an average of 17.
• * * •
N.S.W. stumper J. J. Kelly is easily the boss
of his section of the game. Against Vic. last week he
was very smart, only 14 sundries, including two no
balls, being scored in totals of 207 and 427. Vic.
wicket-keeper Hastings had 58 sundries (including six no-
balls) in totals of 367 and 269. Kelly stumps with auto
matic regularity, and very, very rarely lets an oppor-
tunity slip.
• • * m
“G. A. Y.”: What an extraordinary hold cricket
has upon the Australian community? Even dogs play
cricket —fact—for do we not hear of greyhounds “ run-
ning a bye ? ” Boozers, too, play cricket. I have knowh
a “ beer-chewer” to waltz into a pub. and start running
up “ a big score,” until the landlord “ran him out”;
then a policeman ran him in, and, as he couldn’t find his
“ bails,” he had a good innings for three months. Also
it’s common for the captain of a “ Monkey Park Eleven ”
(age about 9) to bounce into the local tobacconist’s for
“ a penn’orth of cigarettes, and would yer mind putting
the team in yer winder.” Writer once witnessed a
match between a team calling themselves “ The Indepen-
dents ” (independent whether they worked or not) and
another hailing from a well-known brewery. Latter
turned upon a lorry, with tjie usual barrel of squirt on
board. Independents won the toss, grabbed their panni-
kins, and started batting on a “ wet wicket.” The
wicketkeeper was simply marvellous 1 ; he “never let a
beer pass him ” all day. And, as for the fielders, they
couldn t stop a tram, let alone a ball. One bowler did
the “ hat trick.” He took it around the crowd and col-
lected three “ bob.” Independents scored over 100 runs
on the barrel! Captain wanted to “ follow on,” but the
brewery team “ weren’t having any.” Match ended in a
win for Independents by three gallons.

“Nym.”: In an account (recently printed in
the Australasian) of a fishing-excursion to Water Re-
serve No. 101, Edwards River, 45 miles from Deniliquin
the writer states that, using light fly rods and tackle, and
red and copper “ spoons" about 2in. long, in one day he
and another landed 17 perch up to 3£lb., and 25 cod up to
151bs. ; total weight 1261b5. ; and next day caught 54 fish
totalling 1621b5.; giving for two days a total of 2881bs
all taken by spinning from the bank. I have fished the
Murray, Ovens, King, and Campaspe since boyhood, and
have never yet hooked a cod on an artificial bait—frog
shrimp, or red-rag. The golden perch is the only fish that
I have known to dart at a moving bait. The cod only
shows his ferocity in this respect when a small live fish is
being slowly drawn to land. I am inclined to look upon the
“ spoon ’’ business as a page from “ Anglers’ Tales,” and
would like to know if any Bulletin fish-liars have used
“ spoons ” with success. If the Australasian writer’s
tatements are true, then half the trouble of angling is
gone, for the scarcity of bait when fish are “ on the bite ”
is proverbial. I have had my piscatorial leg pulled so
often that I’m very sceptical.

A tale of the Major Taylor cycling carnival as
told byone of the competitors:—“l felt that I could
easily win the event, but, when I tried to get out, I found
I was pocketed, couldn’t get an opening anywhere. The
prize was £lO, and I said to one of the men stopping me,
‘ Say, let us out and I’ll give yer two quid.’ ‘ No,’
he said, very short. 1 Well, take us along a bit,’ I asked.
‘ No,’ he said. I didn’t win—couldn’t. I found after-
wards that they were all in it to keep me blocked.” This
is published for the benefit of donkeys who bet on cycling
races.
• * • *
Motor-cycle racing is not likely to be popular
with the public. The speed attained by the noisy
machine, whereon the rider sits like an amateur jock, on
a bolting horse, is certainly terrific ; but there is no final
“ kick,” no “ lifting over the line ” by inches as with the
man-pushed bike. The average crowd likes to see muscle
bursting itself in a hard finish. The knowledge that
the attainment of more speed is only a matter of turning
a handle takes away most of the interest.
* * • •
“ Tyred ” : Was induced (by the advt.) to go to
Melb. Exhibition Oval, last Saturday afternoon, to see
“ The greatest, most interesting, absorbing, exciting,
electrifying cycling matches ever submitted to sporting
patrons in Victoria.” It was a M.B.C. entertainment.
The third item on the programme was about to begin,
and the notice-board announced that the “ match ’’would
go a distance of 40 laps. O ! the excitement of that
match ! A man started to travel round the track on what
appeared to be a motor bicycle, having its motive-power
stored in a tin arrangement resembling a miniature
baked-potato can. He didn’t work the treadles—just sat
on the saddle, in a crouching position, and steered the
feeble vehicle round the track. As a slow-going motor,
this was probably unique. When the man had pursued
his lonely career for some minutes, another man on a faster
motor commenced to run rings round him. Motor No. 2,
however, was a short-lived joy. As soon as a third vehicle,
the fastest of the three, started its 40-lap journey, with a
man perched upon its saddle, the No. 2 motor struck work.
Many vain attempts were made to restore animation to
this unhappy machine. An M. 8.0. official, in frockcoat and
belltopper (temperature, 105 in the shade) tried to urge
the reluctant No. 2 motor to a sense of duty by pushing
it from the rear. Other willing workers came from the
centre of the arena and contributed their respective
shoves to the lost cause, but No. 2 had seemingly burst
its boiler and could race no more. So it retired from the
match. Meanwhile, the potato-can motorist continued
on the even tenor of his way like a well-regulated funeral.
He rode a strictly inside track, whilst No. 3 motorist, the
lightning-speeder of the occasion, passed him again and
again. The notice board recorded the laps as they were
travelled. 30 more to go—2o to go—lo to go. Still the
absurd potato-can person plodded on. Heaven knows
how many laps he covered. He didn’t appear to win the
blessed match. When No. 3 man had put in 40circuits of
the course a pistol went off, and the limited crowd
cheered him as though he had done something to be
proud of in sitting quietly on his cycle. Then came the
Great Event of the afternoon—Major Taylor v. T. Robl
a 10-laps “ race,” paced by motor cycles, with two riders
on each pacer. About 20 minutes were wasted whilst
the Australian riders of Taylor’s motor-pacer tried to
understand how they were going to make a satisfactory
race against Robl and his expert German pacers. Finally
the pistol was fired, and the Robl trio went right away
from the Taylor party. The procession did not present
the semblance of a real race. Robl’s pioneers simply
showed the way to Taylor’s pioneers, and there was an
end of the “ match.” The idea of submitting stuff like
that to “ sporting patrons! ” Where was the sport ? In
the race between the two double-manned motors, Taylor
and Robl didn’t race against one another as horses, peds
oarsmen and swimmers race. They followed pace. Each
was the tail-piece of a machine, and Robl proved to be the
more expert tail-piece of a machine that outpaced Taylor’s
machine. The darkie seemed to be working harder
with his legs than the other fellow, hut his extra exertion
had no influence upon the motor to which he was allied
Truly an absurd sport is the modern pace-following
competition. Why do a couple of champion cyclists want
motors to assist them in a “ test ” of speed, endurance and
courage (which is something different from mechanical
staying power) over a two-mile course ? They might start
level with one another on opposite sides of the track
their heads turned in opposite directions, and race
like men “on their own.” Each would be chasing
the other at a distance of half-a-lap from the
outset, and the champion who passed the other or
finished within a shorter distance than half-a-lap of
his hind wheel would be declared victor in the sight of
Heaven and the crowd. This is obviously the right way
of deciding the relative merits of two cyclists on a circu-
lar track. And, presuming that bicycle tracks were
straight— a straight mile, or a straight 10 miles with
parallel hnes of railings running the full length of the
track to keep the competitors apart, so that nobody could
hang on to anybody else’s wheel—given those ideal
conditions for a contest—would anybody out of a lunatic
asylum propose to introduce pacing machines into the
contest:
* * * •
Foreign pace - following cyclists, Robl and
Diekentman, upset all local traditions by discarding-
massage wholly. While the Australian cyclist spend!
much time being rubbed down by a horny-handed
trainer, Robl and Diekentman simply take a bath
put on their clothes and start to smoke their wav
through several boxes of cigarettes daily

• • » •
Sydney Gaiety A.C. saw a wondrous scrap last
week when Black Barnes and Newcastle Bill Smith, both
heavyweights, rolled round the floor in one another’s
arms and hit out wildly and anyhow when they did
occasionally get right end uppermost. Twice Barnes was
floored by vicious uppercuts, which landed where they
brand cattle, and sent head first across the ring. The end
came through Smith falling upon Barnes who was resting
on his hands and knees, and flattening him out so that he
was counted out before he could rise.
• * • *
What a forefront bruiser earns. Dan Creedon,
back again after ten years in America and England, tells
that the biggest purse he ever fought for was 12,000d015.
and, though then beaten by “ Kid ” M‘Coy in 16 rounds’
he got half the prize. Twice in one week at Chicago he
met the “ Barbadoes Terror,” black Joe Walcot (gorilla-
like in build and appearance), for a purse of 4000d015.,
and whacked the money each time. After winning the
British heavy-weight championship by defeating Jem
Smith he travelled with a big circus, and was paid £l6O
weekly for two or three months, then £B5 and £7s—these
latter amounts while showing at music-halls.
• • * *
Pug. match at Sydney Gaiety A.C. on Monday
night between welterweights American Jack Grace and
Australian George Johns, weighing 9.2£ and 9.2 J respec-
tively, saw the hall densely packed everywhere. Johns,
deeply sun-tanned all over, stood higher and had greater
length of limb than his pallid, shorter, but more heavily,
constructed adversary. From 2to 1 betting went to 6to
1 on Johns. Grace was never in it once. For three
rounds Johns literally punched him anywhere and every-
where, sending home very heavy blows. Grace would
rise dazed and staggering, to be floored again and come
up in the same condition. This went on till the middle
of the third round. The amount of punishment the
’Murkan took was enormous. Ultimately, while Grace
still rocked and sagged, and looked vacant, the referee
gave Johns the verdict.
• • • *
Pigeon shooter Neild, who, through sheer in-
ability to recognise the ridiculous, calls himself “Locks-
ley,” showed up well in the Berrima (N.S.W.) shoot, the
other day, dropping 56 straight from the 31 yards’ mark
and scoring 35 first-barrel kills. Another exceptional
feat was that of Fonville. The Yass shooter’s sequence of
56 sparrows were all cleanly shot without the semblance
of a fluke.
• • • •
Footscray regatta, formerly one of Victoria’s
leading rowing fixtures, was revived last Saturday after
an interval of 20 years. Among those who witnessed the
show were Mr. George Upward, “father of Australian
rowing,” who stroked a senior four to victory the last
time the regatta was held. Mr. Upward is a man to
whom rowing has been a factor of success in life. Had
Fate fixed him in an inland town, instead of in Geelong, he
would not ush with the black rod in the Senate to-day.
The oldest performer in Vic. waters now is AlecChamley,
who has been rowing for nearly a score of years, and who
was in the winning senior pair at Footscray. He has
rowed in more inter-State races than any other two men.
His peculiarity is that he is a bow-sid‘e oar and never
show's to advantage on the stroke side. In Vic., unlike
N.S.W., the stroke oars are to the right of the oarsman.

Printed in South Australian papers that local
swimmer, Moon, if he beats Cavill, will go to England
with a view to swimming the Channel. This certainly
looks like a bad case of counting the chickens. Moon
hasn’t the calibre fer the Channel swim.
* * • •
The 220yds. championships of Victoria and
N.S.W. will both be decided Saturda}'next. Difference
between respective winners’ times will be interesting.
Victoria’s fastest 100 of late years is somewhere about
1.12, while N.S.W.’s best, accomplished this season, is
59sec. The extent of the Vic. slump is illustrated by the
fact that 20 years ago Walter M’lndoe, the then champion,
did it in 1.5.
Victoria had a furious heat spell on
Saturday and Sunday, Melbourne shade tem-
perature going up to 105, and the whole State
frizzling under 100 or more. A curiosity of the
great Australian climate is that Melbourne and
Adelaide, though nearly 1000 miles further from
the Equator than Brisbane, can beat the northern
city easily in a heat-wave competition.
Mr. J. S. Evison, a journalist well known in
Australia and Maoriland, and whose tales, chiefly
of Oriental soldier-life, have attracted much atten-
tion in a wide circle, has been stricken by internal
cancer, and now lies bravely awaiting the end at
his home at Bondi, Sydney. Mr. Evison’s slender
resources have been quite exhausted by his hope-
less illness, and it now behoves his friends to bear
a hand in alleviating the suffering of his last days.
Any subscriptions which may reach The Bulletin
office will be thankfully acknowledged.

importance having to cadge funds to start a School

ot initiative and self-reliance on the part of our

The Arkansas mixture sold by Mick Simmons
possesses qualities over and above those of any other
tobacco. No mixture ever put upon the market has
caught on as Arkansas has done, and when once a
smoker has enjoyed it, he tells all his friends that
Arkansas is the best smoke in the world.*
The bright man who would be always at
his brightest, needs to pay particular attention to
the brand upon the bottle. Great enterprises
have been utterly ruined by two or three nips of
the whisky that inebriates but does not cheer,
lhe man who has once tried D.C.L. never falters
m his aHegiance. Its title of “The Whisky of
the Wise is now a household phrase, approved
by thousands of keen business men and clever
poets. A bad deal or a dull verse has never been
transacted on D.C.L.•
How liable to tarnish is the financial credit
of the See Govt. ! February 1 fell on a Sunday,
so the See Govt, had to pay either on Saturday
Jan. 31, or Monday, Feb. 2. The banks and the
pay branches of the service alike strongly object
to paying on a Saturday ; so on Thursday it was
announced that no salaries would be paid till
Monday. On Friday somebody wrote to Sydney
D.l. and gloomily hinted that the Govt, had no
money, and that putting off paying until Monday
would, m some mysterious manner, save their
bacon. At once Treasurer Waddell waxed indig-
" a A t A t “L® monstrous imputation, and announced
that the Govt, would pay on Saturday. The man
who wrote to the D.T. then winked joyfully. He
had drawn his badger. J
Vic. ex-Premier Peacock’s “ luck ” sticks
to . mm. A week after his apparent political
suicide in getting defeated for the Senate, his
mistake was forgotten in the hubbub raised by
the charges of “dummying” made against Lands
Minister MKenzie. If these accusations lead to
f .xl? 16 -'? downfall, it will be another instance
ot the bmiler’s luck, as the total disruption of the
Iceberg Govt, will surely follow, and the ap-
parently hopeless Opposition leader will bob up
again, if he plays his cards properly. But
x eacock has of late been a very indefinite sort of
person—out of sorts with some of his own party
openly defiant of the Labor corner, and danger-
ously disinclined to criticise the other side. It
has been rumored that he was likely to become a
cage-mate of Shiels the Haven, but his proverbial
luck may save him from that folly.
The Irvine Govt. (Vic.) has only been in
office six months, and has already achieved the
following record of scandals, or alleged scan-
mu ’’ ,lV can ? ing on the work of “reform”:
lhe MKenzie alleged “dummying” case, the
Waranga basin business (special advantages were
scored by a contractor with influence), the ap-
pointment of five of Mr. Tom Bent’s friends as
railway scouts, and the suggestion that Mr.
Robert Reid should draw two salaries. A sug-
gestion to purchase a useless private line to an
idle sugar-mill was just bowled out in time. The
tame Peacock Ministry was in office nearly two
y ears without any such things occurring. “Re-
form is a banner under which peculiar enter-
prises can be carried out.
From a description of the effects of
salicylic acid (with which Australian beer is so
often 1 ‘ preserved ”) on the human organism :—
Human beings showed, after poisonous doses, vomit-
mg, unconsciousness, delirium, slow and gasping respir-
ation, acute deafness, dysphagia and fall of temperature,
irregular pulse, as well as anaesthesia of the feet.
That characteristic effect of the acid—numbing
and rendering useless the feet—perhaps partly
explains the drunk who “goes in the legs” after
over-free use of doctored beer.

The career of a 14-year-old Victorian boy,
named Tattersall, should be worth watching. This
festive youth, after graduating at a reformatory,
got a billet on a station at Rosedale (Vic.), where,
in due course, he stole some jewellery belonging
to the manager's wife. The theft having been
discovered the resourceful culprit went to the
gardener and made inquiries re the nature of cer-
tain dirty salt-like stuff ” which was kept in the
tool-house and used for killing weeds. The gar-
dener told the boy not to touch the stuff as it was
‘rank poison.” Thereupon he secretly took a
pinch of the rank poison and put it into the family
‘for a lark,” he says—and the manager
and his wife and the governess were taken ill
after drinking, their coffee. Fortunately they
vomited the poison. The medical evidence showed
that the all-round dose “ would have proved fatal
had it been on empty stomachs.” Clearly
the boy’s “lark” was intended to stop further
inquiry into the jewellery theft by silencing
the inquirers for evermore. Last week young
Hopeful came before Judge A’Beckett for
sentence on the. two charges proved against
mm he had, in fact, confessed to both
the robbery and the poisoning “lark”—and his
Honor committed prisoner to the care of a san-
guine philanthropist who gave him a good charac-
ter as “a Try Excelsior boy” in the past, and un-
dertook to look after him in the future. One
naturally hopes that the moral influence of the
Try Excelsior gentleman will be more efficacious
than the Reformatory training. But the question
arises as to whether it will be a fair thing to
place this bright boy in another billet without
putting his employer on his guard. What is the
best thin? to do with a peculative poisoner of
tender years ?

Samuel Davenport, who still lives in
S. Australia, though now exceedingly feeble, was
one of the first trustees of the Savings Bank, 53
years ago. All the other eleven are dead. The
old doctor produces on his estate the most
deliciously delicate olive oil which you can buy
for money. It is as well to know this in a country
where 999-thousandths of the “Lucca” used is
shamelessly adulterated with cotton-seed oil.
Letter published in all innocence by the
painfully-respectable Christchurch (M.L.) Press :
Can you suggest any means by which women and girls,
who walk out in the streets in search of adventure, might
be readily distinguished from quietly dressed ladies. It
seems very hard that a lady of over 30, coming up by the
express, and wishing to stretch her legs, should not be
able to walk home without being accosted by a gentle-
manly man, who, on perceiving his blunder, at once
raised his hat and slunk away. I should be the last to
interfere with personal liberty, but would it not be
possible for those of both sexes, who enjoy roaming the
streets in search of adventure, to establish some sort of
Masonic sign, or could not certain streets be given over
to them after 8 o’clock. —A PERPLEXED MOTHER.
The same ingenuous paper, a while ago, in the
midst of a fierce rumpus arising out of the alleged
collusion between doctors and chemists, solemnly
printed a letter from “ Servant-girl ” complaining
that a chemist charged her twice the Australian
advertised price for a certain Neo-Malthusian
species of pills. The guileless Press made the letter
the text of a virtuously indignant leader upon the
robbery and roguery of the chemist in putting a
prohibitive price upon “medicines that should be
within the reach of even the poorest of the
community.”

Some more devices for squaring the Victorian
public ledger:—
The Argus in its terrible dread of taxation, realising
that something heroic has to be done, has started a series
of papers on “ How the Money Goes ; Hints to Re-
formers.” Taking the first as a sample, it attacks almost
every bit of humane and economic legislation the 48 Rich
Landlords have allowed to pass. It has the beautiful
Christian stand-off title, “ Other People’s Children,” and
shows how the State can save £72,466 a year by boiling
down or making good manure of 5655 neglected children
Fatman doesn’t see in the 5655 neglected children any-
thing but a curse and a worry ; his small soul can’t grasp
the fact that 5655 neglected children, grown to 5655 res-
pectable citizens, are a better asset than 5655 criminals
and loafers, and that the £l2 spent per neglected child
per annum is a good investment all the time. It
is a wretched, mean, sordid, trumpery idea, this
unprincipled grab of charity moneys’ to square
the accounts, making the country’s weakest always the
first to suffer if there is any shortage ; and the proposal
to abandon the neglected children and destroy for ever
their chance of being anything decent, is about the
meanest. Writer knows of a miserable family—father
and mother both drunkards, generally one in and one out
of gaol. The children subsist somehow and that’s all ;
according to the local police they are growing up about
as vicious and depraved as possible. The force can
do nothing unless the children are sleeping out,
otherwise the father is supposed to be keeping a roof
over them. One night one of the waifs was captured,
committed as a vagrant, and boarded out with others
on a farm, where he is being educated and turned into a
respectable human being. His parents do their best to
find out where he is, because he pretty well kept them
with his petty pilfering, but the Government takes care
they won’t, and the police are lying in wait to get hold of
the rest of the family. An institution that does an
immense amount of good, like this system of State ward-
ship, requires to be liberally managed, not skimped and
starved and shorn of half its usefulness just to save
such as Cornelius Job Ham, M.L.0., paying land-tax
on their Melborurne properties.

JAYNE’S^EXPECTORANT
CURES THE WORSTCOUGHiS.
A deputation of W.A. mine-owners asked the
Agent-General to recommend that the tax on profits
should be made applicable to dividends only, and that
such a change should be retrospective. The A G beat
about the bush in reply. Were he half as clever as'he is
supposed to be, he would have made prompt answer •
“Right you are You guarantee that London directors
will limit their fat fees to percentages on actually-divided
profits, and that they will do this retrospectively and I
will strongly recommend your suggestion." The gilded
guinea-pigs would not stomach a dose like that Same
deputation had the cheek to ask that labor on a lease
should be allowed to cover leases held by the same people
in other districts-i.e., that the holder of leases at Norse-
man, Murchison, and Kalgoorlie should be allowed a
monopoly of all of them, provided that the labor em-
ployed on one was enough to cover the aggregate legally
required on all. The evil of consolidation on contiguous
blocks is bad enough in that it often promotes systematic
sitting down, but the proposed change would be ten
thousand times worse. It would mean wholesale shen-
herding without any power to check it. The pioneer
prospector would be practically extinguished.
• • • •
Melbourne papers still devote columns to dis-
c“?s'°“of I , t^ e question of mining tenure. Writers are
chiefly Bull boodlers and would-be monopolists. No one
as yet has cited an instance where, in Australia, a lease
has been unjustly forfeited. If anything, the Mines
departments are too lenient; they allow too many trivial
excuses for non-working to pass muster. There is no
more difficult thing to be done in any State than to dis-
possess the man who won’t work a lease, provided he Da vs
the rent and knows the right button to press. A favorite
suggestion is that money spent on machinery should be
allowed as a set-off against labor. If this were sanctioned
any fourth-rate, second-hand machinery dealer could
monopolise half the country side. An English co erects
an expensive 40-stamp mill, and then proceeds with mine-
development only to demonstrate that it owns a 4-stamn
mine. Should its folly entitle it to possession of the
property in perpetuity to the exclusion of other men who
would make a success of it as a 4-stamp show? This is
not an imaginary case. Every goldfield where English
money has been spent furnishes an example. In some
parts of Q. and N.S.W. there is more rusting than work-
ing machinery, the rust representing Bull foolishness.
Writer knows many good mines which are under the
damnation of the rust of machinery that was either
useless or unnecessary, but despite this indication of
failure some of these mines would be employing hundreds
of hands if men who knew how to handle them could onlv
dispossess the outsiders. Present owners, to all intents
and purposes, have abandoned the mines, yet contrive
by smart manipulation to keep grip, in the hope that as
big fools as themselves will come along and refund them
the money which they have squandered.
Some of the letters on this subject are clever as
cheap advts., but much of the cleverness is shown at the
expense of truth—this, however, is supposed to be as
foreign to the average mining man as polite language
is to a bullock-driver. The men who, for example, say,
in effect, ‘ We have been obliged to send back to London
money which has been remitted to us for investment in
mining because our principals disapproved of the tenure."
are unblushing liars. This scribe has had dealings with
English mining investor,” and he knows that the last
thing they do is “ partit is like pulling butter out of a
dog s throat to get money out of their pockets. The Idea
of their sending money to any agent in anticipation of his
finding some suitable mining in vestment ’’ is almost too
absurd for fiction. “ Insecurity of tenure ”is an excuse
kept in stock for use when dullness reigns in London.
.The trouble is forgotten when anything like a boom sets
in and a new flock of lambs are waiting to be fleeced.

Boulder Main Reef and South Kalgurli (W.A.)
»nd e th b »lt n^ h - e f tW °- gr t eat J disappointments of the year,
as d time I of lmprov ‘ug, becomes worse
as time passes. In both cases, the grade of ore is de-
clining, and, as they could not be made to pay on the
higher grade, their prospects on the lower are utterly
hopeless. It is stated that the Kalgurii is to try the
b^‘ C i n^ Ure 1 of .I 88u its 20 - 000 reserve shares to pay
off its debts. As it is illegal to issue shares at a discount,
and no one 19 likely to take them at par when they can
be bought m the open market at considerably less, the
project is unlikely to come off. Developments in the
lower levels of both mines have been, on the whole,
disappointing and the plants on both are too small to
treat low-grade ores profitably. The only apparent
chance for either co. 18 in its being mopped up by one of
its rich neighbor?.
• • • •
Lake View Cousols and Associated (W.A.), have
11 fe both bee " disappointing, though neither has actu-
ally lost ground. The former’s main shaft is now down
about 1400 ft. and the latter’s 1050 ft. Development is being
pushed ahead in both cases, and any day may see a vast
improvement in their prospects. The Associated is
severely handicapped by being saddled with an extrava-
gant unwieldy treatment plant, a legacy of former
alleged metallurgical experts, and it is doubtful if it can
ever be made sufficiently economical. Drilling for the
continuation of the Oroya rich chute will be started
shortly, and its location would improve things immensely.
Lake View Consols is more than meeting expenses out of
yield, and is in a strong position to continue explorations.
The Elmore oil process will shortly be tested on this
mine, and, if successful, will give a great fillip to low-
grade propositions.
• * • •
The Cape-Australian Cable cost the Eastern
Extension Telegraph Co. *871,973. The paid-up capital
of the co. is £3,000,000, its reserve fund £1,405,000, net
profit £148,526 a year and the div. 7 per cent, per annum.
These figures look healthy enough, and vet the chairman
at last half-yearly meeting wailed bitterly that the Pacific
cable had forced the Eastern to reduce its Australian
rates from 4s. 9d. to 3s. There is not likely to be any
further reduction at present. This particular Telegraph
Co. has enjoyed a monopoly of Australian cable traffic for
many years and it’s a public benefit that the Pacific cable
has come to compete.

Sydney “Royal Agricultural Society,” despite
the fact that it does practically nothing in return for its
subsidy “to encourage agriculture,” and is allowed to
wholly monopolise a big park area for running third-class
variety and cheap-jack shows, ended last year with a
deficit of £9OOO. This was largely incurred by the fol-
lowing works “for the encouragement of agriculture”—
Federal stand, £1450 ; wine bar, £l4lB ; beer and whisky
bar, £1024 ; secretary’s residence, £985.

in Dublin, aged 82 years.” O’Connell died in May
1847, “just 56 years ago.”

“The Cliveden dowager did not make her
house a place of lamentation during her fever
attack. The dinner-parties went on, and her
daughter presided at the head of the table. The
invalid has the services of one of the prettiest
nurses in Melbourne.
“The Melbourne papers proclaim the engage-
ment of one ‘Miss Advance Australia,’ from
Tallangatta way.
“ Rockhamptonites are interested in the
announcement of compatriot Florence Schmidt’s
engagement. The singer is the last unmarried
daughter in her family, and her sisters say that
Florence always was the lucky one, and that
probably her matrimonial future will grow roses
as abundantly as her unmarried past. None of
the Schmidt sisters were pretty, but they are all
attractive —very musical, and not penniless.
“A letter arrived by last mail from a Mel-
bourne sojourner in gay Paree. ‘ Ah,’ she
scribbles, ‘ this is the place. Wrinkles obliterated
—all above board, but taken on in their place a
hundred others in the form of brain etchings.
Here are four of them : The way to create the
outer circle; the manner of suppressing the
pantry ; the trick of building the hat to the head ;
the manufacture of the smile that hides age and
villainy. And there are many others. Mdlle. of
Shopland is a marvel. She is droll and excessive.
You must be fool or rogue to resist her. This is
how she does a big deal in hats. Chatters
incessantly, smiles, shrugs, spreads white
hands, all by way of ostensible civilities.
In truth, all the while she is studying your
style. It is her clever and most pleasant
way of doing it. Measures effects swiftly. During
the operation you are as unconscious as if you
were chloroformed. Then she shows the dreams
of millinery suitable to your style. And what
dreams you behold! Frail shapes like cloud
mists and peach bloom and morning dew, blown
together by a breeze from a bright garden. She
sets to work, your back to the mirror. The
transformation is effected with spider quiet and
cunning. A hairpin is withdrawn here, inserted
there, the thatch softened, loosened, touched ten-
derly. Then the hat is set on the fuzzy (you feel
it fuzzy) thatch, a skewer slides along the scalp,
and pinions the hat. A few more touches. You
look and are lost: you - must have that hat at the
price of anything.’ t
“ The second Castles sister has no sooner got
out of her swaddling clothes and been set on her
platform pins than a third is heralded as crowing
more melodiously than the two already tried.
When will the Castles family end ?
“The wife of a Melbourne wine-merchant heads
the visiting hostesses who are trying to make
Tasmania sit up. At Elswick races, t’other day,
this dressy dame led off with a sparkling basket
banquet—all the choice viands and ice-cased
‘ cham ’ that are portable in a hired carriage*
Afterwards her party promenaded, and the show
of silk and underwear struck Slowbart femininity
dumber than usual.
“Is it going to be the rule for daughters of ex-
Speakers to take to the stage ? Two of Graham
Berry’s daughters tried it, and now F. C. Mason’s
youngest figures pleasantly in panto, ballet. With
a nimble foot for the intricacies of the dance, a
voice of some timbre, and a dainty physique, she
may yet skip to a front rank.
“Up at the State House members tease Mr.
Gillies as to the number of the solicitous fair
sending enquiries every day of his recent illness.
One of the medicoes says gruffly—‘Waal, it’s nae
the wimmen’s foolt that Duncan’s ne’er married.’
“Mr. Robert Harper looked shockingly ill when
he landed from his trip abroad, but bore up a day
or two while the bonfires of ‘ Welcome Home ’
were ablaze. When they went out, he gave up
and collapsed. The shock of that hotel fire,
and its subsequent effects, are held responsible
for the present illness. A further holiday is
advised, but the bait isn’t tempting. The Harpers
will probably be very grateful to be once more in
possession of ‘ Myoora.’
“It seems years since the modish bassinette,
along with baby wardrobes, was on show in the
shops. But since the adoption of mitherless
bairns came into fashion, the bassinette has be-
come an article of sale again. Next month, seve-
ral young damsels of a smart set, who happened
to be in various parts of the State when the local
trumpets announced the domestic occurrence,
have arranged to confer together and hold private
views of the newlv-arrived infants. One of the
number boasts that there is £4O worth of Maltese
lace in her ‘ darling’s ’ long robe.
“The astonishing spectacle was witnessed on
our Block recently of three small boys ‘rigged
out ’ in the costumes of Franciscan monks, but
without the flagellating cords and the sandals, the
youngsters having no footwear. They were in
charge of a benevolent-looking old lady who
probably has ideas of her own on the subject of
dress for waifs. But it is fortunate that Rev.
Digby Berry, and his Sydney prototype, Dill
Macky, were not about at the time. If they were,
food would have been afforded for a six months’
controversy.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
Melb. Age fell in rather heavily, but un-
deservedly, over the “unauthorised” announce-
ment that the Iceberg Govt, intended to threaten
the House of Landlords with its resignation if the
Probate Duties Bill were cast out a second time.
The news, probably communicated by an honorary
Minister hungering for a portfolio, was divulged
at the same time as Lands Minister M’Keuzie
found himself in a mess over his land transactions.
The M’Kenzie business, coming hot-foot on the
resentment caused by Robert Reid’s retention in
the Govt., naturally made the Shiels-Irvine crowd
a little more careful how they scattered threats
about. Therefore, the prematurely-announced
decision was revoked, but, when questioned in the
Assembly, Irvine did not dare to say there was
no foundation for the statement. He merely re-
marked that it was “unauthorised.”
The editors of Sydney E. News, Sydney
D.T., and the Argus are all being, or have just
been, changed; and there are rumors of big
changes in the Age and S.M. Herald staffs.
Howard Willoughby retires for a year from
the editorial chair of Melb. Argus. After a
month’s holiday at the Lakes, the editor returned
to Melbourne only to collapse. Six months' com-
plete rest is ordered, with six months’ partial
rest to follow. The illness of the chief, and the
transfer of Mr. F. Ward from his leader-writer-
ship to Sydney D.T. editorship, place the Argus
office in a hole, as it will be difficult to find men
for the vacant positions. Mr. F. W. Haddon,
ex-editor, and now trustee for the Wilson interest
in the proprietary fund, has just returned from
England, but is not likely to go into literary
harness again.
At Poverty Point.
How easily veteran R. S. Smvthe takes the cake for
tricky advertisement ! As business-manager of Artnes
Beaumont’s recent benefit concert, the old tactician got
over the difficulty of announcing one more last final
farewell for the ofttimes-farewelled tenor by styling it a
Concert D’Adieu. And then, on the morning of the
event, he summed up the attraction in the neatest of 3in.
display advts. There were 16 lines in the ad.; “ Madame
Melba” had 4 consecutive lines, ahd “The House Full”
was repeated 8 times. The hand of the born pressman is
always visible in R.S.S.’s work.
“ J.A.C.”: The late William Elton- his death, of
stomach cancer, is cabled—although by no means an
“ emotional actor,” was a prey to great nervousness. I
have stood beside him, in the wings of the Princess’s
Theatre, Melb., while he was waiting, on a “ first night,”
to make his entry as Lurcher, in “ Dorothy,” and he was
all “ pins and needles.” He said to me, “ I never felt so
nervous irony life.” Yet he had already established his
reputation in the same part in Melbourne, and was cer-
tain of a brilliant reception. He had also a distinct dis-
like for melodrama, although very good in some parts—
Tom Bassett, in “ Hands Across the Sea,” for instance,
which he played on the first production of the drama by
Charles Warner in Australia. He then spent most of the
time he was off the stage in “ curses, not loud, but deep.”
“ Altesiinder ” : No doubt a lot of mischief is worked
by such plays as “Zaza” and “Camille.” Apart from
their doctrine of “purification by love,” they tend to
create an inexperienced female belief in the possibility of
an insatiable passion controlled by absolute faithfulness
on the woman’s part. They present in dramatic form
the green-sick damsel’s dream of an eternal domestic
honeymoon, such as Nature couldn’t tolerate. The
woman in these plays has no maternal aspirations.
Camille and Zaza are the ideal heroines of the mock senti-
mental barmaid who puts her elbows on the counter and
gives Bertie to understand that a gorgeous capacity for
undying affection lies hidden within her padded bosom
if Bertie will only persevere with his suit and take her
away from her “ hollow life ” among the beer and
whisky. Every second or third barmaid talks
Zaza; gazes tenderly into space and remarks that
it’s an awful thing for a girl to get enslaved by a
Married Man, and be thereby prevented from “ ever
loving again.” After which she repeats, parenthetically,
the old gag—“ But men don’t know what love is.” These
cerulean passion plays must be false in sentiment or they
wouldn’t appeal so strongly to the admiration of the
more fraudulent members of the Sex.
Lalla Miranda’s latest notable success was scored at
Nice, where she appeared as the Queen of Navarre in
“Les Hugenots." One notice described her voice as “of
the caressing stamp, fresh and pure,” and said, “her
beautiful diction and perfect vocalisation captivated the
audience, who overwhelmed with applause this exquisite
artist.” These repeated successes of the ex-Melbourne
girl are a satire on the verdict of the London critic—a
barracker for the German school of vocalism—who wrote,
in a loud, Podsnapian tone of voice, “I love not such
singers as Lalla Miranda.”
