ance of J. O. Williamson in a round of his favorite
characters, say, John Stofel, Rip Van Winkle, Kerry, and
Shaun.
Melba put in an active 24 hours in Adelaide. Arriving
on Monday afternoon, she drove almost at once to the
races at Victoria Park. In the evening she attended
Wirth’s Circus, and attempted to puzzle the thought-
reading Howards, finishing up with a long drive round
the suburbs. On the Tuesday afternoon she sang to a
crowded and perspiring female audience in the Town
Hall—and caught the Melbourne express at 4.30 !
Consider the poor playwright, how he gets his crust.
In Melbourne,-Ignatio Tyrrell lately sued Howard Harvey,
of the Gaiety Theatre, for £4 45., being remuneration for
writing two dramas. Forty-two “ bob ” per drama 1 He
got a verdict for the price of one drama, which would just
about pay a week’s board if there weren’t too many
drinks in the board. There are some names that require
a great deal of living up to, and when Howard and
Ignatio take to scrambling in the courts over the price of
the forty-two shilling melodrama the writer feels glad
that his own name is Bill. (N.B. —His other name is
Theodore).
Married, the other day, Ethel Buckley to George Mar-
low, treasurer of one of the Anderson co’s. Ethel, who
is about 17 or so, made her first impression on Sydney
audiences some two years ago, when she appeared in
“ when the Lamps are Lighted ” as Semolina, a par-
ticularly weird type of stage slavey. She was in George
Rignold’s co. as a child, and makes a dainty little
soubrette whenever the management lets her.
The “ Continentals ” which were to have been carried
on through the summer on Sydney Cricket Ground have
been discontinued, public support not being considered
adequate. Though large attendances were the rule
before Xmas, the huge proportion of deadheads reduced
the paying portion to a small crowd, considering the
expenses. The class of programme put forward (mostly
fourth class Music Hall turns) was not calculated to draw
big crowds.
The David Belasco who adapted “Zaza” from the
original French play, is son of the late David James, a
rather famous London comedian and old-time burlesque
actor. David James’s proper name was'Belasco.
Wilson Barrett has received some candid notices for his
London performance of “Alfred.” The Express of Dec. 19
says that his appearance as a harpist was far from
happy.” Edyth Latimer was “ winsome, but hardly con-
vincing,” and the jokes of the Saxon churls were ‘ nearly
as old as the historical incidents depicted. But it is
admitted that Barrett had a big popular success, as he
had all over the “ provinces.”
Miss Ada Lawrence, the up-country Camille, just now
appearing with Anderson’s co. at Melbourne Royal, is the
daughter of Carrie George, an old-time Australian
favorite. The question is sometimes asked, “What
became of Carrie George?” For many years she has been
living in the North. Her second husband, J. S. Lyle, is
sec. to the school of arts at Mount Morgan, and he contri-
butes to the press thereabouts.
On the first night of “ Zaza,” some of the characters in
the play referred to the heroine as Z'ctr-za, whilst others
called her Zazzer, which was, seemingly, the official pro-
nunciation— i.e., she was Zazzer to her best boy. Under
the circumstances, the conflict of pronunciations gave
realism to the play. Zaza is supposed to be a music-hall
girl, who has assumed a fancy name. Once there was a
Zazel, who used to be fired out of a cannon to a trapeze,
or shot up in the air by a spring trap. Different people
gave different sounds to the name —Zay-zel, Zar-zel, or
Zazzle.
In “ From Door to Door ” Bernard Capes tells of a play-
ful custom of the Spanish ladies. In their excitement
after a toreador’s victory they often cast their stays
(worn on the surface) at his feet, redeeming them after
the performance. A middle-aged widow fell in love with
a toreador. She lined her stays with some of her for-
tune in gold coins, and when the time to applaud came
she “ loosen’d her fragrant bodice ” (as Keats would say)
and hurled it at the hero. The toreador was stunned by
the golden offering, and the romantic widow was arrested
and fined. Her court trouble over, she found that the
toreador had clung to the corsets, left the profession, and
married a younger woman.
“ The Conservatorium voice ” is a descriptive term now
commonly used in Melbourne musical circles. It means,
a voice that has been carefully constructed with common
material. There is no wear in the Conservatorium
soprano voice. It pleases the ear and impresses the un-
initiated at first, whilst it is still new. But, instead of
improving with hard work, the voice loses flesh and
deteriorates into a shriek.
“T.D.” A Bulletin par asks, “ Where are the male
singers?” Well, there are plenty of them about, but they
do not rush to the nearest well-advertised Conservatorium
and play at being aesthetes before they have well mastered
the technicalities of music or learned to handle their
voices. The man who says he is going to make a
profession of it, settles down to hard work for a year or
two, and at the end he puts his name on to every
programme that comes in his way. His development is
so sudden that most people take him for a singer of years’
standing. But the Conservatorium girl makes a “ pupil’s
concert ” the dream of her existence, insists on singing
Brahms and Tschaikowsky, and if called upon to warble
an ordinary ballad written by a common English person,
indignantly replies that she doesn’t know any such
rubbish. The public, nine times out of ten, wants to hear
something like “ Mary of Argyle ’’ or “ Bid me to Love,”
and the average budding man professional gives what he
is asked for, but he doesn’t start talking in January
about a pupils’ concert at which he may possibly be
allowed to chortle some Rubinstein in the following
December. He has no time to lose.
“Fingleur”: Now that a move in the right direction
has been made by the production of the Hungarian comic
opera of “ The Fortune Teller,” why not follow on with a
season of legitimate comic opera instead of the frothy
foolery of the modern French school? The genuine comic
opera would be well within the powers of the artists now
available here. The first of all comic operas is Mozart’s
“ La Nozze di Figaro,” then comes Rossini’s “ Barber of
Seville,” with Cimaroso’s “II Matrimonio Segreto" a
good third. After these come Donizetti’s sparkling trio,
“ The Daughter of the Regiment,” “ The Elixir of Love,”
and “ Don Pasquale,” Auber’s “ Fra Diavolo ” and “ Le
Domino Noir,” and Boieldieu’s “ Caliph of Bagdad.”
Probably Lyster’s most successful venture in the comic-
opera line was when he staged “ La Nozze di Figaro,”
with Signora Magi as the pretty page, Cherubino. Who
that saw her then could ever forget her delightful sing-
jng, not to mention her delightful legs 1
Work of demolishing the old walls of Her Majesty’s
Theatre, Sydney, has been commenced, and the new
theatre will be opened, it is thought, within six months.
Hall’s Australian Juveniles are playing “The Red
Hussar” in South Africa.
One of the richest of violinists is Kubelik. He is but
22 years old, and his London earnings in a fortnight
recently were nearly £4OOO. He is insured against acci
dents for a very large amount, the premiums being paid,
not by the violinist, but by the managers under whom he
appears.
The rule of the Jewhannesburg capitalist
in S’Africa stands confessed by the announcement
that Alfred Beit, of Beit, Wernher and Co. (how
truly British is the name of the firm !) has held an
informal conference with the Boer generals, to
whom he expressed “ moderately conciliatory
views. ”
Tasmania is contributing generously to the
N.S.W. drought-relief fund. The island State
has done very well indeed from the blessings of
Federation and the curses of the mainland
drought. Federation opened the whole Common-
wealth market to her produce, and the drought
made values for anything green and eatable very
high.

That lamentable Westralian public job,
the granting of something like 100,000 acres of
land gratis to an English land-syndicate, may yet
prove the downfall of the James Ministry. It
certainly should lead to the downfall of somebody.
The English syndicate took up an immense area
as a “ poison lease,” the terms being that, if all the
poisonous plants were eradicated by 1903, and if
the land was kept quite clear for three years there-
after, the syndicate was entitled to a freehold grant
in 1906. The syndicate did nothing lor over 15
years, and then its lease was practically lost, for it
was impossible to eradicate the poison-plant in the
short time remaining. But just here the syndi-
cate discovered that part ot its ground had been
included in a water-reserve on which the regula-
tions, it is said, prohibited the keeping of stock,
and it demanded compensation because, if it gob
the grant, which, through its own neglect, it no
longer had the slightest chance of getting, it
wouldn’t be able to keep pigs on the land. (For
15 years it hadn’t shown the slightest desire to
keep pigs). And, by way of compensation for the
pigs it didn’t have on the land it didn’t have
eitiier, it got a free grant of about 100,000 acres !!!

“ Burglar Bill,” re the “ religious '
trimin&l:—
Doing a “ stretch ” in a first-timers’ gaol, I found that
#ie “ religious ” criminal was, in 8 cases out of 10, a
sexual offender —normal or perverted. _ There were two
Ihoirs, O. of E. and R.C., one organist officiating for
both ; and during my stay the three organists (prisoners)
who occupied the position were of this class. Out of 11
prisoners composing the 0. of E. choir seven were sexual
offenders—two of these being “lifers”; and the R.O,
choir had one unnatural offender to every four choristers.
The “ numbers ” of these prisoners were always down in
the request-book for interviews with the clergymen of their
respective denominations when they should visit the
gaol, and in the cell over mine a man, serving 15 years
for a horrible offence, used to drone out prayers of
his own composition during meal-hours and up
to 9 p.m. During the time I acted as prisoners
librarian I had a standing offer of three “ long
tailors ” (gaol-plugs) to one that I could pick the
sexual offender on his library lists (each prisoner selects a
list from the library catalogue). “ Stepping Heaven-
wards,” “ The Gate’s Ajar,” “ Pilgrim’s Progress,”
and the like, were in great demand with this sort;
indeed, in the next cell to mine, an elderly man, con-
victed of a heinous crime against his own daughter, read
nothing but the “Pilgrim’s Progress” for 2J years.!
Nearly all the men of this stamp were loathsome hypo-
crites and crawlers —hated by warders and prisoners
alike for their snuffle and sneakery. I protested
against being exercised in a yard with 27 others —18 of
whom were unnatural offenders; because the sexual
maniac’s conversation is always turned in a sexual direc-
tion. The Comptroller-General is, I believe, in favor of a
division, but at present the underpaid clerk who took a
few pounds of his employer’s money is classed, exercised
and worked with the sexual lunatic. It certainly does
not tend to reform the petty-cash pilferer to find that
the creature in front of him on the exercise-track is an
outrager of his own daughter, or that the case of the
object who is mumbling a prayer behind him was too
filthy for even the gutter press to touch.

Along the Castlereagh.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Uncle Jim was building a house-boat. He had
been reading a Sunday paper which pointed out
in its leading article on melon jam, that every
selector with a creek frontage could, with a small
outlay, spend his summer months on a house-boat,
instead of sweltering night and day ashore under
an iron roof.
Uncle had bought eight dozen egg-boxes from
Brannigan, the storekeeper, also some twine and
a pound of nails. Three palings laid endways
made a capital garboard-strake ; there were corn-
sacks to make an awning, and several buckets of
tar from Feeney’s wool-shed to caulk the timbers.
Uncle said it would be worth looking at when
finished; strong in the hull, and guaranteed
not to founder when uncle Bill fell out of bed.
Uncle hammered at the garboard-strake until
the sweat dripped from his arms. A man came
round the liend carrying a bundle on his shoulder;
he wore blue serge clothes and walked with the
swagger of a seafaring man. His legs curved
slightly, and there were knots and wrinkles on his
skin, showing that the sea had warped him badly.
He stopped near our fence, lit his pipe, and
watched uncle measure an imaginary deck-house
with a piece of twine.
“Buildin’ a barge?” he asked, and threw a
lighted match into the dry scrub.
Uncle glanced at him sideways, but made no
answer. I he sailor-man’s eye wandered towards
the homestead, and then down to the boulders in
the creek bed.
“Any gold about here?” he said huskily. “I
was told you’d got it rich up here.”
Uncle stopped hammering the garboard-strake
and looked kindly at the new-chum. There could
lie no evil in a man, he thought, who hunted for
nuggets along the Castlereagh.
“There ain’t no gold in my paddick, matey;
th’ niggers took it all away. We grow nothing
but lyre birds an’ cassowaries here. There might
be a few diamonds, but we ain’t had the time ter
look.”
The sailor-man appeared depressed, and threw
another lighted match into our dry scrub. “ Want
a hand with yer boat?” he asked, anxiously.
“It ain’t a boat.” Uncle turned his back and
piled 13 egg-boxes where he intended the dining-
room to be. “ It’s a machine fer shearin’ goats,”
he went on. “Shearer goes in one end, goat
t’other. If the shearer stays in, ye send in ’nother
goat.” He touched a board with the hammer
gently. ‘This is the slot ye put the shearer in.
It’s me own idea.”
It s a boat,” said the sailor-man, stubbornly,
“ and you’ll never shift her without the right sort
o’ power.”
Uncle looked flattered and lit his pi > “ You
seem to know a bit about boats, ma! What
sort of power would yer use, if it’s » Jr ques-
tion ? ”
The sailor-man cast another matcn in.,,, our
scrub, and swung his bandy legs over the rail.
“ Why hitch her to a team o’ cows?” he answered
quietly. “Stick yer compass between the near
cow’s horns an’ steer by her head. Plenty of
cow-power in this country.” Uncle walked
towards him, the hammer in his fist. “ Get off
my land,” he said, looking hard at the sailor’s
feet; “get off .”
The man slipped away hastily, and disappeared
round the bend.
“Danm ye! why didn’t ye stop him?” cried
uncle to me. But I sat still, and argued that it
was unlucky to stop a man with curved legs.
Uncle cursed us both as he stamped out the tiny
flames that crackled along the fence.
Towards the end of the month the Castlereagh
fell 9in., and one dark night Cassidy, accompanied
by 18 underfed bullocks, walked through the
boathouse from stem to stern ; he is now threaten-
ing to sue uncle for obstructing the thoroughfare.
* * « *
A circus-agent came to the house one day, and
gazed fixedly at our paddock. “Plenty of level
space here,” he said loudly. There were rings on
his fingers half concealed in the flesh.
Uncle came to the verandah. “What the
h ,” he began.
The agent lit a cigar. “We’ve seven caravans,
14 horses, a performing goat and a bear,” he said.
“ Git out!” thundered uncle; “d’ye hear?”
“ I expect ’em to arrive here to-morrow,” went
on the agent. “We don’t object to paying a
little ground-rent.”
“But—” uncle came off the verandah, his
hands in his pockets.
The agent began striding up and down the
paddock, as though measuring it off. “Yer look
like a business man,” said uncle, following him.
“What’s yer name?” Ten minutes afterwards
the agent lent him a shilling. Uncle walked back
with him to the end of the paddock and shook
hands at the sliprail.
* * * »
Aunt, who knew nothing about the circus, was
standing at the door, deep in thought. Suddenly
the shaggy head of a full-grown bear appeared
over the fence and looked straight into her face.
Aunt’s exclamation was heard up the river and
down the river ; then she backed inside and piled
furniture against the doors.
“What’s th’ matter?” shouted uncle, looking
in at the window. “Frightened of a poor old
bear. What rot, Emma ! Call yourself a bush-
woman?” He walked deliberately towards the
slothful apparition lolling over the fence. “Poor
old chap,” he began, soothingly. “What’s th’
matter with yer, eh ? Poor old boy ! ”
The bear’s ears twitched slightly ; the tip of his
black muzzle moved up and down. Uncle’s voice
seemed to brighten him up, and when uncle
stroked the thick fur between his ears he emitted
a pleased noise, and put two huge paws on uncle’s
shoulder. “ Poor old chap, poor old chap ! ” The
bear and uncle began to swing rythmically to
and fro.
“Don’t be top friendly, old chap,” breathed
uncle. “Don’t spile me clothes.”
The brute moaned softly, but drew uncle closer
and began to lick his face, eyes, ears and cheek.
“ Blast ye, let me go ! Don’t try ter wash me.”
He struggled gamely, but the bear seemed lonely
and pining for company, and after he had licked
uncle’s face thoroughly, he started with his rasp-
like tongue to lick the clothes off uncle’s back.
Just then the keeper came running towards them,
and he hit the bear on the hind leg with a paling.
„ “ He's a great wrassler, boss,” he said apolo-
getically to uncle, “ but he aint used to your
style.”
‘ ‘ Holy Moses ! ” Uncle wiped his wet face and
retreated from the fence. “What d’ye mean by
tyin’ a thing like that to my property?”
Been licking yer? ” grinned the keeper.
“Lick?” choked uncle; “’Struth, he’s worse
than a member of Parliment.”
• * • *
Uncle walked towards the Swag and Pannikin
somewnat dubiously. The bear had torn his coat
and licked his hair into a permanently upright
condition. He stayed a moment at the crossing
to read a police notice nailed to a tree which
stated in big letters that the sum of one hundred
pounds would be paid to any person supplying
information that would lead to the arrest of
Jimmy and Joe Governor,
“ ter meet Jimmy or Joe,” said uncle
aloud ; “if I was to meet 'em in the scrub. . ,
By cnpes ! A hundred quid’s a lot of money.”
He entered the pub. and discovered Brannigan
and Pat Casey playing quoits. Before he had
time to call for his drink, Professor Buzzi, the
circus contortionist, walked in and ordeied a glass
of port wine. The professor wore a suit of white
duck, and his black hair fell in oiled ringlets
about his neck. Uncle stared at him for three
minutes, it was the first time he had ever seen an
oiled professor, and he burst into a fit of horrible
laughter. The pub.’s pet jackass echoed the laugh
and sent it across the river. The professor put
aside his wine ; his face reddened.
‘ What fora you laugh lika that ?” he demanded
hotly.
Maybe yer lookin’ for a scrap?” Uncle’s
temper changed suddenly. The bear had ruffled
his temper, and the sight of a newly-washed con-
tortionist with oiled ringlets was more than he
could stand.
“Maybe yer’d like to put ’em up?” Uncle,
who prides Himself on his footwork, walked round
the professor, feinting with his dirt-smeared left.
The professor watched him until uncle’s left
came towards his ear ; with a sudden leap he rose
in the air and descended with both legs round
uncle’s neck, then bending his back he twisted his
body and arms into a figure 8 knot round uncle’s
ribs. “You dara hita me now,” he said, cheer-
fully. Uncle staggered about the bar with the
contortionist’s head peeping from under his left
arm.
Brannigan and Casey continued playing quoits.
Publican Primrose leaned over the counter read-
ing a paper.
“ Take this—bloke—off—me—neck—Casey,”
cried uncle. “Yer can have me plough-horses
any time next week.”
Casey stared at uncle, and then looked hard at
Brannigan. “I can’t see a bloke round yer neck
Jim. ”
“Go home, Jim.” Brannigan picked up the
quoits and put them on the peg. “’Tis a good
slape ye want. Ye’ve been dhrinkin’ heavy of
late. Go home, Jim, like a good mahn.”
Uncle stood still for a moment, then sprang for-
ward, twisting himself like a buckjumper. The
contortionist smiled, and knocked his head on
uncle’s breast. “You hita me now,” he whis-
pered.
Uncle ran to the fireplace and rubbed the pro-
fessor’s head up and down the chimney for three
minutes, then, with a shout, he started down the
road towards the Flat.
Several mounted shearers coming towards the
pub. pulled up in surprise, at sight of uncle and
the soot-smeared contortionist.
* Don’t stop me, lads!” he shouted; “I’m
carryin’ this cove for a wager.” He dashed full-
speed over the bridge into the police-station. »
Constable Murphy, brightening his handcuffs
with a rag, looked up hastily. “F’what have ye
there, Sloan ? ” he demanded.
“ Jimmy Governor ! ” gasped uncle; “I’ve bin
chasin’ him for a week, an’ here he is at last. Git
him off me neck, Murphy ! ”
Constable Murphy hit the contortionist with
the point of his finger under a certain rib, and the
contortionist straightened out immediately. An-
other policeman, standing by, rushed him into a
cell and banomd the
Uncle stood by and breathed.
“Yer a good malm, toioan,” said Murphy,
admiringly. “ A diver mahn, Sloan.”
“I played a waitin’ game, Murphy.* Uncle
leaned familiarly against the door. “How about
th’ reward ?” he asked, anxiously.
“How dara you locka me up?” screamed the
Professor from the cell ; “you leta me out now 1 ”
“Behave there, Jimmy,” growled Murphy,
peeping in at the cell grating, “or I’ll shake the
thripes out av ye.”
The Professor wept bitterly in Italian.
Uncle borrowed a shilling from Murphy, pro-
mising to repay it when the reward-money arrived ;
but that night Jimmy Governor contorted his
way through the lock-up roof and was not seen
again in the district.
Uncle said it was a put-up job to rob him of
the reward. Anyhow, he never paid Murphy his
shilling.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
Hard Luck.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Little Charlie had sat near his pen on the shear-
ing-hoard nearly all through the dinner-hour,
listening silently to the others spinning buck-
jumping lies before he started to tell his small,
plain truth.
“I could ride a bit, meself, one time,” he said.
“ Oh yes, I’ll own up that I was thrown once, but
it was only pure hard luck.
“You see, the old man, when he owned a
s’lection on G-uniworil, had a horse called Archer
that simply licked creation to buck. In fact, 1
was the only one could ride him. Me brother
Dan said he rode him once, but he often told lies.
“Once, I had to go to Wombindi with wool,
and was away for a month, and Archer had a
longer spell than he’d had since bein’ broke-in. I
catches him the mornin’ after my return, and puts
the saddle on him. He was quite quiet; you
couldn’t frighten him with the saddle; but we all
knew what to expect once I mounted. As luck
happen’d, me girl was there that mornin’ to have a
look, an’ I made up me mind to sit Archer if only
for the sake of her presence. Spare me days ! that
was just the reason I had to be slung. Archer let
me seat meself, an’ then he starts.
“ First me cap came olf, but that was nothing ;
nor when me tobacco was slung out of one
trousers-pocket, an’ me knife out of the other, I
wasn’t greatly int’rested. But when both heels
were bucked off me boots an’ the laces whisked
out an’ me boots fell off, I reckoned that Archer
was gettin’ into form. Next the studs and buttons
began to leave me shirt; an’ then—as Archer
bucked from the middle of the yard an’ struck the
top-rail on one side, an’ then whipped round in
mid-air an’ landed at the other side perpendic’lar
on his head —me shirt was whipped up over me
shoulders an’ slung in the dirt, an’a false tooth
was jerked down me throat.
“Still, me an’ Archer was inseparable. Me
girl rushed in an’ picked up me shirt an’ gave me
an admirin’ smile. I saw it; an’ I silently prayed
that the Lord would, at least, spare me pants, an’
I’d never get slung in front of that girl.
“ But, the very next buck, me belt shot up
under me arms an’ one leg of me pants was shook
right off, an’ then—well, in the int’rests of
decency, I had to leave. Hard luck, wasn’t it ? ”

The Star that Fell.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a slave of the snaffle and spur;
She was a queen in the land,
With the white of the snow on her blue-veined
brow
And the earth in her hollowed hand.
She, who had all of the wide world’s best
To wait on her whispered word,
Stood by the moon with him breast to breast
Where the box-leaves fluttered and stirred.
“ Ah ! why are your lips so sweet, so sweet ?
And why do your soft arms twine ?
And the golden heart of you, why does it beat
On this rough man’s heart of mine ?
“And why do you care for my love?” he said,
“Who am under your feet so far.
Distant and dim as the glow-worm’s red
Might seem to the midnight star?”
“Ah! Love, my Love!” And her fair face
flushed—
“ Am I richer than you, after all ?
For the glow-worm’s life may indeed be crushed,
But you know that a star may fall! ” *
Then they wandered away in the silver night
Down the meadows that march with Hell—
And the glow-worm shines with his old warm
light,
—lt was only the star that fell!
U.K.
WILL OGILVIE.
T’e Overdraf'.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The little M.L. town was shaken to its centre.
All sorts of rumors buzzed through the streets.
One thing was certain —a new manager sat in the
bank parlor up the street, and the former manager
walked disconsolate. Then the local paper added
to the sensation. One of their most respected
citizens, who had been recently banqueted on the
eve of his departure for England and the Con-
tinent, was returning from ’Frisco without com-
pleting his trip. A restricted overdraft was popu-
larly supposed to be the cause.
The overdraft became the one subject of dis-
cussion throughout the town ; over in the pah, up
the river, the Maoris had a talk on the subject.
Meanwhile, the new manager gathered up his pre-
decessor’s reins and conducted the bank’s business
in the strictest manner, especially in regard to
overdrafts.
» • * »
“T’e boss in?”
“ Knock at the door,” said the solitary and
youthful clerk to the 17-stone Maori.
“ Come in ! ” cried the manager. “ Aw, I don’t
buy whitebait. ”
“ Me Paul,” said the Maori, depositing his tin
of whitebait on the floor and sinking into the
clients’ chair.
“ Yes,” said the manager.
“Me your fren’; shake hart’s,” said the Maori.
“ Me going away trip, Nort’ Island.”
“ Well,” said the manager.
“You make me t’e overdraf’.”
The manager laughed.
“ Ten poun’ overdraf’,” said Paul, seriously.
“ No overdrafts to people who can’t give secu-
rity.”
“ What you call him ? ”
“Property, land, things,” explained the mana-
ger.
“Plenty gun, canoe.”
“ No, Paul, no good ; I’m busy this morning.”
Then Paul, ticking off on his fingers the names
of some of the leading citizens, “You make t’e
overdraf Brown—be no pay, he no good. You
make t’e overdraf Jones—he no pay, he no good.
You make t’e overdraf Robinson—all no good.
You make t’e overdraf Paul—he right, good,
werry good.”
“It won’t do, Paul—good-day,” pointing to the
door.
Paul rose and stooped over the table.
“You make t’e overdraf five poun’.”
“No.”
“You make t’e overdraf one poun’.”
“Not a shilling.”
“Pah!’’said Paul, “bank no good,” and he
walked majestically towards the door.
“ Don’t let any more of these Maoris enter my
room,” the manager said to the clerk when Paul
was safely off the premises.
The clerk was adding a column of figures when
a soft footfall caught his ear.
“ Hi ! stop ! You can’t go in there,” he cried.
A Maori woman, no longer in the first bloom
of her youth, bare-headed and bare-footed, looked
over her shoulder and laughed. She opened the
manager’s door, without knocking, and walked in.
“ Hurro ! ” she cried ; “ Me Miriama.”
“Go away, Miriama ; this is not an hotel. No
place for Maoris.”
“You know Paul, t’e Maori? You no make
him t’e overdraf ? ”
“Of course not.”
“ Paul, he no good—he no pay. You no make
him t’e overdraf. You know Miriama ” (tapping
herself on the breast)—“you make t’e overdraf
Miriama ; she all right, she good”—advancing on
him with a smile from ear to ear and with
extended hands.
The manager grabbed a ruler and dodged round
the table.
“ Go out! ” he cried.
“One poun’ overdraf’.”
“Go out !”
“One bob overdraf’.”
‘ ‘ Go out! ”
“You got t’e match?” asked Miriama, pro-
ducing her pipe from under her shawl.
4 ‘ Take some and go,” cried the relieved manager,
handing her his silver match-box.
She took one, and slipped the match-box under
her shawl.
Then two ladies, the leaders of “society ” in the
township, who were calling upon the manager
about some important social function, walked into
the room.
“Hurro!” cried Miriama to them. “You
make t’e overdraf’, too? No damn good,” she
added, shaking her head.
Then waving her pipe to the speechless
manager, “Good day, boy,” she disappeared into
the street.
* • * *
That afternoon was the first the manager had
felt free to enjoy away from the office, and he
walked for miles along the one road that led
inland. .
He was unused to the bush, and its beauty
tempted him to leave the road and explore its
labyrinths. At first he was certain he could re-
gain the road in five minutes; then he was
doubtful how long it would take, but was confident
of ultimate success. Finally, he was tired-out,
and whether the road lay north, south, east or
west was an unsolvable problem. All around was
a tangled, almost impenetrable mass of vegetation
—nothing but moss and ferns and undergrowth
and trees in wild profusion.
“Coo-ee!” he shouted. No response. He
shouted and shouted again. Then there was an
answering cry—a noise of parting branches, and
a Maori stepped into view, carrying a brace of
snared kakas (parrots).
“ Hurro t’e bank ! ” he exclaimed.
“ Is that you, Paul ? ” said the manager.
“You look for t’e pah to bring me t’e overdraf, ’
inquired Paul.
“I’ve lost my way.”
“ Don’t know where ? ” asked the Maori.
“ No; which is the way to the road ? ”
Paul looked at the manager ; his wan, pale face,
torn hands and clothes —mud-red to the knee.
“ You make me t’e overdraf’ fifty poun’.”
“ Nonsense ! I’ll give you five shillings to show
me the road.”
“Pah ! ” exclaimed Paul, picking up the birds.
“ Ten shillings,” said the manager.
“Pah, you boy, you tired, you go home,” said
Paul as he turned on his heel.
“ Stop. I say, I’ll give you a pound.”
“Twenty poun’ overdraf.”
It was already dark in the recesses of the bush,
and the manager, in despair, cried:
“Look here, I’ll give you five pounds, all the
money I have with me.”
“Show,” said Paul.
Paul put the five-pound note in his pocket, led
the manager a few steps through some luxuriant
ferns to a beaten track —pointed down it, and,
turning round, was lost in the depths of the bush.
The manager walked along the narrow track for
fifty paces and turned the flank of a big rata tree.
“Well, I’m d ——d!” he exclaimed, as he
looked across a large clearing and saw a horseman
jogging lazily homewards along the road.
• * • •
“Me make t’e overdraf’ five poun’,” said Paul,
and bis financial mana was great.
MAORI LAND.
T. H.
LIZ.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Kit was the woman for him, after all; he had as
much as told her so, and she had as much as
agreed.
Everyone understood that Liz and he were to
be spliced in a month, and as even the wedding
things had been arranged, this conviction ;uout
Liz being the right one had come at a difficult
time. He was wondering how he could back-out
of the business.
• “ I’ll go down to Liz and tell the whole
truth, ” he said at last. ‘ ‘ She never went agin me
yet, an’ I’ll arsk her ter give me the kick-off, an’
say as ’ow I carn’t ’elp what’s happened. . . . I’ll
tell her as how I always knowed she wuz a brick,
an j » He drew his hat over his eyes and set
off in the direction of Liz’s home.
Liz met him at the door. Her manner seemed
somewhat distant. She was dressed to kill in a
whitish-looking thing with frills roui d it. Jim
was fond of whitish things.
“Oh, it’s yah!” she said. “I didn’t quite
know yer at first; step in. I wuz expectin’ some-
one else.”
“That accounts fer yer bein got up so fine.
Who’s ther girl, Liz ? ”
•'lt ain’t a girl; it’s someone yer—yer knows
yerself.”
'i'ne expression of Jim’s face became more
decided : “Spit his name out. Ain’t he got ’er
name?” . .
Liz stepped up closer and laid a hafld on his
arm. “ Jirri,” she said, “we was wonderin’ if—if
you’d let me orf that promise I give yer about
gettin’ spliced ? ”
Jim was too taken aback to remember that he
had come to ask that same question : “ Why are
yer wantin’ ter back out o’ it, Liz?” he asked,
grumblingly. .
“ Because o’ a friend o’ unne. It ain t fair that
me, who can have me pick and choice of the
blokes around, should cotton to yer, when she’d
give her very eyes fer yer. It weren’t as if I were
perticular which I accepts. But ct f I b lieve
she’d let yer walk on ’er if yer was ter arsk ’er.
Lor’! she do love yer. Yer’ll never get another
so willin’ ter have yer; so yer oughter take yer
charnce now it’s come.”
Jim tried to say something, but Liz went on :
“I know she ain’t a beauty—but watter that?
Sal was laughin’ at her mouth larst night. But a
mouth don’t make a woman—do it, Jim ? ”
Jim looked at Liz’s pretty mouth and said
nothing, while Liz continued: “ An’ her eyes,
they ain’t nothin’ ter be ashamed of. They say
I’ve a fine pair o’ eyes, but I carn’t say as I’d be
ashamed o’Tier’s if 1 got ’em.”
“It ain’t fair o’ them ter be pointin’out her
faults,” said Jim, looking down.
“No, it ain’t; an’ when I goes ter ther circus
Monday I’ll tell ’em so. I’ll say, ‘lf Kit ain’t
favorable-lookin’, she’s a friend o’ mine all the
same. ” .
“ O ! Monday, who’s a-takin’ yer, Liz ?
Liz looked down and traced the cracks in the
hearthstone with her foot. “ ’E’s awful ’andsome,
Jim,” she murmured.
“Liz !”
“Yes!”
“Who is it?”
“It ain’t any interest ter you, Jim.” There
was silence for a long minute, and then Liz spoke
again: “Yer don’t mind, do yer, Jim? I was
meanin’ to keep it secret till Monday.” _
“ Won’t yer go on keepin’ it secret, Liz?”
“Why’”
“ ’Cause—’cause —’cause yer promised ter come
out with me o’ Monday.”
“ But there’s Kit; she ain’t got no one tor take
her, an’ I can get lots. It don’t seem fair ter be
talcin’ her last chance; she’s bin watching it fer
months, too. I used ter think yer ratty when yer
couldn’t see what she were at.”
“’Speets I wuz, Liz—but, say, Liz, are yer
really tryin’ ter back out o’ that bein’ spliced next
month ? ”
“ Well, I bin thinkin’ o’ Kit-—what of her ?
“ Bust ’er; wot about us bein’ spliced o’ Mon-
day?”
“ Are yer meanin’ it, Jim ? ”
‘‘ Course ; wot yer take me for ? ”
